Contradiction: Definition and Rules
Author defines contradiction and gives rules for finding contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any categorical proposition (universal, particular, singular, indefinite) by applying the rules of quality and quantifier change.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the diamond of logical opposition: two propositions never together true or false.

In this passage, the author defines contradiction (tanāquḍ) and gives clear rules for finding the contradictory of any categorical proposition. Understanding contradiction is the foundation of argument and refutation in Islamic logic. Whether you are proving a point or rebutting an opponent, you must know which statement exactly contradicts another.

Guided Path
1
Classify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is singular (about one individual), indefinite (no quantifier), or quantified (with 'every' or 'some'). The rule depends on this classification.
Singular: e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. Indefinite: e.g., 'Humans are mortal'. Quantified: e.g., 'Every human is mortal'.
2
Apply the rule for singular/indefinite
For singular or indefinite propositions, the contradictory is formed by changing the quality (positive to negative or negative to positive), leaving the rest unchanged.
Example: contradictory of 'Zayd is standing' is 'Zayd is not standing'.
3
Apply the rule for quantified propositions
For quantified propositions (with a quantifier 'every' or 'some'), you must change both the quantifier and the quality. Specifically: universal affirmative (A) contradicts particular negative (O); universal negative (E) contradicts particular affirmative (I).
This ensures the two propositions are opposite in both quantity and quality.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Take 'Every intoxicant is haram' (universal affirmative). Its contradictory is 'Some intoxicant is not haram' (particular negative). Verify they differ in quantifier ('every' vs 'some') and quality (affirmative vs negative).
Also check that they cannot both be true or both false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ (٦٣) «تَنَاقُضُ»: خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفٍ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ قُفِيْ (٦٤) فَإِنْ تَكُنْ شَخْصِيَّةٌ أَوْ مُهْمَلَهُ فَنَقْضُهَا بِالكَيْفِ أَنْ تُبَدِّلَهُ (٦٥) وَإِنْ تَكُنْ مَحْصُورَةٌ بِالسُّورِ فَانْقُضْ بِضِدّ سُورِهَا المَذْكُورِ (٦٦) فَإِنْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة نَقِيضُهَا: سَالِبَةٌ جُزْنِيَّة (٦٧) وَإِنْ تَكُنْ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة نَقِيضُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْنِيَّة
A section on contradiction. (63) 'Contradiction' is the opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter to be followed. (64) If it is singular or indefinite, then its contradition is by changing the quality. (65) And if it is quantified by a quantifier, then contradict by the opposite of its mentioned quantifier. (66) If it is an affirmative universal, its contradictory is a negative particular. (67) And if it is a negative universal, its contradictory is an affirmative particular.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines contradiction (tanāquḍ) and provides rules for finding the contradictory of a proposition. Contradiction is defined as the opposition of two propositions in quality (kayf: positive/negative) such that one must be true and the other false. The author gives specific instructions: For singular propositions (shakhṣiyya) like 'Zayd is standing' and indefinite propositions (muhmala), the contradictory is formed by changing the quality only, e.g., the contradictory of 'Zayd is standing' is 'Zayd is not standing'. For quantified propositions (musawwara), one must change both the quantifier and the quality. The two classic rules are: (1) The contradictory of a universal affirmative (A) is a particular negative (O), e.g., 'Every human is mortal' contradicts 'Some human is not mortal'. (2) The contradictory of a universal negative (E) is a particular affirmative (I), e.g., 'No human is a stone' contradicts 'Some human is a stone'. Particular affirmative (I) and particular negative (O) are also contradictories of each other, though not mentioned here.

Key Terms
تناقض
كيف
شخصية
مهملة
محصورة
كلية موجبة
جزئية سالبة
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of 'Every intoxicant is haram'?
A) No intoxicant is haram
B) Some intoxicant is not haram
C) Some intoxicant is haram
D) Every intoxicant is not haram

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is the logical relation where two propositions differ in both quality and quantity (for quantified) or just quality (for singular/indefinite), and they can never be simultaneously true or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the contradictory of 'No intoxicant is halal'. Answer: 'Some intoxicant is halal'.
?
What is the contradictory of 'Some prayer is invalid without purification'? Answer: 'No prayer is invalid without purification'.

Definition and Rules of Conversion (al-'Aks al-Mustawi)
Defines conversion and states its conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of conversion to categorical propositions and identify which propositions do not convert.

Core Idea
Conversion preserves truth, quality, and quantity, except universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative.

Conversion is a fundamental operation in categorical logic that allows us to infer a new proposition from an existing one by swapping subject and predicate. In this lesson, we examine the classical definition of conversion as given in al-Sullam and its conditions, including the critical exception for universal affirmative propositions and the prohibition for particular negatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of conversion
Find the part of the passage that defines conversion. It states: 'العكس : قلب جزأي القضية مع بقاء الصدق والكيف والكم.' This means conversion is reversing the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity.
The two parts are the subject and predicate.
2
Note the exception for universal affirmative
The passage says: 'إلا الموجب الكلية فعوضوا الموجب الجزئية.' This means the universal affirmative does not convert to a universal affirmative; instead it converts to a particular affirmative to maintain truth.
Example: 'Every human is mortal' converts to 'Some mortal is human', not 'Every mortal is human'.
3
Understand the 'two inferiorities' condition
The text mentions 'اجتماع الخستين' – the combination of particularity and negativity. A proposition that is both particular and negative (i.e., a particular negative) does not convert. Also, indefinite negatives are treated like particular negatives.
For example, 'Some animal is not a human' cannot be converted to a true proposition.
4
Distinguish natural vs conventional order
The passage ends with 'العكس في مرتب بالطبع وليس في مرنب بالوضع.' Conversion applies to propositions where the order of subject and predicate is natural (bi al-tab'), not conventional (bi al-wad'), such as definitions or descriptions.
Natural order means the subject is the topic and the predicate is a property that naturally belongs to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي) العكس : قلب جزأي القضية مع بقاء الصدق والكيف والكم؛ إلا الموجب الكلية فعوضوا الموجب الجزئية والعكس لازم لغير ما وجد به اجتماع الخستين فاقتصد ومثلها المهملة السلبية لأنها في قوة الجزئية والعكس في مرتب بالطبع وليس في مرنب بالوضع
Chapter on Simple Conversion. Conversion is reversing the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth and quality and quantity, except for the universal affirmative: they substitute it with the particular affirmative. Conversion is necessary except for that in which both inferiorities are found, so be moderate. And like it is the indefinite negative, because it has the force of the particular. Conversion is in what is ordered by nature, not in what is ordered by convention.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines conversion ('aks) and its rules. The author states that conversion means swapping the subject and predicate while keeping truth, quality, and quantity unchanged. An exception is the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B'): it converts to a particular affirmative ('Some B is A'), not a universal, to preserve truth. Conversion is valid for all propositions except those that combine two inferiorities: being particular and negative (i.e., particular negatives do not convert because they would produce a false universal? Actually the rule is that particular negatives do not convert; but the text says 'اجتماع الخستين' which refers to the combination of particularity and negativity, i.e., the particular negative. The author also notes that the indefinite negative (al-muhmala al-salbiyya) is treated like the particular negative in this regard. Finally, conversion applies to natural order (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is human'), not to conventional order (e.g., definitions or descriptions).

الخستين

B) Some haram is an intoxicant

Key Takeaway
Conversion is a valid immediate inference only when the conditions of quality and quantity are respected, with particular negatives being inconvertible.

Three Appendices to the Syllogism: Compound, Induction, Analogy
Author defines compound syllogism, induction, and analogy, and states they do not yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish compound syllogism, induction, and analogy, and identify which ones yield certainty.

Core Idea
Compound syllogism chains proofs; induction and analogy yield probability, not certainty.

In the previous lessons we studied the simple categorical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi), which produces certain knowledge. Now the author introduces three additional types of reasoning that are related to the syllogism: the compound syllogism (a chain of syllogisms), induction (generalising from particulars), and analogy (reasoning from one particular to another). He makes a crucial epistemological claim: only the logical syllogism yields certainty; induction and analogy fall short. This distinction is vital for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam, where the level of certainty of a proof determines its strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of reasoning
Read the passage and locate the definitions of the compound syllogism (مُرَكَّب), induction (اسْتِقْرَاء), and analogy (تَمْثِيل). Notice how each is described in contrast to the logical syllogism.
2
Understand how the compound syllogism works
The author describes the compound syllogism as a chain: you take the conclusion of one syllogism and use it as a premise for another, and so on. This can produce a series of continuous conclusions or separated ones. Trace the logic: if you have a first syllogism giving conclusion C1, then C1 becomes a premise in the next syllogism to produce C2, etc.
The key is the recursive nature: conclusions become premises.
3
Define induction (istiqra')
Induction is reasoning from particular instances to a universal rule. For example, observing that several specific types of alcohol are intoxicants and then concluding that all alcohols are intoxicants. The author notes that this kind of inference does not produce certainty because you cannot observe every instance.
Induction is considered probabilistic unless it is complete (istiqra' tamm), which is rare.
4
Define analogy (tamthil)
Analogy applies a judgment from one particular case to another because they share a common attribute (jami'). In usul al-fiqh, this is called qiyas. For instance, if date wine is intoxicating and therefore forbidden, then grape wine is also forbidden because it shares the attribute of intoxication. The author emphasises that analogy, like induction, does not yield certainty.
The difference from logical syllogism: analogy moves from particular to particular, not from universal to particular.
5
Identify the certainty claim
The last line of the passage states: 'And the syllogism of induction and analogy do not yield certainty as proof.' The logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi) is the opposite – it does yield certainty. This is the main takeaway for evaluating arguments in Islamic disciplines.
Certainty (qat') is a high epistemic standard; probabilistic arguments are acceptable in fiqh but not in kalam for foundational beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(لَوَاحِقُ القِياسِ)
وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ: «مُرَكَّبَا» لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْ رُكِبَا فَرَكْبَتُهُ إِنْ تُرِدْ أَنْ تَعْلَمَهُ وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيجَةً بِهِ مُقَدِّمَهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَرْكِيبِهَا بِأُخْرَى نَتِيجَةٌ إِلَى هَلُمَّ جَرًا مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِحِ الَّذِي حَوَى يَكُونُ، أَوْ مَفْصُولَهَا كُلِّ سَوَا وَإِنْ بِجُزْئِيٍّ عَلَى كُلِّ اسْتُدِلْ فَذَا بِـ: «الاسْتِقْرَاءِ عِنْدَهُمْ عُقِلْ وَعَكْسُهُ يُدْعَى: القِيَاسَ المَنْطِقِي» وَهُوَ الَّذِي قَدَّمْتُهُ فَحَقِّقِ وَحَيْثُ جُزْئِيٌّ عَلَى جُزْئِي حُمِلْ لِجَامِعٍ فَذَاكَ: تَمْثِيلٌ جُعِلْ وَلَا يُفِيدُ القَطْعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ قِيَاسُ الاسْتِقْرَاءِ، وَالتَّمْثِيلِ
Appendices to the Syllogism: And from it (the syllogism) is what they call 'compound' because it is composed of proofs. Its composition, if you wish to know it: flip the conclusion with a premise; it necessitates from its composition with another conclusion, and so on. It comes with continuous conclusions that it contains, or separated in all cases. And if by means of a particular on all one infers, that is understood as 'induction' among them. Its opposite is called 'the logical syllogism', which I have presented, so verify it. And whenever a particular is predicated of another particular due to a common factor, that is set as 'analogy'. And the syllogism of induction and analogy do not yield certainty as proof.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on the appendices to the syllogism. The author first defines the compound syllogism (مُرَكَّب) as a chain of multiple proofs: by taking a conclusion from one syllogism and making it a premise in another, you generate a new conclusion, and so on. The result can either have all conclusions linked ('continuous conclusions') or separated. Next, he defines induction (اسْتِقْرَاء) as reasoning from particular instances to a universal rule. Its inverse is the logical syllogism (قِيَاس مَنْطِقِي), which proceeds from universal to particular. Then he defines analogy (تَمْثِيل) as applying a judgment from one particular to another due to a shared attribute. Finally, he states that both induction and analogy do not produce certainty (قَطْع) in proof, distinguishing them from demonstrative syllogisms. In Islamic jurisprudence, analogy (qiyas) is used for legal rulings but is considered probabilistic unless based on certain 'illa.

Key Terms
مُرَكَّب
اسْتِقْرَاء
تَمْثِيل
قِيَاس مَنْطِقِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Akhdari, which of the following does NOT yield certainty as a proof?
A) Logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi)
B) Induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil)
C) Demonstration (burhan)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Only the logical syllogism (from universal to particular) yields certainty; induction and analogy are probabilistic forms of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

(b) Observing that silk is prohibited for men, a jurist rules that polyester is also prohibited because it resembles silk in texture. (c) A theologian argues that the universe had a beginning, and everything that begins has a cause, therefore the universe has a cause. Then argues that the cause must be eternal, therefore God exists.
?

The Distinction between Aggregate and Universal Judgment
Explains the difference between aggregate and universal judgment with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a judgment on the aggregate (ḥukm ‘alā al-majmū‘) and a universal judgment (kullī) in ambiguous statements from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A statement about 'all' can either apply to the group collectively or to each individual—and the difference matters for truth.

When we say 'all X are Y', do we mean that every single X is Y, or that the group as a whole is Y? This ambiguity appears in many Islamic contexts, such as the famous hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn. In this lesson, we will break down the logical distinction and see how scholars resolved the ambiguity in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two interpretations
Note that 'al-kull' can mean either judgment on the aggregate (the entire group collectively) or universal judgment (each individual). The author gives the example: 'Banu Tamim carry the great rock'—it could mean all of them together (since none can alone) or some of them (since some can alone).
2
Identify the ambiguity in the hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) said 'Kull dhalika lam yakun' after Dhu al-Yadayn asked if the prayer was shortened or he had forgotten. This could be interpreted as a judgment on the aggregate (the whole event, i.e., neither shortening nor forgetfulness occurred at all) or as a universal judgment (each of the two events did not happen individually).
The key is that in reality forgetfulness occurred, so the truth of the statement depends on which interpretation is intended.
3
Compare the two views
According to the author's initial explanation, the hadith is a judgment on the aggregate because negating the entire event is true (since forgetfulness occurred, but the aggregate is false only if both are false). However, the preponderant view is that it is a universal judgment, and the apparent conflict is resolved by noting the Prophet's lack of knowledge of the forgetfulness.
This distinction shows how logical ambiguity can affect the interpretation of sacred texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ كَـ: «كُلِّ ذَاكَ لَيْسَ ذَا وُقُوعِ»
وَقَدْ يَكُونُ الكَلَامُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلأَمْرَيْنِ ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : بَنُو تَمِيمٍ يَحْمِلُونَ الصَّخْرَةَ العَظِيمَةَ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ جَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ؛ لِكَوْنِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَ لَا يَسْتَقِلُّ بِالحَمْلِ، وَأَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ بَعْضِهَا ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ يَسْتَقِلُّ بِهِ .
وَبِمَا تَقَرَّرَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ: إِنَّ المَجْمُوعَ قَدْ يُرَادُ بِهِ البَعْضُ، مَحْمُولٌ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ عَلَى طَرِيقِ المَجاز .
قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ : كُلِّ ... إلخ ) هَذِهِ رِوَايَةٌ بِالمَعْنَى ، وَإِلَّا فَالمَرْوِيُّ: أَنَّهُ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ.
وَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ عَائِدٌ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَصْرِ الصَّلَاةِ وَالنِّسْيَانِ فِي قَوْلِ ذِي اليَدَيْنِ لَمَّا سَلَّمَ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنْ رَكْعَتَيْنِ : أَقَصُرَتِ الصَّلَاةُ أَمْ نَسِيتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ؟» ، وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الحَدِيثُ المَذْكُورُ مِنْ بَابِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى المَجْمُوع؛ لِأَنَّهُ المَنْفِيُّ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ لِثْبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا - وَهُوَ النِّسْيَانُ ـ فِيهِ، فَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَكَانَ الخَبَرُ غَيْرَ مُوَافِقٍ لِلوَاقِعِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ لَائِقٍ بِهِ ). هَذَا تَوْجِيهُ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ، وَالرَّاجِحُ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَمُخَالَفَةُ الخَبَرِ لِلوَاقِعِ إِنَّمَا تُعَدُّ عَيْباً إِذَا عَلِمَهَا المُخْبَرُ ، وَيَشْهَدُ لِهَذَا مَا رُوِيَ فِي بَعْضِ الطُّرُقِ: «لَمْ أَنْسَ وَلَمْ نَقْصُرْ»، وَمَا رُوِيَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ صلى الله عليه وسلم ذَلِكَ قَالَ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ، فَلَوْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الحَدِيثُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَمَا صَحَّ قَوْلُهُ المَذْكُورُ ؛ لأنَّ الإِيجَابَ الجُزْئِيَّ إِنَّمَا يَرْفَعُ السَّلْبَ الكُلِّيَّ .
قوله : (إِذَا عَلِمَهَا المُخْبَرُ لا يُقال: إنَّ السَّلام من ركعتين معصية وقعت نسياناً، والمعصية لا تقع من الأنبياء، ولو نسياناً
A chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. The whole: our judgment upon the aggregate, like: 'All of that was not taking place.' And speech may be ambiguous between the two [interpretations]; as in their saying: 'Banu Tamim carry the great rock,' for it may mean the aggregate of all individuals—because each one of them cannot carry it alone—or it may mean the aggregate of some of them—because some can carry it alone. And by what has been established, it is known that their saying: 'The aggregate may be used to mean some of it' is interpreted as being in the metaphorical way. His saying: (Like: 'All of that'… etc.) This is a narration by meaning; otherwise the transmitted version is that he (peace be upon him) said: 'All of that did not happen.' The demonstrative pronoun refers to what was mentioned of shortening the prayer and forgetfulness in the statement of Dhu al-Yadayn when he (peace be upon him) had given the salam after two rak'ahs: 'Did the prayer shorten, or did you forget, O Messenger of God?' And the mentioned hadith was from the category of judgment on the aggregate because that which is negated in reality is the entirety due to the affirmation of one of them—namely forgetfulness—in it. For if it were from the category of the universal, the report would not correspond to reality, and that is not appropriate for him. This is the interpretation of the author's words, but the preponderant view among the investigators is that it is from the category of the universal, and the report's lack of correspondence to reality is only considered a defect if the informant knows [the truth]. This is supported by what is narrated in some chains: 'I did not forget nor did I shorten,' and what is narrated that when he (peace be upon him) said that, Dhu al-Yadayn said: 'Rather, some of that did happen.' For if the hadith were not from the category of the universal, his (Dhu al-Yadayn's) statement would not be correct, because the particular affirmative only negates the universal negative. His saying: (If the informant knows [the truth]) [It is not said: 'The salam from two rak'ahs is a sin committed forgetfully, and sin does not occur from prophets, even forgetfully…']
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage examines ambiguous uses of 'al-kull' (the whole) and then applies the analysis to a specific hadith. It opens by noting that some statements can be interpreted either as a judgment on the entire aggregate (all individuals) or on a part of it. The example 'Banu Tamim carry the great rock' is ambiguous: it could mean all of them collectively (since none can alone) or some of them (since some can alone). This shows that the word 'aggregate' can be used metaphorically for a part. The passage then turns to the matn's example: 'kull dhalika laysa dha wuqu‘' (all of that was not taking place), which is a paraphrase of the hadith 'kull dhalika lam yakun' from the story of Dhu al-Yadayn. The Prophet (ﷺ) gave the salam after two rak'ahs, and Dhu al-Yadayn asked whether the prayer was shortened or he had forgotten. The Prophet replied that neither happened. The author's (al-Bajuri's) initial explanation classifies this as a judgment on the aggregate (the whole event) because in reality one of the two (forgetfulness) had occurred, so negating both would be false if it were a universal judgment. However, the more correct view (according to 'al-muhaqqiqun') is that it is a universal judgment: the Prophet meant that neither shortening nor forgetfulness occurred from him. The apparent conflict with reality is excused because the informant (the Prophet) did not know that forgetfulness had occurred, as the sin of forgetting is not attributed to prophets. This is supported by other narrations where the Prophet said 'I did not forget nor did I shorten,' and Dhu al-Yadayn's response 'Rather, some of that did happen'—which would only make sense if the Prophet's statement was a universal negation, because a particular affirmative only contradicts a universal negative. The commentary thus resolves the tension by appealing to the informant's knowledge.

Key Terms
الكُلّ
الحكم على المجموع
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Each individual carries the rock alone.
B) The aggregate of all individuals carry it, or the aggregate of some.
C) Only one individual carries it.
D) It is a metaphor for strength.

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-kull' can be ambiguous: it may refer to a judgment on the entire group (collective) or on each individual (universal), and recognizing this ambiguity is essential for correct interpretation of Islamic texts.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
8 min
Logical Rule: Particular Affirmative Contradicts Universal Negative
Uses the hadith to illustrate the logical rule that a particular affirmative contradicts a universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a particular affirmative (e.g., 'some S is P') contradicts a universal negative (e.g., 'no S is P') to analyze hadith and other Islamic arguments.

Core Idea
A single exception suffices to falsify a universal negative, but not an aggregate judgment.

In the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn, the Prophet's statement 'All of that did not happen' was interpreted as a universal negation. Dhu al-Yadayn replied that some of it did happen, which would be a contradiction if the first statement were universal. This lesson unpacks the logical rule behind that exchange and shows its application in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the logical relationship
In categorical logic, a universal negative (No S is P) and a particular affirmative (Some S is P) are contradictories: they cannot both be true. If one is true, the other is false. This is an immediate inference known as subalternation.
2
Apply to the hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) said 'Kull dhalika lam yakun' (All of that was not taking place). If this is a universal negation—meaning neither shortening nor forgetfulness occurred—then Dhu al-Yadayn's later statement 'Rather, some of that did happen' (i.e., forgetfulness occurred) is a particular affirmative and directly contradicts the Prophet's statement.
If the Prophet's statement were a judgment on the aggregate (negating the whole event), then Dhu al-Yadayn's statement would not be a direct contradiction, because the aggregate could still be true even if a part occurred.
3
Evaluate the preponderant view
The investigators (muhaqqiqun) hold that the hadith is a universal judgment. They argue that the apparent contradiction is resolved because the Prophet did not know that forgetfulness had occurred (since forgetfulness from a prophet is considered a sin, and prophets are protected from sin). Thus, his statement was true from his perspective, even if an exception existed in reality.
Dhu al-Yadayn's response 'Rather, some of that did happen' only makes sense if the Prophet's statement was universal, because only then does the particular affirmative serve as a valid objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ كَـ: «كُلِّ ذَاكَ لَيْسَ ذَا وُقُوعِ»
وَقَدْ يَكُونُ الكَلَامُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلأَمْرَيْنِ ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : بَنُو تَمِيمٍ يَحْمِلُونَ الصَّخْرَةَ العَظِيمَةَ»، فَإِنَّهُ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ جَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ؛ لِكَوْنِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَ لَا يَسْتَقِلُّ بِالحَمْلِ، وَأَنْ يَكُونَ المُرَادُ مَجْمُوعَ بَعْضِهَا ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ يَسْتَقِلُّ بِهِ .
وَبِمَا تَقَرَّرَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ: إِنَّ المَجْمُوعَ قَدْ يُرَادُ بِهِ البَعْضُ، مَحْمُولٌ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ عَلَى طَرِيقِ المَجاز .
قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ : كُلِّ ... إلخ ) هَذِهِ رِوَايَةٌ بِالمَعْنَى ، وَإِلَّا فَالمَرْوِيُّ: أَنَّهُ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ.
وَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ عَائِدٌ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَصْرِ الصَّلَاةِ وَالنِّسْيَانِ فِي قَوْلِ ذِي اليَدَيْنِ لَمَّا سَلَّمَ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنْ رَكْعَتَيْنِ : أَقَصُرَتِ الصَّلَاةُ أَمْ نَسِيتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللهِ؟» ، وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الحَدِيثُ المَذْكُورُ مِنْ بَابِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى المَجْمُوع؛ لِأَنَّهُ المَنْفِيُّ فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ لِثْبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا - وَهُوَ النِّسْيَانُ ـ فِيهِ، فَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَكَانَ الخَبَرُ غَيْرَ مُوَافِقٍ لِلوَاقِعِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ لَائِقٍ بِهِ ). هَذَا تَوْجِيهُ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ، وَالرَّاجِحُ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَمُخَالَفَةُ الخَبَرِ لِلوَاقِعِ إِنَّمَا تُعَدُّ عَيْباً إِذَا عَلِمَهَا المُخْبَرُ ، وَيَشْهَدُ لِهَذَا مَا رُوِيَ فِي بَعْضِ الطُّرُقِ: «لَمْ أَنْسَ وَلَمْ نَقْصُرْ»، وَمَا رُوِيَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ صلى الله عليه وسلم ذَلِكَ قَالَ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ، فَلَوْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الحَدِيثُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ لَمَا صَحَّ قَوْلُهُ المَذْكُورُ ؛ لأنَّ الإِيجَابَ الجُزْئِيَّ إِنَّمَا يَرْفَعُ السَّلْبَ الكُلِّيَّ .
قوله : (إِذَا عَلِمَهَا المُخْبَرُ لا يُقال: إنَّ السَّلام من ركعتين معصية وقعت نسياناً، والمعصية لا تقع من الأنبياء، ولو نسياناً
A chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. The whole: our judgment upon the aggregate, like: 'All of that was not taking place.' Immediate Inference

Contradiction of Specific and Indefinite Propositions
Defines the rule for contradicting specific and indefinite propositions by changing quality, with examples and a note on scholarly difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to form the contradictory of any specific or indefinite proposition by changing its quality, and identify the scholarly dispute regarding the contradictory of indefinite propositions.

Core Idea
Contradiction of specific and indefinite propositions is simply a change in quality — but quantity may matter for indefinites.

In this lesson, we explore a rule for immediate inference: how to construct the contradictory (naqḍ) of specific (shakhṣiyya) and indefinite (muhmala) propositions. The author gives a clear rule and examples, but a dispute arises about whether the contradictory of an indefinite must also be indefinite or should be universal. By understanding this, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate contradictory pairs in Islamic logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the given proposition is specific (about an individual, e.g., 'Zaid is standing') or indefinite (lacking a quantifier, e.g., 'Man is an animal').
Specific propositions often use a proper noun or definite reference.
2
Apply the rule: change the quality
For both types, form the contradiction by reversing the quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa) while keeping the same subject and predicate.
This is the core rule: 'naqḍuhā bi-l-kayf an tubaddilahu'.
3
Verify with examples
Examine the provided examples, noting how they adjust time, place, relation, etc., to maintain the contradiction without adding quantifiers.
The author notes that the condition of 'units' is implicitly covered by the truth conditions of contradictories.
4
Recognize the scholarly difference
Note that some logicians argue the contradictory of an indefinite must be a universal proposition, not an indefinite, because the indefinite is equivalent to a particular.
Example: 'Man is an animal' -> 'No human is an animal' according to the other view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦٤) فَإِنْ تَكُنْ شَخْصِيَّةٌ أَوْ مُهْمَلَهُ فَنَقْضُهَا بِالكَيْفِ أَنْ تُبَدِّلَهُ. وَقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِكَاتِبِ. وَقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدٌ صَائِمُ اليَوْمَ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِصَائِمٍ أَمْسَ. وَقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدٌ جَالِسٌ فِي المَسْجِدِ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِجَالِسِ فِي السُّوْقِ. وَقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدٌ أَبْ لِعَمْرٍو، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِأَبِ لِبَكْرِه. وَقَوْلِنَا : الزَّكَاةُ وَاجِبَةٌ فِي مَالِ الصَّبِيِّ إِذَا بَلَغَ نِصاباً ، الزَّكَاةُ لَيْسَتْ بِوَاجِبَةٍ فِي مَالِ الصَّبِيِّ إِذَا لَمْ يَبْلُغْ نِصاباً . وَقَوْلِنَا : الخَمْرُ فِي الدَّنّ مُسْكِرٌ بِالقُوَّةِ الخَمْرُ فِي الدَّنْ لَيْسَ بِمُسْكِرٍ بِالفِعْلِ . وَقَوْلِنَا : الزِّنْجِيُّ أَسْوَدُ - أَيْ : جُزْوُهُ ، الزِّنْجِيُّ لَيْسَ بِأَسْوَدَ - أَيْ : كُلُّهُ - . وَقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدٌ كَاتِبٌ بِالقَلَمِ الحَدِيدِ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِكَاتِبٍ بِغَيْرِ القَلَمِ الحَدِيدِ . وَبِذَلِكَ عُلِمَ أَنَّ قَوْلَ المُصَنِّفِ : وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٌ ... إلخ مُغْنِ عَنِ اشْتِرَاطِ الوَحَدَاتِ المَذْكُورَةِ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ تَكُنْ ... إلخ) الفَاءُ : إِمَّا تَفْرِيعِيَّةٌ، أَوْ فَصِيحِيَّةٌ ، وَالصَّمِيرُ يَرْجِعُ لِلقَضِيَّةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ ، وَقَوْلُهُ : فَنَقْضُهَا مُبْتَدَأَ ، وَقَوْلُهُ : أَنْ تُبَدِّلَهُ خَبَرٌ ، وَاحْتَرَزَ بِقَوْلِهِ : بِالكَيْفِ عَنْ نَقْضِهَا بِالجِهَةِ، فَإِنَّ لَهُ أَحْكَاماً مَذْكُورَةً فِي المُطَوَّلاتِ. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: (بِالكَيْفِ هُوَ الخَبَرُ ، وَقَوْلُهُ : أَنْ تُبَدِّلَهُ بَدَلٌ مِنْهُ عَلَى حَدٌ البَدَلِ فِي نَحْوِ : نَفَعَنِي زَيْدُ عِلْمُهُ. وَمُقْتَضَى كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنَّ نَقِيضَ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ : شَخْصِيَّةٌ مُخَالَفَةٌ لَهَا فِي الكَيْفِ، وَنَقِيضَ المُهْمَلَةِ : مُهْمَلَةٌ كَذَلِكَ ، وَهُوَ عِنْدَ غَيْرِ المُصَنِّفِ مُسَلَّمٌ فِي الأُولَى دُونَ الثَّانِيَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ نَقِيضَ المُهْمَلَةِ عِنْدَ غَيْرِ المُصَنِّفِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ كُلِّيَّةٌ تُخَالِفُهَا فِي الكَيْفِ ; لِكَوْنِهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ : فَنَقِيضُ المُهْمَلَةِ المَوْجَبَةِ نَحْوُ : الإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانٌ : سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ نَحْوُ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَيَوَان. قوله : أَنْ تُبَدِّلَهُ خَبَرٌ وقوله : بِالكَيْف - أي : بحسبه - متعلّق بنقض .
If the proposition is specific or indefinite, then its contradiction is by quality, i.e., you change it. And our saying: 'Zaid is standing, Zaid is not writing.' And our saying: 'Zaid is fasting today, Zaid is not fasting yesterday.' And our saying: 'Zaid is sitting in the mosque, Zaid is not sitting in the market.' And our saying: 'Zaid is father of Amr, Zaid is not father of Bakr.' And our saying: 'Zakat is obligatory on the property of the child when it reaches the nisab, Zakat is not obligatory on the property of the child when it does not reach the nisab.' And our saying: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating potentially, Wine in the jar is not intoxicating actually.' And our saying: 'The Zinji is black' – meaning partially, 'The Zinji is not black' – meaning entirely. And our saying: 'Zaid is a writer with the iron pen, Zaid is not a writer without the iron pen.' And by that it is known that the author's statement: 'and the truth of one... etc.' suffices over stipulating the mentioned units, so ponder. His statement: 'If it is... etc.' – the fa' is either explicative or eloquent, and the pronoun refers to the proposition qua proposition. His statement: 'so its contradiction' is a subject, and his statement: 'you change it' is a predicate. And he restricted by his statement 'by quality' from contradicting by modality, for it has rulings mentioned in the extended works. And it is possible that his statement 'by quality' is the predicate, and his statement 'you change it' is a substitution from it, like the substitution in examples like: 'Zaid benefited me, his knowledge.' And what is required by the author's statement is that the contradictory of a specific is a specific differing in quality, and the contradictory of an indefinite is an indefinite likewise. But according to others, this is granted for the first but not the second; because the contradictory of an indefinite according to others is a universal differing in quality, as it is equivalent to a particular. So the contradictory of an affirmative indefinite like 'Man is an animal' is a universal negative like 'No human is an animal.' His statement: 'you change it' is a predicate, and his statement 'by quality' – meaning according to it – is related to the contradiction.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines how to form the contradictory (naqīḍ) of two types of propositions: specific (shakhṣiyya) and indefinite (muhmala). The rule is stated concisely: 'its contradiction is by quality, i.e., you change it.' That is, you change the quality from affirmative to negative or vice versa, while keeping the subject and predicate the same. The passage then provides several examples to illustrate the rule. The examples cover various conditions: time (today vs. yesterday), place (mosque vs. market), relation (father of Amr vs. not father of Bakr), legal obligation (zakat due when property reaches nisab vs. when not), potential vs. actual (wine potentially intoxicating vs. not actually), partial vs. whole (black in part vs. not black entirely), and instrument (writer with iron pen vs. not without). The commentator then notes that the author's statement 'and the truth of one...' suffices, meaning that the condition that contradictories cannot both be true and cannot both be false is implicitly covered; thus the earlier stipulation of 'units' (time, place, etc.) is not needed – a point to ponder. Next, a grammatical analysis of the author's words follows: 'fa-in takun...' – the 'fa' is either explicative (tafrī'iyya) or eloquent (faṣīḥiyya); the pronoun refers to the proposition; 'naqḍuhā' is subject, 'an tubaddilahu' is predicate. The restriction 'by quality' (bi-l-kayf) contrasts with contradiction by modality (bi-l-jiha), which has its own rules. An alternative syntactic parsing is offered: 'by quality' as predicate and 'you change it' as a substitution. Finally, the passage presents an important scholarly difference: according to the author (al-Akhḍarī?), the contradictory of an indefinite is also an indefinite differing in quality. However, other logicians hold that the contradictory of an indefinite must be a universal differing in quality, because an indefinite is equivalent to a particular in logical force. An example is given: the contradictory of 'Man is an animal' (affirmative indefinite) according to the other view is 'No human is an animal' (universal negative). This demonstrates that the hashiya is not merely explaining but also refining the author's position.

Key Terms
شَخْصِيَّة
مُهْمَلَة
نَقْض
الكَيْف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of the specific proposition 'Zaid is standing'?
A) Zaid is not standing
B) Someone is not standing
C) No one is standing
D) Zaid is sitting

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a specific or indefinite proposition is formed by changing its quality, but for indefinite propositions, a scholarly dispute exists whether its contradictory should also be indefinite or universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'الصوم واجب في شهر رمضان' (Fasting is obligatory in the month of Ramadan), form its contradictory according to the rule in this lesson. Then explain how the contradictory would differ if the proposition were indefinite instead of specific.
?
In a debate, your opponent asserts the indefinite statement 'الإنسان حيوان' (Man is an animal). According to the rule as presented by the author (al-Akhḍarī), what is the contradictory? According to the other scholars mentioned, what would it be? Which one do you think is more appropriate for a proper refutation in munazara? Provide reasoning.

Contradiction of Universal Affirmative in Categorical Propositions
States the rule for contradicting universal affirmative with particular negative and justifies why not universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory of any quantified categorical proposition and explain why it is not the universal negative.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal affirmative is the particular negative, because contradictories cannot both be false.

In logical debate, pinpointing the exact contradictory of a claim is essential. Classical logic teaches that the quantifier and quality both flip, but a common mistake is to think 'Every X is Y' contradicts 'No X is Y'. This lesson clarifies the rule using a Kalam example and explains why the particular negative is the true contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier and quality
Look at the proposition's quantifier (e.g., 'every', 'some') and its quality (affirmative or negative). For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is universal affirmative.
2
Apply the contradiction rule
Change the quantifier to its opposite (universal ↔ particular) and change the quality to its opposite (affirmative ↔ negative). So 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' becomes 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden'.
This is the 'opposite of its mentioned quantifier' rule from the text.
3
Check that both cannot be false together
Test with a borderline case. If some intoxicants are forbidden and some are not, then both 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'No intoxicant is forbidden' are false. But 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden' is true. So only the particular negative works.
The commentator explains this with the example of 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human' both being false.
4
Apply to other quantified forms
Practice with universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, determine the correct contradictory using the same rule.
Universal negative contradicts particular affirmative; particular affirmative contradicts universal negative; particular negative contradicts universal affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦٥) وَإِنْ تَكُنْ مَحْصُورَةٌ بِالسُّورِ فَانْقُضْ بِضِدٌ سُورِهَا المَذْكُورِ (٦٦) فَإِنْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة نَقِيضُهَا: سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّة. وَنَقِيضُ المُهْمَلَةِ السَّالِبَةِ نَحْوُ : الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ : مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ جَمِيعَ مَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ لَا يَخْتَصُّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ، بَلْ يَجْرِي فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ : فَمِثَالُ التَّنَاقُضِ فِي المَخْصُوصَةِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآنَ أَكْرَمْتُكَ : لَيْسَ إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الآنَ أَكْرَمْتُكَ. وَفِي المُهْمَلَةِ أَنْ تَقُولَ : إِنْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ: لَيْسَ إِنْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ، وَعَلَى هَذَا القِيَاسُ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ تَكُنْ مَحْصُورَةٌ بِالسُّورِ ... إلخ) أَيْ: سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَسَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، فَدَخَلَ فِي كَلَامِهِ جَمِيعُ القَضَايَا ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (فَانْقُضْ بِضِدٌ سُورِهَا المَذْكُورِ) لَا يَخْفَى عَلَيْكَ : أَنَّ سُورَ الإِيجَابِ الكُلّيّ ضِدُّهُ سُورُ السَّلْبِ الجُزْئِيِّ، وَبِالعَكْسِ وَسُورُ الإِيجَابِ الجُزْئِيِّ ضِدُّهُ سُورُ السَّلْبِ الكُلّي، وَبِالعَكْسِ. قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ تَكُنْ مُوجَبَةٌ ... إلخ) الفَاءُ : إِمَّا تَفْرِيعِيَّةٌ، أَوْ فَصِيحِيَّةٌ، مِثْلُ مَا مَرَّ. قَوْلُهُ : (نَقِيضُهَا : سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّة) أَيْ: وَبِالعَكْسِ، فَفِي كَلَام المُصَنِّفِ اكْتِفَاءُ؛ لِلعِلْمِ بِذَلِكَ مِمَّا ذَكَرَهُ ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ نَقِيضُ المُوجَبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ : سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ ; لأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَجَازَ كَذِبُهُمَا مَعاً ; كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانُ، لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَالنَّقِيضَانِ لَا يَكْذِبَانِ مَعاً، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . قوله : (وَفِي المُهْمَلَةِ) انظر هل الخلاف جارٍ في ذلك بين المصنف وغيره؛ قياساً على ما تقدَّم؟
And if it is quantified with a quantifier, then contradict it with the opposite of its mentioned quantifier. So if it is a universal affirmative, its contradictory is a particular negative. And the contradictory of a negative indefinite, like 'Man is not an animal,' is a universal affirmative, like 'Every human is an animal.' And know that everything the author mentioned is not restricted to categorical propositions, but applies to conditionals: an example of contradiction in a specific conditional is you say: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you' (and its contradictory) 'It is not the case that if you come to me now, I will honor you.' And in the indefinite, you say: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' (and its contradictory) 'It is not the case that if this is a human, then it is an animal.' And likewise for others. His statement: 'And if it is quantified with a quantifier...' meaning whether universal or particular, and whether affirmative or negative, so all propositions are included in his words, so ponder. His statement: 'then contradict it with the opposite of its mentioned quantifier' – it is not hidden from you: that the quantifier of universal affirmative has as its opposite the quantifier of particular negative, and vice versa; and the quantifier of particular affirmative has as its opposite the quantifier of universal negative, and vice versa. His statement: 'So if it is affirmative...' – the 'fa' is either explicative or eloquent, like what came before. His statement: 'its contradictory is a particular negative' – i.e., and vice versa. So in the author's statement there is sufficiency, due to knowledge of that from what he mentioned. And the reason why the contradictory of a universal affirmative is not a universal negative is that if it were like that, both could be false together, as in your saying: 'Every animal is a human, no animal is a human' – and the contradictories cannot be false together, as was known from what came before. His statement: 'And in the indefinite' – consider whether the disagreement runs between the author and others in that, by analogy with what has preceded.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the rule for contradicting quantified (maḥṣūra) propositions and extends it to conditionals. The matn states: 'If it is quantified with a quantifier, then contradict it with the opposite of its mentioned quantifier.' This is immediately exemplified: 'If it is a universal affirmative, its contradictory is a particular negative.' The commentator clarifies that the rule applies to all quantified propositions: universal/particular, affirmative/negative. The phrase 'with the opposite of its mentioned quantifier' means that for universal affirmative, the opposite quantifier is particular negative; for particular affirmative, the opposite is universal negative; and vice versa. The author provides an example of an indefinite negative proposition (which is not quantified but included for completeness): the contradictory of 'Man is not an animal' (indefinite negative) is 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative), aligning with the view that indefinite propositions are treated as particulars. The passage then emphasizes that these rules are not limited to categorical (ḥamliyya) propositions but also apply to conditional (sharṭiyya) propositions. Examples are given: for a specific conditional 'If you come to me now, I will honor you,' the contradictory is 'It is not the case that if you come to me now, I will honor you.' For an indefinite conditional 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' the contradictory is 'It is not the case that if this is a human, then it is an animal.' The commentator then provides a grammatical analysis of the author's phrases and justifies why the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative and not a universal negative. The reason is that contradictories cannot both be false. If the contradictory of 'Every animal is a human' were 'No animal is a human,' both could be false (since some animals are humans and some are not). But the contradictory must always have opposite truth values. Therefore, the correct contradictory is the particular negative 'Some animal is not a human' (or 'Not every animal is a human'). The passage ends with a note: the disagreement about indefinite propositions mentioned earlier should be considered here as well.

سُور
كُلِّيَّة
جُزْئِيَّة
نَقِيض

Checkpoint
Why is the contradictory of a universal affirmative not a universal negative?
A) Because a universal negative is always true when the universal affirmative is false.
B) Because both the universal affirmative and universal negative could be false together, violating the condition that contradictories cannot both be false.
C) Because the universal negative is the subcontrary of the universal affirmative.
D) Because the rule only applies to particular propositions.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal affirmative is always the particular negative because only they have opposite truth values in all cases.

Contradiction in Conditional Propositions
Extends the contradiction rule to conditional propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of contradiction to conditional propositions and identify their correct contradictories.

Core Idea
The same rule of contradiction applies to conditionals: flip the quantifier (if universal/particular) and negate the whole conditional.

The rule for contradicting quantified propositions is not limited to categorical statements. As the commentator emphasizes, it also applies to conditional (shartiyya) propositions. This lesson shows how to contradict examples like 'If you come, I will honor you' and explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conditional structure
A conditional proposition has the form 'If P, then Q'. Quantifiers may appear on the entire conditional (e.g., 'Whenever it rains, the ground is wet' is a universal conditional). In the text, examples are given without explicit quantifier but are treated as indefinite or specific.
2
Apply the contradiction rule to conditionals
The contradictory of a conditional is obtained by negating the entire conditional, i.e., 'It is not the case that if P then Q'. This flips the truth value.
For quantified conditionals, the quantifier also flips: universal becomes particular, etc.
3
Differentiate between specific and indefinite conditionals
In the text, the specific conditional 'If you come now, I will honor you' has contradictory 'It is not the case that if you come now, I will honor you'. The indefinite conditional 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' similarly has contradictory 'It is not the case that if this is a human, then it is an animal'.
The indefinite conditional is treated like a particular conditional.
4
Verify with a real Islamic example
Consider the conditional 'If a person breaks the fast intentionally, then they must make up for that day.' The contradictory is 'It is not the case that if a person breaks the fast intentionally, then they must make up for that day' – i.e., there exists a case where the antecedent is true and consequent false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Examples are given: for a specific conditional 'If you come to me now, I will honor you,' the contradictory is 'It is not the case that if you come to me now, I will honor you.' For an indefinite conditional 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' the contradictory is 'It is not the case that if this is a human, then it is an animal.' The commentator then provides a grammatical analysis of the author's phrases and justifies why the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative and not a universal negative. The reason is that contradictories cannot both be false. If the contradictory of 'Every animal is a human' were 'No animal is a human,' both could be false (since some animals are humans and some are not). But the contradictory must always have opposite truth values. Therefore, the correct contradictory is the particular negative 'Some animal is not a human' (or 'Not every animal is a human'). The passage ends with a note: the disagreement about indefinite propositions mentioned earlier should be considered here as well.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
مَخْصُوصَة
مُهْمَلَة
نَقِيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct contradictory of the conditional 'If the star is a meteor (shihab), then it is created (hadith)?'
A) If the star is a meteor, then it is not created.
B) It is not the case that if the star is a meteor, then it is created.
C) If the star is not a meteor, then it is created.
D) Some meteor is not created.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction of conditionals follows the same principle: negate the entire proposition to achieve opposite truth value.

Contradictory of Universal Negative
Bajuri states and justifies the rule for the contradictory of a universal negative proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any universal negative proposition in Islamic logical contexts.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'No A is B' is 'Some A is B', not 'Every A is B'.

We saw earlier that contradictories cannot both be true or both false. Here, al-Bajuri explains the specific rule for universal negatives. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly interpreting opposition in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal negative proposition
Look for the form 'No S is P' or 'لا شيء من S هو P' in Arabic. This is the universal negative (sāliba kulliyya).
Example: 'No intoxicant is permissible'.
2
Recall the definition of contradictory
Two propositions are contradictories if they always have opposite truth values: one is true and the other false, never both true or both false.
This was established earlier in the text.
3
Test the candidate contradictory: universal affirmative
If the contradictory of 'No S is P' were 'Every S is P', both could be false (e.g., 'No animal is a man' and 'Every animal is a man' are both false). This violates the rule of contradiction.
Thus universal affirmative cannot be the contradictory.
4
Confirm the correct contradictory: particular affirmative
The only proposition that always takes the opposite truth value is 'Some S is P'. If 'No S is P' is true, 'Some S is P' is false; if 'No S is P' is false, 'Some S is P' is true.
This is the only valid contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦٧) وَإِنْ تَكُنْ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة نَقِيضُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْنِيَّهُ. الباجوري: قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ تَكُنْ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة نَقِيضُهَا ... إلخ) أَيْ وَبِالعَكْسِ، فَفِي كَلَامِهِ اكْتِفَاءٌ لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ نَقِيضُ الصَّالِيَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ : مُوجَبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَجَازَ كَذِبُهُمَا مَعاً، كَمَا مَرَّ .
(67) And if it is a universal negative, its contradictory is a particular affirmative. al-Bajuri: His statement: 'And if it is a universal negative, its contradictory ... etc.' i.e., and vice versa, so in his speech there is sufficiency for what preceded. And the reason why the contradictory of the universal negative is not a universal affirmative is that if it were so, both could be false together, as passed.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the second specific rule for contradiction: the contradictory of a universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B') is a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some A is B'). Al-Bajuri comments that the author's statement implies the converse (i.e., that the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative), so the matn is sufficient. He then justifies why the contradictory of a universal negative is not a universal affirmative: because if it were, both could be false simultaneously. For example, 'No animal is a man' is false, and 'Every animal is a man' is also false; but contradictories cannot both be false, as established earlier. Therefore, the contradictory must be a particular affirmative, which cannot be false when the universal negative is false, and vice versa.

Key Terms
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية

Checkpoint

B) بعض الخمر حلال (Some intoxicant is permissible).
C) بعض الخمر ليس بحلال (Some intoxicant is not permissible).
D) لا شيء من الخمر بحلال (No intoxicant is permissible).

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal negative proposition is always a particular affirmative, never a universal affirmative, because only the particular affirmative guarantees opposite truth values.

The Three Types of Conversion (al-'Aks) in Islamic Logic
Author defines conversion linguistically and technically, then lists its three types with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conversion (simple, concordant contradictory, discordant contradictory) and apply them to convert a given categorical proposition into its converse according to each type.

Core Idea
Conversion is not just swapping terms—there are three distinct operations that preserve truth while transforming propositions.

In this passage, the author defines conversion linguistically and technically, and then enumerates three types of conversion: simple, concordant contradictory, and discordant contradictory. Each type has a specific rule for exchanging terms and a specific effect on the quality (affirmative/negative) of the proposition. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of 'aks'
Identify that 'aks' can refer to the process (masdari) or the resulting proposition (al-qadiyya allati waqa'a al-tahwil ilayha). The definitions are given in both senses.
2
Identify the three types
Recognize the three types: simple (mustawi), concordant contradictory (muwafiq), and discordant contradictory (mukhalif). Note that 'muwafiq' agrees in quality with the original, while 'mukhalif' differs.
3
Learn the rule for each type
For simple conversion: swap the two terms directly. For concordant: replace each term with the contradictory of the other (i.e., both terms are substituted by their negations in opposite positions). For discordant: replace the first term with its contradictory and the second term with the original first term.
The discordant rule changes the quality (affirmative becomes negative, etc.) while preserving truth.
4
Verify preservation of truth with examples
Take the original 'Every human is an animal' (true). Simple converse: 'Some animal is a human' (true, particular affirmative). Concordant: 'Every non-animal is a non-human' (true, universal affirmative). Discordant: 'No non-human is a human' (true, universal negative). Note the changes in quantity and quality.
The author emphasizes that all three types preserve truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي.
قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ في العَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي) أَيْ: فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ.
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ العَكْسَ لُغَةً : مُطْلَقُ التَّبْدِيلِ وَالقَلْبِ؛ بِأَنْ يُجْعَلَ السَّابِقُ لَاحِقاً، وَاللَّاحِقُ سَابِقاً.
وَاصْطِلاحاً يُطْلَقُ بِإِطْلَاقَيْنِ :
أَحَدُهُمَا: إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى القَضِيَّةِ الَّتِي وَقَعَ التَّحْوِيلُ إِلَيْهَا .
وَثَانِيهُمَا : إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى المَعْنَى المَصْدَرِي .
وَعَلَى كُلِّ مِنَ الإِطْلَاقَيْنِ، فَهُوَ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَامٍ :
(1) - الأَوَّلُ : عَكْسٌ مُسْتَوٍ، وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : عَكْسٌ مُسْتَقِيمٌ ؛ لِاسْتِوَاءِ طَرَفَيْهِ وَاسْتِقَامَتِهِمَا بِسَبَبِ سَلَامَةِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ التَّبْدِيلِ بِالنَّقِيضِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ المُصَنِّفُ، وَيُعَرَّفُ :
- عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بـ: «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِالْآخَرِ». - وَعَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الثَّانِي بـ: «أَنَّهُ قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ»... إِلَى آخِرِ مَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ .
(2) - الثَّاني : عَكْسُ نَقِيضٍ مُوَافِقٍ ; لِمُوَافَقَتِهِ لِأَصْلِهِ فِي الكَيْفِ، وَيُعَرَّفُ :
- عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بـ : «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيضِ الآخَرِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِيَّةِ .
- وَعَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الثَّانِي بـ : «أَنَّهُ تَبْدِيلُ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيضِ الآخَرِ»، مَعَ القَيْدِ المَذْكُورِ ; كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» : «كُلُّ مَا لَا حَيَوَانَ لَا إِنْسَانَ».
(3) - الثَّالِثُ : عَكْسُ نَقِيضٍ مُخَالِفٍ ; لِمُخَالَفَتِهِ لِأَصْلِهِ فِي الكَيْفِ، وَيُعَرَّفُ : عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بـ : «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ الطَّرَفِ الأَوَّلِ مِنَ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيضِهَا ، وَتَبْدِيلِ الثَّانِي بِعَيْنِ الأَوَّلِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ دُونَ الكَيْفِ».
الأنبابي: قوله : «كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» ... إلخ) أي : فعكس الموجبة الكلية موجبة كلية، بخلافه على العكس المستوي، فإنَّها تنعكس موجبة جزئية، كما صرح به المصنف، وكذلك بخلافه على العكس المخالف، فإنَّ الكلَّيَّة الموجبة تنعكس كلية سالبة.
A chapter on simple conversion. His statement: (A chapter on simple conversion) meaning: concerning its definition and rulings. And know that conversion (ʿaks) linguistically is absolute substitution and inversion, by making the antecedent subsequent and the subsequent antecedent. Technically, it is used in two senses: one: applying it to the proposition to which the transformation is made; the second: applying it to the infinitive sense. And in both senses, it has three divisions: (1) The first: simple conversion (ʿaks mustawi), also called direct conversion (ʿaks mustaqīm) because of the equality of its two extremes and their soundness due to each being free from substitution by its contradictory. This is the one the author limited himself to. It is defined: in the first sense as 'the proposition composed by exchanging each of the two terms of the proposition with the other'; in the second sense as 'the inversion of the two parts of the proposition'... up to what the author mentioned. (2) The second: concordant contradictory conversion (ʿaks naqīḍ muwāfiq), because it agrees with its original in quality. It is defined: in the first sense as 'the proposition composed by exchanging each of the two terms of the proposition with the contradictory of the other, with preservation of truth and quality'; in the second sense as 'the exchange of each of the two terms of the proposition with the contradictory of the other' with the mentioned condition, as in your saying in the conversion of 'Every human is an animal': 'Every non-animal is a non-human'. (3) The third: discordant contradictory conversion (ʿaks naqīḍ mukhalif), because it differs from its original in quality. It is defined: in the first sense as 'the proposition composed by exchanging the first term of the proposition with its contradictory and exchanging the second with the exact first term, with preservation of truth without quality'. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'as in your saying in the conversion of Every human is an animal... etc.' meaning: the conversion of the universal affirmative is a universal affirmative, contrary to simple conversion, where it converts to a particular affirmative, as stated by the author, and contrary to discordant conversion, where the universal affirmative converts to a universal negative.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'conversion' (ʿaks) and enumerates its three types. The author first explains the linguistic meaning: absolute substitution or reversal. Then the technical meaning has two applications: the resulting proposition (the 'converse') and the process of conversion itself. In both senses, there are three divisions: (1) Simple (mustawi) or direct (mustaqīm) conversion: the terms are swapped directly without replacing either by its contradictory. For example, 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human' (a particular affirmative). This is the most common type. (2) Concordant contradictory conversion (ʿaks naqīḍ muwāfiq): each term is replaced by the contradictory of the other, preserving truth and the quality (affirmative/negative) of the original. Example: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Every non-animal is a non-human' (universal affirmative). This is essentially contraposition by negation. (3) Discordant contradictory conversion (ʿaks naqīḍ mukhalif): only the first term is replaced by its contradictory, while the second term stays as is, preserving truth but changing the quality (e.g., from affirmative to negative). Example: 'Every human is an animal' would convert to something like 'No non-human is a human'? Actually the definition says exchange first term with its contradictory and second with the exact first. For 'Every human is an animal': first term 'human' replaced by its contradictory 'non-human', second term 'animal' replaced by the original first term 'human', so 'Every non-human is a human' would be false. But with change of quality: 'No non-human is a human'? This is confusing; the annotation clarifies that the universal affirmative becomes a universal negative. Al-Anbabi's note clarifies: for the universal affirmative, under simple conversion it becomes particular affirmative; under discordant conversion it becomes universal negative. The example given for concordant conversion is already shown: 'Every human is an animal' to 'Every non-animal is a non-human'. This passage provides the complete classification of conversion types in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
العكس
العكس المستوي
عكس نقيض موافق
عكس نقيض مخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Conversion has three types: simple (swap terms directly), concordant (swap each term with the contradictory of the other, preserving quality), and discordant (replace first term with its contradictory and second with the original first, changing quality); all preserve truth.

The Three Types of Conversion (al-'Aks)
Bajuri defines 'aks linguistically and technically, then divides it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of 'aks and identify which type is used in a given immediate inference.

Core Idea
The three types of 'aks are the tools for immediate inference in Islamic logic.

In this passage, Bajuri unpacks the concept of 'aks (conversion), distinguishing its linguistic and technical meanings, then dividing it into three precise types. Understanding these divisions is crucial for correctly performing immediate inferences—arguments that derive a new proposition from a single premise without a middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of 'aks
Linguistically, 'aks means substitution and reversal. Technically, it is used in two senses: either the proposition resulting from the transformation or the process itself.
2
Understand the two technical senses
In the first sense, 'aks refers to the converted proposition. In the second sense, it refers to the act of converting. Both senses apply to all three types.
3
Distinguish simple conversion from the other two types
Simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) swaps terms without using opposites. The other two types involve substituting with opposites: one preserves quality (conversion of agreeing opposite), the other changes quality (conversion of differing opposite).
4
Apply the definitions to a concrete example
Take the proposition 'Every human is an animal'. For simple conversion, swap terms: 'Every animal is human' (invalid). For agreeing opposite: 'Everything that is not animal is not human' (valid). For differing opposite: 'Nothing that is not animal is human' (valid, but quality changes).
The example demonstrates how truth is preserved in the latter two, even if quality changes in the third.
5
Recognize the author's focus on simple conversion
Bajuri notes that the author (al-Sullam) limited the chapter to simple conversion because it occurs more frequently. This prioritization simplifies the study of immediate inferences.

فَصْلٌ فِي العَكْسِ المُسْتَوِي. قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ فِي العَكْسِ المُسْتَوِي) أَيْ: فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ وَأَحْكَامِهِ. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ العَكْسَ لُغَةٌ : مُطْلَقُ التَّبْدِيلِ وَالقَلْبِ؛ بِأَنْ يُجْعَلَ السَّابِقُ لَاحِقاً، وَاللَّاحِقُ سَابِقاً. وَاصْطِلاحاً يُطْلَقُ بِإِطْلَاقَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا: إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى القَضِيَّةِ الَّتِي وَقَعَ التَّحْوِيلُ إِلَيْهَا . وَثَانِيهُمَا : إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى المَعْنَى المَصْدَرِي . وَعَلَى كُلِّ مِنَ الإِطْلَاقَيْنِ، فَهُوَ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَامٍ : (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : عَكْسُ مُسْتَوِ ، وَيُقَالُ لَهُ : عَكْسٌ مُسْتَقِيمٌ ; لِاسْتِوَاءِ طَرَفَيْهِ وَاسْتِقَامَتِهِمَا بِسَبَبِ سَلَامَةِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ التَّبْدِيلِ بِالنَّقِيضِ، وَهَذَا هُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ المُصَنِّفُ، وَيُعَرَّفُ : عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بـ: «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِالْآخَرِ». - وَعَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الثَّانِي بـ: «أَنَّهُ قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ»... إِلَى آخِرِ مَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ . (۲) - الثَّاني : عَكْسُ نَقِيض مُوَافِقٍ ; لِمُوَافَقَتِهِ لِأَصْلِهِ فِي الكَيْفِ، وَيُعَرَّفُ : - عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بـ : «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيض الآخَرِ. مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِيَّةِ . - وَعَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الثَّانِي بِـ : «أَنَّهُ تَبْدِيلُ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفَيِ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيضِ الآخَرِ»، مَعَ القَيْدِ المَذْكُورِ ; كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ : كُلُّ مَا لَا حَيَوَانٍ لَا إِنْسَانُ». (۳) - الثَّالِثُ : عَكْسُ نَقِيض مُخَالِفٍ ; لِمُخَالَفَتِهِ لِأَصْلِهِ فِي الكَيْفِ، وَيُعَرَّفُ : عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الأَوَّلِ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ القَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي تَرَكَّبَتْ بِتَبْدِيلِ الطَّرَفِ الأَوَّلِ مِنَ القَضِيَّةِ بِنَقِيضِ الثَّانِي مِنْهَا ، وَتَبْدِيلِ الثَّانِي بِعَيْنِ الأَوَّلِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ دُونَ الكَيْفِ ) . - وَعَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ الثَّانِي بِـ : «أَنَّهُ تَبْدِيلُ الطَّرَفِ الأَوَّلِ مِنَ القَضِيَّة بِنَقِيضِ الثَّانِي إلخ» ; كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَا شَيْء مِمَّا لَا حَيَوَانٍ بِإِنْسَانٍ». وَإِنَّمَا اقْتَصَرَ المُصَنِّفُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ ; لأَنَّهُ أَكْثَرُ دَوَرَاناً مِنْ غَيْرِهِ، فَافْهَمْ .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. His statement: (Chapter on Simple Conversion) meaning: concerning its definition and rules. Know that 'aks linguistically is absolute substitution and reversal: making the earlier later and the later earlier. Technically, it is used in two senses: one is applying it to the proposition into which the transformation is made; the second is applying it to the infinitive meaning. In both senses, it is three types: (1) First: Simple conversion, also called direct conversion; because its two ends are equal and straight due to each being safe from substitution with the opposite. This is what the author limited himself to. It is defined – in the first sense: 'the proposition composed by substituting each of the two terms of the proposition with the other'; in the second sense: 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' up to what the author mentioned. (2) Second: Conversion of the agreeing opposite; because it agrees with its original in quality. It is defined – in the first sense: 'the proposition composed by substituting each of the two terms of the proposition with the opposite of the other, while preserving truth and quality'; in the second sense: 'substituting each of the two terms of the proposition with the opposite of the other' with the mentioned condition, as in your statement in converting 'Every human is an animal': 'Everything that is not animal is not human'. (3) Third: Conversion of the differing opposite; because it differs from its original in quality. It is defined – in the first sense: 'the proposition composed by substituting the first term of the proposition with the opposite of the second from it, and substituting the second with the identical first, while preserving truth but not quality'; in the second sense: 'substituting the first term of the proposition with the opposite of the second etc.' as in your statement in converting the mentioned example: 'Nothing that is not animal is human'. And the author only limited himself to the first because it occurs more frequently than others, so understand.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concept of conversion (al-'aks). It opens by clarifying that the chapter heading 'Simple Conversion' concerns its definition and rules. Bajuri then explains the linguistic meaning: 'aks is simply substitution and reversal, like making the antecedent consequent. Technically, the term is used in two ways: either referring to the proposition resulting from conversion (the transformed proposition) or to the process itself (the infinitive meaning). For both usages, there are three types of conversion. The first is simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi), also called direct conversion, where the subject and predicate swap places without any substitution with their opposites. This is the type the author (al-Sullam) focuses on. Its definition: in the first technical sense, it is the proposition formed by swapping the two terms of the original; in the second sense, it is the act of reversing the two parts. The second type is conversion of the agreeing opposite (al-'aks al-naqid al-muwafiq), where each term is replaced by the opposite of the other, preserving both truth and quality (affirmative/negative). For example, converting 'Every human is an animal' yields 'Everything that is not animal is not human' (a universal negative). The third type is conversion of the differing opposite (al-'aks al-naqid al-mukhalif), where the first term is replaced by the opposite of the second, and the second by the identical first, preserving truth but changing quality. For example, from the same original, we get 'Nothing that is not animal is human' (a universal negative), which differs in quality from the original affirmative. Bajuri concludes by noting that the author limited the chapter to simple conversion because it is more common. This classification is crucial for understanding immediate inferences in logic.

Key Terms
العكس
العكس المستوي
عكس النقيض الموافق
عكس النقيض المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of 'aks preserves truth and quality but substitutes each term with the opposite of the other?
A) Simple conversion
B) Conversion of the agreeing opposite
C) Conversion of the differing opposite
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The three types of 'aks—simple, agreeing opposite, differing opposite—are distinguished by how terms are substituted and whether quality is preserved, forming the foundation for valid immediate inferences in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram' into its three types of 'aks (simple, agreeing opposite, differing opposite).
?
Identify which type of 'aks is used in the following inference: 'If the prayer is valid, then wudu was performed. Therefore, if wudu was not performed, the prayer is not valid.' (Hint: it involves substituting with the opposite and preserving truth but changing quality.)
