The Definition of Simple Conversion and Natural Order
Bajuri defends the definition 'قلب جزئي القضية' against an objection regarding disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' excludes disjunctive conditionals and justify the natural order condition within the definition.

Core Idea
The definition itself implies natural order; no extra condition needed.

In this passage, Bajuri defends the matn's definition of simple conversion against an objection about disjunctive conditionals. He argues that the phrase 'reversing the two parts' already presupposes a natural order, so disjunctives are excluded. Let's see how the logic works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being defended
Find the phrase 'قلب جزئي القضية' in the Arabic and note that it is presented as the definition of simple conversion (عكس مستوي). Understand that it is preferred over an alternative definition ('subject becomes predicate, predicate becomes subject') because it covers both categorical and conjunctive conditionals.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that the definition would include disjunctive conditionals (شرطية منفصلة), which have no conversion because their parts lack natural order (ترتيب طبيعي). See how the objector claims the definition should be qualified with 'having natural order'.
3
Analyze the answer
Bajuri answers that the wording 'reversing the two parts' itself implies natural order, because 'reversal' presupposes a natural position for each part. Additionally, the author will later explicitly require natural order. Thus no separate qualifier is needed.
4
See how the definition excludes other conversions
The definition excludes the other two types of conversion (عكس النقيض الموافق and عكس النقيض المخالف) because they involve opposites, not simple reversal of the two parts. It also excludes construct phrases like 'Zayd's striker' because 'of the proposition' restricts it to propositional parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : ((العَكْسُ أَي : المُسْتَوِي؛ بِدَلِيلِ التَّرْجَمَةِ، وَلِأَنَّهُ المُنْصَرِفُ إِلَيْهِ اللَّفْظُ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ، وَمِنْ هَذَا يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ تَقييد المُصَنِّفِ بِذَلِكَ فِي التَّرْجَمَةِ لِلإِيضَاحِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّة ... إلخ) هُوَ أَوْلَى مِنْ قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ: «أَنْ يَصِيرَ المَوْضُوعُ مَحْمُولاً ، وَالمَحْمُولُ مَوْضُوعاً » ; لِشُمُولِهِ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ . نَعَمْ؛ يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّهُ يَشْمَلُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ المُنْفَصِلَةَ ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا ; لِعَدَمِ التَّرْتِيبِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ بَيْنَ جُزْأَيْهَا ، فَكَانَ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يُقَيِّدَ القَضِيَّةَ بـ: «كَوْنِهَا ذَاتَ تَرْتِيبٍ طَبِيعِيِّ». وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ مُغْنِ عَنْ ذَلِكَ القَيْدِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا لَهُ مَوْضِعٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ ; عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْع . إلخ» . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ : بِإِضَافَةِ القَلْبِ إِلَى الجُزْأَيْنِ : كُلٌّ مِنْ عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ ، وَعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُخَالِفِ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ قَلْبُ الجُزْأَيْنِ، بَلِ الأَوَّلُ قَلْبُ نَقِيضِهِمَا ، وَالثَّانِي قَلْبُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَنَقِيض الآخَرِ ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . - وَبِإِضَافَةِ الجُرْأَيْنِ إِلَى القَضِيَّةِ : قَلْبُ جُرْأَيْ غَيْرِهَا :ـ المُرَكَبِ الإِضَافِيِّ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ ضَارِبُ غُلَام»: «غُلَامٌ ضَارِبٌ» .  الأنبابي قوله : (عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ... إلخ) أي: فاستغنى عن القيد هنا بما يأتي. اهـ صبان (۱) قال العطار : هذا لا يتمُّ ، فإنَّ التَّعاريف مستقلةٌ قائمة برأسها، والأصل أن تذكر فيها القيود، ولا يجعل ما يذكر في كلام أجنبي [ب/ ۳۲] عنِ التَّعريف دليلاً لقيد فيها، فتأمل(٢).
His statement: ('Aks meaning: simple conversion, as evidence of the chapter heading, and because the term is applied to it when unqualified. From this it is known that the author's qualification in the heading is for clarification. And his statement: (Reversing the two parts of the proposition... etc.) is better than the statement of some: 'that the subject becomes predicate and the predicate becomes subject' because it includes both categorical and conditional conjunctive propositions. Yes, an objection arises: it includes disjunctive conditionals, although they have no conversion because there is no natural order between their two parts; so one should qualify the proposition as 'being with natural order'. And the answer is that his statement 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' suffices for that condition because it implies that each part has a natural position; moreover, the author will explicitly state that in his saying: 'And conversion is in something naturally ordered etc.' And it is not hidden that by adding 'reversal' to 'the two parts', it excludes both conversion of the agreeing opposite and conversion of the differing opposite; because it is not reversal of the two parts, but rather the first is reversal of their opposites, and the second is reversal of one of them and the opposite of the other, as known from what passed. And by adding 'the two parts' to 'the proposition', it excludes reversal of two parts of something else: the construct compound, like your saying in converting 'Zayd's striker' [to] 'a striker of Zayd'. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (Moreover, the author will explicitly state that in his saying... etc.) means: so he dispensed with the condition here because of what will come. End. Al-Sabban (1) said: Al-Attar said: This does not work, because definitions are independent and stand on their own; the principle is that conditions should be mentioned in them, and one should not make what is mentioned in foreign speech [i.e., elsewhere] a proof for a condition in them, so reflect (2).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of simple conversion ('aks mustawi). Bajuri begins by explaining that the term 'aks in the matn refers specifically to simple conversion, as indicated by the chapter heading and because it is the default meaning. He then defends the matn's definition 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' (قلب جزئي القضية) as superior to an alternative definition 'the subject becomes predicate and vice versa' because it covers both categorical and conditional conjunctive propositions. However, an objection is raised: the definition would also include disjunctive conditional propositions, which have no conversion because their parts lack a natural order. The answer is that the wording 'reversing the two parts' itself implies a natural order, since 'reversal' presupposes that each part has a natural position. Furthermore, the author (al-Sullam) will later explicitly require natural order. Bajuri then shows that the definition excludes the other two types of conversion (agreeing and differing opposite) because they involve reversing opposites, not the simple parts. It also excludes conversions of construct phrases like 'Zayd's striker' because 'reversal of the two parts of the proposition' specifically refers to the proposition being converted. Anbabi notes that the qualification from later text suffices. Al-Sabban reports al-Attar's critique that definitions should be self-contained and not rely on later statements. This is a classic example of the hashiya methodology, where super-commentators refine the adequacy of definitions.

قلب جزئي القضية
الترتيب الطبيعي
الشرطية المنفصلة

Key Takeaway
The definition of simple conversion as 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' inherently implies a natural order, thus excluding disjunctive conditionals without needing an explicit qualifier.

Self-Contained Definitions in Mantiq
Bajuri defends the definition 'قلب جزئي القضية' against an objection regarding disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the requirement that logical definitions be self-contained, using al-Attar's critique as an example.

Core Idea
A definition must stand on its own, not borrow conditions from elsewhere.

In the final part of the passage, al-Attar critiques the idea that a definition can rely on a later statement to supply a missing condition. This raises an important methodological principle: definitions in mantiq should be complete and self-sufficient. Let's explore why this matters for rigorous logical work.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Attar's objection
In the passage, al-Attar says of al-Anbabi's defense that it 'does not work' because definitions are independent and stand on their own. The defense used the fact that the author will later mention natural order to justify the definition's completeness. Al-Attar rejects this.
2
Understand the principle
Al-Attar's principle is that definitions should include all necessary conditions explicitly. One should not rely on statements made elsewhere (كلام أجنبي) to supply a condition. This is because definitions are foundational and must be understandable without referring to other parts of the text.
3
Apply the principle to the case
In the earlier defense, al-Anbabi said the qualification of natural order will come later, so it is not needed in the definition. Al-Attar argues this is insufficient; the definition itself should contain the condition. Consider whether you agree and how this affects the adequacy of the definition.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This principle is crucial for all definitions in mantiq and other Islamic sciences. A poorly defined term can lead to confusion and invalid arguments. Think of an example from usul al-fiqh where a definition lacking a condition caused controversy.

قَوْلُهُ : ((العَكْسُ أَي : المُسْتَوِي؛ بِدَلِيلِ التَّرْجَمَةِ، وَلِأَنَّهُ المُنْصَرِفُ إِلَيْهِ اللَّفْظُ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ، وَمِنْ هَذَا يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ تَقييد المُصَنِّفِ بِذَلِكَ فِي التَّرْجَمَةِ لِلإِيضَاحِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّة ... إلخ) هُوَ أَوْلَى مِنْ قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ: «أَنْ يَصِيرَ المَوْضُوعُ مَحْمُولاً ، وَالمَحْمُولُ مَوْضُوعاً » ; لِشُمُولِهِ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ . نَعَمْ؛ يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّهُ يَشْمَلُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ المُنْفَصِلَةَ ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا ; لِعَدَمِ التَّرْتِيبِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ بَيْنَ جُزْأَيْهَا ، فَكَانَ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يُقَيِّدَ القَضِيَّةَ بـ: «كَوْنِهَا ذَاتَ تَرْتِيبٍ طَبِيعِيِّ». وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ مُغْنِ عَنْ ذَلِكَ القَيْدِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا لَهُ مَوْضِعٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ ; عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْع . إلخ» . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ : بِإِضَافَةِ القَلْبِ إِلَى الجُزْأَيْنِ : كُلٌّ مِنْ عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ ، وَعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُخَالِفِ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ قَلْبُ الجُزْأَيْنِ، بَلِ الأَوَّلُ قَلْبُ نَقِيضِهِمَا ، وَالثَّانِي قَلْبُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَنَقِيض الآخَرِ ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . - وَبِإِضَافَةِ الجُرْأَيْنِ إِلَى القَضِيَّةِ : قَلْبُ جُرْأَيْ غَيْرِهَا :ـ المُرَكَبِ الإِضَافِيِّ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ ضَارِبُ غُلَام»: «غُلَامٌ ضَارِبٌ» .  الأنبابي قوله : (عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ... إلخ) أي: فاستغنى عن القيد هنا بما يأتي. اهـ صبان (۱) قال العطار : هذا لا يتمُّ ، فإنَّ التَّعاريف مستقلةٌ قائمة برأسها، والأصل أن تذكر فيها القيود، ولا يجعل ما يذكر في كلام أجنبي [ب/ ۳۲] عنِ التَّعريف دليلاً لقيد فيها، فتأمل(٢).
His statement: ('Aks meaning: simple conversion, as evidence of the chapter heading, and because the term is applied to it when unqualified. From this it is known that the author's qualification in the heading is for clarification. And his statement: (Reversing the two parts of the proposition... etc.) is better than the statement of some: 'that the subject becomes predicate and the predicate becomes subject' because it includes both categorical and conditional conjunctive propositions. Yes, an objection arises: it includes disjunctive conditionals, although they have no conversion because there is no natural order between their two parts; so one should qualify the proposition as 'being with natural order'. And the answer is that his statement 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' suffices for that condition because it implies that each part has a natural position; moreover, the author will explicitly state that in his saying: 'And conversion is in something naturally ordered etc.' And it is not hidden that by adding 'reversal' to 'the two parts', it excludes both conversion of the agreeing opposite and conversion of the differing opposite; because it is not reversal of the two parts, but rather the first is reversal of their opposites, and the second is reversal of one of them and the opposite of the other, as known from what passed. And by adding 'the two parts' to 'the proposition', it excludes reversal of two parts of something else: the construct compound, like your saying in converting 'Zayd's striker' [to] 'a striker of Zayd'. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (Moreover, the author will explicitly state that in his saying... etc.) means: so he dispensed with the condition here because of what will come. End. Al-Sabban (1) said: Al-Attar said: This does not work, because definitions are independent and stand on their own; the principle is that conditions should be mentioned in them, and one should not make what is mentioned in foreign speech [i.e., elsewhere] a proof for a condition in them, so reflect (2).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of simple conversion ('aks mustawi). Bajuri begins by explaining that the term 'aks in the matn refers specifically to simple conversion, as indicated by the chapter heading and because it is the default meaning. He then defends the matn's definition 'reversing the two parts of the proposition' (قلب جزئي القضية) as superior to an alternative definition 'the subject becomes predicate and vice versa' because it covers both categorical and conditional conjunctive propositions. However, an objection is raised: the definition would also include disjunctive conditional propositions, which have no conversion because their parts lack a natural order. The answer is that the wording 'reversing the two parts' itself implies a natural order, since 'reversal' presupposes that each part has a natural position. Furthermore, the author (al-Sullam) will later explicitly require natural order. Bajuri then shows that the definition excludes the other two types of conversion (agreeing and differing opposite) because they involve reversing opposites, not the simple parts. It also excludes conversions of construct phrases like 'Zayd's striker' because 'reversal of the two parts of the proposition' specifically refers to the proposition being converted. Anbabi notes that the qualification from later text suffices. Al-Sabban reports al-Attar's critique that definitions should be self-contained and not rely on later statements. This is a classic example of the hashiya methodology, where super-commentators refine the adequacy of definitions.

Key Terms
التعاريف مستقلة
كلام أجنبي
الأنبابي
العطار

Key Takeaway
Logical definitions must be self-contained; they cannot rely on later statements to supply missing conditions.

Immediate Inference

Refining the Definition of Simple Conversion
Clarifies that al-aks refers to simple conversion, excludes disjunctives and contraposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the precise definition of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) and distinguish it from contraposition and conversion of non-propositional phrases.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps the two parts of a proposition with natural order, excluding disjunctives and contrapositions.

In this lesson, we examine how the commentator refines the definition of simple conversion to exclude disjunctive conditionals and other types of conversion. The base definition 'swapping the two parts of the proposition' is shown to implicitly require natural order, thereby excluding disjunctives, and its wording excludes contrapositions and possessive constructions. Understanding this refinement ensures precision in immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being refined
The base definition under discussion is 'swapping the two parts of the proposition' (قلب جزأي القضية). This is preferred over saying 'the subject becomes predicate and vice versa' because it also covers conjunctive conditionals.
The definition is from the matn; the commentator (Bajuri) is explaining its advantages.
2
Spot the objection
An objection arises: the definition seems to include disjunctive conditionals, which have no simple converse because their parts lack a natural order (لا ترتيب طبيعي).
Disjunctives are symmetric; swapping parts does not change meaning, so conversion is meaningless.
3
Examine the solution
The answer is twofold: (1) The phrase 'قلب جزأي القضية' already implies each part has a natural position, so disjunctives are excluded. (2) The author will later explicitly restrict conversion to propositions with natural order.
Al-Anbabi supports this; al-'Attar criticizes relying on later statements, but the primary answer stands.
4
Determine what is excluded
By adding 'swapping' to 'the two parts', the definition excludes both types of contraposition (عكس النقيض الموافق والمخالف) because they involve swapping contradictories. By adding 'the two parts' to 'the proposition', it excludes possessive phrases (المركب الإضافي) like 'beater of a boy'.
This shows the precision of the definition in excluding non-simple conversions and non-propositional phrases.

وَإِنَّمَا اقْتَصَرَ المُصَنِّفُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ أَكْثَرُ دَوَرَاناً مِنْ غَيْرِهِ، فَافْهَمْ .
قَوْلُهُ : ((العَكْسُ أَي : المُسْتَوِي؛ بِدَلِيلِ التَّرْجَمَةِ، وَلِأَنَّهُ المُنْصَرِفُ إِلَيْهِ اللَّفْظُ عِنْدَ الإِطْلَاقِ، وَمِنْ هَذَا يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ تَقييد المُصَنِّفِ بِذَلِكَ فِي التَّرْجَمَةِ لِلإِيضَاحِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّة ... إلخ) هُوَ أَوْلَى مِنْ قَوْلِ بَعْضِهِمْ: «أَنْ يَصِيرَ المَوْضُوعُ مَحْمُولاً ، وَالمَحْمُولُ مَوْضُوعاً » ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ .
نَعَمْ؛ يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ : أَنَّهُ يَشْمَلُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ المُنْفَصِلَةَ ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَا عَكْسَ لَهَا ؛ لِعَدَمِ التَّرْتِيبِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ بَيْنَ جُزْأَيْهَا ، فَكَانَ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يُقَيِّدَ القَضِيَّةَ بـ: «كَوْنِهَا ذَاتَ تَرْتِيبٍ طَبِيعِيِّ».
وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ مُغْنِ عَنْ ذَلِكَ القَيْدِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا لَهُ مَوْضِعٌ طَبِيعِيٌّ ؛ عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْع . إلخ» . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يَخْرُجُ :
بِإِضَافَةِ القَلْبِ إِلَى الجُزْأَيْنِ : كُلٌّ مِنْ عَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ ، وَعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُخَالِفِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ قَلْبُ الجُزْأَيْنِ، بَلِ الأَوَّلُ قَلْبُ نَقِيضِهِمَا ، وَالثَّانِي قَلْبُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَنَقِيض الآخَرِ ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . - وَبِإِضَافَةِ الجُرْأَيْنِ إِلَى القَضِيَّةِ : قَلْبُ جُرْأَيْ غَيْرِهَا :ـ المُرَكَبِ الإِضَافِيِّ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ ضَارِبُ غُلَام»: «غُلَامٌ ضَارِبٌ» .
الأنبابي
قوله : (عَلَى أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيُصَرِّحُ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ... إلخ) أي: فاستغنى عن القيد هنا بما يأتي. اهـ
صبان (۱)
قال العطار : هذا لا يتمُّ ، فإنَّ التَّعاريف مستقلةٌ قائمة برأسها، والأصل أن تذكر فيها القيود، ولا يجعل ما يذكر في كلام أجنبي [ب/ ۳۲] عنِ التَّعريف دليلاً لقيد فيها، فتأمل(٢).
And according to the second sense [it is]: 'It is the replacement of the first term of the proposition with the contradictory of the second, etc.' as in your saying in the converse of the mentioned example: 'Nothing that is not animal is human.' And the author only restricted himself to the first because it is more frequent than others, so understand. His saying: 'Conversion' means simple; by the evidence of the chapter heading, and because the word is generally understood that way. From this it is known that the author's qualification in the heading is for clarification. And his saying: 'Swapping the two parts of the proposition etc.' is preferable to the saying of some: 'The subject becomes predicate and the predicate becomes subject' because it includes both categorical and conditional propositions of the conjunctive type. Yes, it is objected that it includes the disjunctive conditional, even though it has no converse due to the lack of natural order between its two parts. So he should have restricted the proposition by: 'it being one with natural order.' It is answered that his saying 'swapping the two parts of the proposition' suffices for that restriction, because it implies that each part has a natural position; moreover, the author will state that later in his saying: 'and conversion is in something ordered by nature, etc.' And it is not hidden that by adding 'swapping' to 'the two parts' there is excluded: both 'conversion of the contradictory that agrees' and 'conversion of the contradictory that differs' because it is not swapping the two parts, but rather the first is swapping their contradictories, and the second is swapping one of them and the contradictory of the other, as known from what preceded. And by adding 'the two parts' to 'the proposition' [there is excluded]: swapping the two parts of something other than it, [namely] the composite phrase, as when you say in the converse of 'beater of a boy': 'a boy beater'. Al-Anbabi: His saying: 'Moreover, the author will state that in his saying...' meaning: he dispensed with the restriction here by what will come. End. Al-Sabban: Al-'Attar said: This is not proper, for definitions are independent and stand by themselves, and the principle is that restrictions be mentioned in them, and what is mentioned in speech extrinsic to the definition is not made evidence for a restriction in it. So ponder.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) found in the commentary on al-Sullam. It begins by referencing an alternative definition of conversion that uses contraposition (replacing the first term with the contradictory of the second), but notes that the author (of the matn) restricts himself to the first definition (simple conversion) because it is more common. The commentator (Bajuri) then explains that the word 'conversion' in the definition means simple conversion, supported by the chapter heading and common usage. He argues that the definition 'swapping the two parts of the proposition' is preferable to saying 'the subject becomes predicate and vice versa' because it also covers conjunctive conditionals (e.g., 'if p then q'), not just categoricals. However, an objection arises: this definition also includes disjunctive conditionals (e.g., 'either p or q'), which cannot be converted because there is no natural order between their parts. The answer is twofold: first, the phrase 'swapping the two parts' already implies a natural order (each part has a natural position), and second, the author will later explicitly restrict conversion to propositions with natural order. The passage then explains what the definition excludes by its phrasing: (1) adding 'swapping' to 'the two parts' excludes 'conversion by contraposition' (both the agreeing and differing types) because those involve swapping contradictories, not just the original parts; (2) adding 'the two parts' to 'the proposition' excludes swapping parts of non-propositional phrases like 'beater of a boy' (an idafa construction), because that is not a proposition. Finally, a marginal note from al-Anbabi supports the answer. A further critical comment from al-'Attar (via al-Sabban) objects that definitions should be self-sufficient and not rely on later statements; but this is a meta-critical note.

All examples are standard in Islamic logic: 'Nothing that is not animal is human' is a contrapositive of 'Every human is animal'; 'beater of a boy' is a possessive construction, not a proposition. The discussion uses technical terminology proper to the tradition.

عكس النقيض الموافق
عكس النقيض المخالف
الشرطية المنفصلة
المركب الإضافي

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion strictly swaps the two parts of a proposition that has natural order, excluding disjunctives and non-propositional phrases.

Immediate Inference

Definition and Conditions of Simple Conversion
Defines conversion with conditions and counterexamples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential conditions for simple conversion and apply them to check whether a given conversion is valid.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth, quality, and quantity necessarily.

The matn defines conversion as swapping the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth and quality. The commentator, al-Bajuri, elaborates on each condition with counterexamples, showing what happens when truth, quality, or quantity is not preserved. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly performing conversions in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Take the given categorical proposition and write it in subject-predicate form. For example, 'Every human is an animal' has subject 'human' and predicate 'animal'.
The proposition must be categorical (not conditional) for simple conversion.
2
Swap the subject and predicate
Exchange the two parts: the subject becomes predicate and the predicate becomes subject. For 'Every human is an animal', the swapped form is 'Every animal is human'.
3
Check preservation of truth
Verify that the truth of the original necessarily implies the truth of the converse. If the converse is false (as in 'Every animal is human'), the conversion fails.
Truth must be preserved on the face of necessity, not accidentally.
4
Check preservation of quality
Ensure the affirmative/negative status remains the same. Converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Some animal is not human' changes quality, so it is invalid.
5
Check preservation of quantity
The universal/particular status should remain the same unless explicitly excluded. Converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Animal is human' changes from particular to universal, losing quantity.
The author excludes cases like universal affirmative (A) which converts to particular affirmative (I).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي
(٦٨) «العَكْسُ : قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّهُ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِيَّة
الباجوري
-
وَبِقَوْلِهِ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الصِّدْقُ ، كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ».
- وَبِقَوْلِهِ: وَالكَيْفِيَّةِ : مَا إِذَا لَمْ تَبْقَ الكَيْفِيَّةُ ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ »: لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانِ» . - وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «وَالكَمْ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الكَمُ إِلَّا فِيمَا اسْتَتْنَاهُ المُصَنِّفُ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ» : «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانُ». فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَتَأَنَّى قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَامَ»؛ لِأَنَّ الفِعْلَ لَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُهُ مَوْضُوعاً .
أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصِحَ جَعْلُهُ بِذَاتِهِ مَوْضُوعاً، يُجْعَلْ فِي مَحَلِّهِ مَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْضُوعاً : بَعْضُ القَائِمِ أَوْ بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ، وَيُرْتَكَبُ هَذَا فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، فَيُقَالُ: بَعْضُ : القَائِمِ، أَوْ : بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ زَيْدٌ».
لَا يُقَالُ : لَمْ يَحْصُلْ قَلْبُ لِجُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، حَتَّى يُسَمَّى ذَلِكَ: «عَكْساً». لأَنَّا نَقُولُ : المَدَارُ فِي مِثْلِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى نِيَّةِ المُتَكَلِّمِ ؛ بِأَنْ يَنْوِيَ أَنَّ مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً وَبِالعَكْسِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ تَقْدِيمُ وَتَأخِيرٌ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ .
قَوْلُهُ: (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ) أَيْ : عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ»: «كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ»، فَإِنَّ بَقَاءَ الصِّدْقِ فِي ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ أَمْرُ اتَّفَقَ مِنْ مُسَاوَاةِ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوع؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَوْ عُكِسَ كُلِّيَّةٌ .
وَلَمْ يَقُلِ المُصَنِّفُ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الأَصْلِ كَذِبُ العَكْسِ.
الأنبابي
قوله : (فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ زَيْدٌ قَامَ) أي: مِن كلِّ تركيب كان المحكوم به فعلاً متقدماً، والمحكوم عليه فاعلاً مؤخّراً، ونظيره تركيب الفعل ونائبه .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. (68) 'Conversion: swapping the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth and quality.' Al-Bajuri: By his saying 'with preservation of truth' [it excludes] when truth is not preserved, as when you say in the converse of 'Every human is an animal': 'Every animal is human.' And by his saying 'and quality' [it excludes] when quality is not preserved, as when you say in the converse of 'Some human is an animal': 'Some animal is not human.' And by his saying 'and quantity' [it excludes] when quantity is not preserved except what the author excluded, as when you say in the converse of 'Some human is an animal': 'Animal is human.' So if it is said: The swapping of the two parts of the proposition is not possible in something like your saying 'Zaid stood' because the verb cannot be made a subject. It is answered: Even if it cannot be made a subject in itself, put in its place what can be a subject: some of the standing one or some of those who stood, and this is done in something like your saying 'Stood Zaid', so it is said: 'Some of the standing one, or some of those who stood, is Zaid.' It should not be said: The swapping of the two parts of the mentioned proposition has not occurred, so that this is called 'conversion'. Because we say: The criterion in such matters is the intention of the speaker; that he intends that what was subject becomes predicate and vice versa, even if there is no verbal advance or postponement. Let it be pondered. His saying: ('with preservation of truth') means: on the face of necessity, to exclude what is not on the face of necessity but on the face of coincidence, as in your saying in the converse of 'Every human is rational': 'Every rational is human', for preservation of truth in that is not on the face of necessity, but a matter that coincided from the equality of the predicate to the subject, as evidenced by its failure in your saying: 'Every human is animal' if converted universally. And the author did not say 'with preservation of truth and falsehood' because falsity of the original does not necessitate falsity of the converse. Al-Anbabi: His saying: ('in something like your saying Zaid stood') means: from every construction where the predicated is a verb that comes first, and the subject is an agent that comes later, and its parallel is the construction of the verb and its deputy.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the concept of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) in logic. It opens with the definition from the matn: 'Conversion is the swapping of the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth and quality.' The commentator (Bajuri) then explains each condition using counterexamples. First, preserving truth: if you convert 'Every human is an animal' to 'Every animal is human', the converse is false, so conversion fails. Second, preserving quality: converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Some animal is not human' changes affirmative to negative, so quality is lost. Third, preserving quantity (introduced implicitly): converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Animal is human' changes particular to universal, losing quantity. The commentator notes that the author excluded certain cases where quantity might not be preserved. The text then raises an objection: how can you swap parts in a verb-initial sentence like 'Zaid stood' (which is analyzed as 'Stood Zaid' with the verb taking the place of predicate)? The answer explains that even if the verb cannot literally be a subject, you can substitute its nominal form (e.g., 'some of the standing one') to perform the conversion, and the essence of conversion relies on the speaker's intention rather than mere word order. Finally, a key refinement: the condition 'preservation of truth' must be understood as necessary truth (على وجه اللزوم), not accidental. For example, converting 'Every human is rational' to 'Every rational is human' yields a true converse because the terms are coextensive, but this is accidental truth, not necessary—evidence being that if you convert 'Every human is animal' (where the terms are not coextensive), the converse is false. Thus, true conversion requires that the original truth logically necessitates the truth of the converse. The commentator also clarifies why the author did not say 'preservation of truth and falsehood': because falsity of the original does not guarantee falsity of the converse. The marginal note from al-Anbabi explains the verb-initial construction further, saying it includes any construction where the predicate is a verb preceding the subject.

All examples are from Islamic logic: human-animal, human-rational, etc. These are standard examples in the tradition.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion requires swapping subject and predicate while necessarily preserving truth, quality, and quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Converting Verb-Initial Sentences
Addresses objection about verb-initial sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the correct converse of a verb-initial proposition by substituting a nominal equivalent.

Core Idea
Even if a verb cannot be a subject, conversion is possible by using its nominal form.

An objection is raised: how can you swap parts in a verb-initial sentence like 'Zaid stood' (originally 'Stood Zaid') since the verb cannot function as a subject? The commentator answers that we can substitute a nominal expression such as 'some of the standing one' and then perform the conversion. This lesson shows that the essence of conversion lies in the speaker's intention, not merely in word order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb-initial proposition
Take a sentence where the predicate is a verb that comes before the subject, e.g., 'Stood Zaid' (قَامَ زَيْدٌ). This is typical of Arabic verbal sentences.
The verb here is 'stood' and the subject is 'Zaid'.
2
Recognize the obstacle
The verb cannot simply become the subject in a new proposition because a verb cannot be a subject in Arabic grammar.
3
Substitute a nominal form
Replace the verb with a nominal equivalent that can serve as subject, such as 'some of the standing one' (بعض القائم) or 'some of those who stood' (بعض من قام).
The substitution preserves the meaning.
4
Perform the conversion
Now swap: the original subject 'Zaid' becomes predicate, and the nominal substitute becomes subject. The result: 'Some of the standing one is Zaid' (بعض القائم زيد) or 'Some of those who stood is Zaid'.
This is the converse, though the word order does not literally swap; the intention suffices.
5
Validate by intention
Understand that the definition of conversion does not require literal word order change; it only requires that what was originally subject becomes predicate and vice versa in the speaker's intention.
The commentator says: 'the criterion is the speaker's intention.'

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the concept of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) in logic. It opens with the definition from the matn: 'Conversion is the swapping of the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth and quality.' The commentator (Bajuri) then explains each condition using counterexamples. The marginal note from al-Anbabi explains the verb-initial construction further, saying it includes any construction where the predicate is a verb preceding the subject.

Necessary Truth in Conversion
Refines truth condition to necessary truth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and accidental truth in conversion and identify why accidental truth does not constitute valid conversion.

Core Idea
Conversion requires that the truth of the original necessarily entails the truth of the converse, not just coincidence.

The commentator clarifies that the condition 'preservation of truth' must be understood as necessary truth (على وجه اللزوم). He gives the example of converting 'Every human is rational' to 'Every rational is human' – although the converse is true, this truth is accidental because the terms are coextensive. He shows that if you convert 'Every human is animal', the converse is false, proving the necessity of the condition. This lesson refines our understanding of the truth condition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic truth condition
The definition of conversion requires that the truth of the original is preserved in the converse. But not all cases of truth preservation are equal.
2
Identify the accidental truth example
Consider the conversion of 'Every human is rational' to 'Every rational is human'. Both are true, but this truth is accidental because 'human' and 'rational' have the same extension.
The text says: 'preservation of truth in that is not on the face of necessity, but a matter that coincided from the equality of the predicate to the subject.'
3
Test with a non-coextensive pair
Take 'Every human is animal' and convert it universally to 'Every animal is human'. This converse is false, showing that the truth in the original does not necessarily carry over.
The failure of this conversion proves that the earlier success was accidental.
4
Formulate the necessary condition
The truth condition must be interpreted as: the truth of the original logically necessitates the truth of the converse. Accidental truth is insufficient.
The commentator says: 'with preservation of truth... on the face of necessity.'
5
Apply to real examples
When converting any proposition, always test whether the truth of the converse is forced by the original's truth, or merely coincidental.

فصل في العكس المستوي
(٦٨) «العَكْسُ : قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّهُ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِيَّة
الباجوري
-
وَبِقَوْلِهِ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الصِّدْقُ ، كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ».
- وَبِقَوْلِهِ: وَالكَيْفِيَّةِ : مَا إِذَا لَمْ تَبْقَ الكَيْفِيَّةُ ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ »: لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانِ» . - وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «وَالكَمْ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الكَمُ إِلَّا فِيمَا اسْتَتْنَاهُ المُصَنِّفُ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ» : «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانُ». فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَتَأَنَّى قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَامَ»؛ لِأَنَّ الفِعْلَ لَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُهُ مَوْضُوعاً .
أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصِحَ جَعْلُهُ بِذَاتِهِ مَوْضُوعاً، يُجْعَلْ فِي مَحَلِّهِ مَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْضُوعاً : بَعْضُ القَائِمِ أَوْ بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ، وَيُرْتَكَبُ هَذَا فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، فَيُقَالُ: بَعْضُ : القَائِمِ، أَوْ : بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ زَيْدٌ».
لَا يُقَالُ : لَمْ يَحْصُلْ قَلْبُ لِجُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، حَتَّى يُسَمَّى ذَلِكَ: «عَكْساً». لأَنَّا نَقُولُ : المَدَارُ فِي مِثْلِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى نِيَّةِ المُتَكَلِّمِ ؛ بِأَنْ يَنْوِيَ أَنَّ مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً وَبِالعَكْسِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ تَقْدِيمُ وَتَأخِيرٌ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ .
قَوْلُهُ: (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ) أَيْ : عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ»: «كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ»، فَإِنَّ بَقَاءَ الصِّدْقِ فِي ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ أَمْرُ اتَّفَقَ مِنْ مُسَاوَاةِ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوع؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَوْ عُكِسَ كُلِّيَّةٌ .
وَلَمْ يَقُلِ المُصَنِّفُ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الأَصْلِ كَذِبُ العَكْسِ.
الأنبابي
قوله : (فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ زَيْدٌ قَامَ) أي: مِن كلِّ تركيب كان المحكوم به فعلاً متقدماً، والمحكوم عليه فاعلاً مؤخّراً، ونظيره تركيب الفعل ونائبه .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. (68) 'Conversion: swapping the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth and quality.' Al-Bajuri: By his saying 'with preservation of truth' [it excludes] when truth is not preserved, as when you say in the converse of 'Every human is an animal': 'Every animal is human.' And by his saying 'and quality' [it excludes] when quality is not preserved, as when you say in the converse of 'Some human is an animal': 'Some animal is not human.' And by his saying 'and quantity' [it excludes] when quantity is not preserved except what the author excluded, as when you say in the converse of 'Some human is an animal': 'Animal is human.' So if it is said: The swapping of the two parts of the proposition is not possible in something like your saying 'Zaid stood' because the verb cannot be made a subject. It is answered: Even if it cannot be made a subject in itself, put in its place what can be a subject: some of the standing one or some of those who stood, and this is done in something like your saying 'Stood Zaid', so it is said: 'Some of the standing one, or some of those who stood, is Zaid.' It should not be said: The swapping of the two parts of the mentioned proposition has not occurred, so that this is called 'conversion'. Because we say: The criterion in such matters is the intention of the speaker; that he intends that what was subject becomes predicate and vice versa, even if there is no verbal advance or postponement. Let it be pondered. His saying: ('with preservation of truth') means: on the face of necessity, to exclude what is not on the face of necessity but on the face of coincidence, as in your saying in the converse of 'Every human is rational': 'Every rational is human', for preservation of truth in that is not on the face of necessity, but a matter that coincided from the equality of the predicate to the subject, as evidenced by its failure in your saying: 'Every human is animal' if converted universally. And the author did not say 'with preservation of truth and falsehood' because falsity of the original does not necessitate falsity of the converse. Al-Anbabi: His saying: ('in something like your saying Zaid stood') means: from every construction where the predicated is a verb that comes first, and the subject is an agent that comes later, and its parallel is the construction of the verb and its deputy.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the concept of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) in logic. It opens with the definition from the matn: 'Conversion is the swapping of the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth and quality.' The commentator (Bajuri) then explains each condition using counterexamples. First, preserving truth: if you convert 'Every human is an animal' to 'Every animal is human', the converse is false, so conversion fails. Second, preserving quality: converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Some animal is not human' changes affirmative to negative, so quality is lost. Third, preserving quantity (introduced implicitly): converting 'Some human is an animal' to 'Animal is human' changes particular to universal, losing quantity. The commentator notes that the author excluded certain cases where quantity might not be preserved. The text then raises an objection: how can you swap parts in a verb-initial sentence like 'Zaid stood' (which is analyzed as 'Stood Zaid' with the verb taking the place of predicate)? The answer explains that even if the verb cannot literally be a subject, you can substitute its nominal form (e.g., 'some of the standing one') to perform the conversion, and the essence of conversion relies on the speaker's intention rather than mere word order. Finally, a key refinement: the condition 'preservation of truth' must be understood as necessary truth (على وجه اللزوم), not accidental. For example, converting 'Every human is rational' to 'Every rational is human' yields a true converse because the terms are coextensive, but this is accidental truth, not necessary—evidence being that if you convert 'Every human is animal' (where the terms are not coextensive), the converse is false. Thus, true conversion requires that the original truth logically necessitates the truth of the converse. The commentator also clarifies why the author did not say 'preservation of truth and falsehood': because falsity of the original does not guarantee falsity of the converse. The marginal note from al-Anbabi explains the verb-initial construction further, saying it includes any construction where the predicate is a verb preceding the subject.

All examples are from Islamic logic: human-animal, human-rational, etc. These are standard examples in the tradition.

According to the passage, why is the conversion 'Every human is rational' to 'Every rational is human' not considered a valid conversion?
A) The converse is false.
B) The truth is accidental, not necessary.
C) The quality is not preserved.
D) The quantity is not preserved.

Key Takeaway
Valid conversion requires that the truth of the original necessarily implies the truth of the converse, not just accidental truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the conversion: 'Every intoxicant is haram' to 'Every haram thing is intoxicant'. Is the truth necessary or accidental? Explain using the criterion of necessary truth.
?
Provide an example of a conversion that preserves truth accidentally (like the human-rational example from the text) and one that fails, showing the necessity condition.

Immediate Inference

The Definition and Conditions of Simple Conversion
Author defines 'aks (conversion) with conditions and exception for universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion (al-ʿaks) and identify the three conditions for a valid conversion, including the exception for universal affirmative.

Core Idea
Conversion flips subject and predicate while preserving truth, quality, and quantity — except universal affirmative becomes particular.

In the Islamic logical tradition, converting a proposition is not merely swapping words; it must preserve fundamental properties. The matn of al-Sullam al-Munwaraq gives a concise definition, and al-Bājūrī unpacks each condition. Let's walk through the definition and its exception with examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of conversion
The matn states: 'Conversion is reversing the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity.' Note that the reversal is logical, not merely linguistic.
The definition excludes cases like 'Zaid stood' where the wording cannot be reversed literally.
2
Identify the three conditions
The conversion must preserve: (1) truth (sidq) — the converted proposition must be true necessarily, (2) quality (kayfiyya) — affirmation or negation must stay the same, (3) quantity (kamm) — universality or particularity must remain unchanged, except for universal affirmative.
Preserving truth means necessary truth, not coincidental.
3
Apply the conditions to Islamic examples
Example 1: 'Every prophet is truthful' (true universal affirmative). Its valid conversion is 'Some truthful person is a prophet' (true particular affirmative). Violation of truth: 'Every truthful person is a prophet' is false. Violation of quality: 'Some truthful person is not a prophet' changes affirmation to negation. Violation of quantity: 'Some prophet is truthful' converted to 'Every truthful person is a prophet' changes quantity.
Use these examples to see how each condition operates.
4
Recognize the exception for universal affirmative
The matn says: 'except the universal affirmative, for they convert it to the particular affirmative.' Thus, 'Every S is P' converts to 'Some P is S', not to 'Every P is S'.
This exception is why 'Every prophet is truthful' converts to 'Some truthful person is a prophet', not to 'Every truthful person is a prophet'.

كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ »: لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانِ» . - وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «وَالكَمْ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الكَمُ إِلَّا فِيمَا اسْتَتْنَاهُ المُصَنِّفُ; كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ» : «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانُ». فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَتَأَنَّى قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَامَ»; لِأَنَّ الفِعْلَ لَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُهُ مَوْضُوعاً . أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصِحَ جَعْلُهُ بِذَاتِهِ مَوْضُوعاً، يُجْعَلْ فِي مَحَلِّهِ مَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْضُوعاً : بَعْضُ القَائِمِ أَوْ بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ، وَيُرْتَكَبُ هَذَا فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، فَيُقَالُ: بَعْضُ : القَائِمِ، أَوْ : بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ زَيْدٌ». لَا يُقَالُ : لَمْ يَحْصُلْ قَلْبُ لِجُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، حَتَّى يُسَمَّى ذَلِكَ: «عَكْساً». لأَنَّا نَقُولُ : المَدَارُ فِي مِثْلِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى نِيَّةِ المُتَكَلِّمِ ; بِأَنْ يَنْوِيَ أَنَّ مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً وَبِالعَكْسِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ تَقْدِيمُ وَتَأخِيرٌ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ: (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ) أَيْ : عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ»: «كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ»، فَإِنَّ بَقَاءَ الصِّدْقِ فِي ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ أَمْرُ اتَّفَقَ مِنْ مُسَاوَاةِ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوع; بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَوْ عُكِسَ كُلِّيَّةٌ . وَلَمْ يَقُلِ المُصَنِّفُ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الأَصْلِ كَذِبُ العَكْسِ (١) ، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَكَ : كُلُّ حَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، كَاذِبٌ، مَعَ صِدْقٍ عَكْسِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ»، وَبِذَلِكَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ : وُجُودُهُ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَوْجُوداً فِي الأَصْلِ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى. قَوْلُهُ : (وَالكَيْفِيَّة) أي : الإِيجَابِ أَوِ السَّلْبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالكَمْ) أَي : الكُلِّيَّةِ أَوِ الجُزْنِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (إِلَّا المُوجَبَ الكُلِّيَّهُ اسْتِثْنَاءُ مِنَ الأَخِيرِ، وَحَذْفُ التَّاءِ مِنَ «المُوجَبَةَ» تَرْخِيماً ; لِلضَّرُورَةِ. فَإِنْ قِيلَ : التَّعْرِيفُ لَا يَدْخُلُهُ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ; لأَنَّهُ لِلمَاهِيَّةِ، لَا لِلأَفْرَادِ. أَجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ هَذَا لَيْسَ تَعْرِيفاً ، بَلْ هُوَ ضَابِط ، كَمَا يُشْعِرُ بِهِ كَلَامُ المُصَنِّفِ فِي شَرْحِهِ» (۲) ، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيم أَنَّهُ تَعْرِيفٌ، فَمَا ذَكَرَ مِنْ تَدْقِيقَاتِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، وَالمُصَنِّفُ لَمْ يَعْتَنِ بِذَلِكَ; تَقْرِيباً وَتَسْهِيلاً لِلْمُبْتَدِي ; أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ» (۳) .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. (68) Conversion: reversing the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity; except the universal affirmative, for they convert it to the particular affirmative. Al-Bajuri: And by his saying: 'with preservation of truth' [is excluded] that when truth does not remain, as if you say in the conversion of 'Every human is an animal': 'Every animal is a human'. And by his saying: 'and quality' [is excluded] that when quality does not remain, as if you say in the conversion of 'Some human is an animal': 'Some animal is not a human'. And by his saying: 'and quantity' [is excluded] that when quantity does not remain except in what the author excepted; as if you say in the conversion of 'Some human is an animal': 'Animal is a human'. If it is said: The reversal of the two parts of the proposition is not feasible in instances like your saying 'Zaid stood' because the verb cannot validly be made a subject. It is answered: Even though it cannot be made a subject in itself, one puts in its place what can be a subject: 'some standing one' or 'some who stood', and this is employed in instances like your saying 'Zaid stood', so it is said: 'Some standing one' or 'Some who stood is Zaid'. It is not said: The reversal of the two parts of the mentioned proposition has not occurred, such that it is called 'conversion'. For we say: The criterion in such matters is the intention of the speaker; that he intends that what was subject becomes predicate and vice versa, even if there is no advancement and postponement in the wording. So let it be pondered. His saying: 'with preservation of truth' means in the manner of necessity; to exclude that which is not in the manner of necessity but rather in the manner of coincidence, as in your saying in the conversion of 'Every human is rational': 'Every rational is a human'. For the preservation of truth in that is not in the manner of necessity, but rather a matter coincidental from the equality of the predicate to the subject; as evidenced by its failing in your saying 'Every human is an animal' if it were converted as a universal. And the author did not say: 'with preservation of truth and falsehood' because it is not necessary that from the falsehood of the original follows the falsehood of the conversion. For your saying 'Every animal is a human' is false, while its conversion is true, namely 'Some human is an animal'. And by that it is known that the intended meaning of 'preservation of truth' is its existence, even if it is not present in the original, as is not hidden. His saying: 'and quality' means affirmation or negation. And his saying: 'and quantity' means universality or particularity. His saying: 'except the universal affirmative' is an exception from the latter, and the dropping of the tā' from 'al-mūjabah' is a truncation for necessity. If it is said: The definition does not admit exception, because it is for the essence, not for the individuals. It is answered: This is not a definition, but rather a rule, as is suggested by the author's words in his commentary. And on the assumption that it is a definition, what he mentioned is from the refinements of the logicians, and the author did not pay attention to that, simplifying and facilitating for the beginner; al-Malawī stated this in his major commentary.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawī) as taught by the author of al-Sullam al-Munwaraq, with commentary by al-Bajuri explaining each condition. The matn states that conversion is reversing the subject and predicate of a proposition while preserving three things: truth (sidq), quality (kayfiyya, i.e., affirmation or negation), and quantity (kamm, i.e., universality or particularity). However, there is one exception: the universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') does not convert to a universal affirmative but rather to a particular affirmative ('Some animal is a human'). Al-Bajuri then illustrates what happens if each condition is violated: if truth is not preserved, the conversion is invalid (e.g., 'Every animal is a human' is false); if quality changes, it is invalid (e.g., 'Some animal is not a human' changes from affirmative to negative); if quantity changes without necessity, it is invalid (e.g., 'Animal is a human' changes from particular to universal without warrant). A objection arises about propositions with verbal predicates like 'Zaid stood' — the verb cannot become a subject. The answer is that conversion can still be performed by using a participle or relative clause (e.g., 'Some standing one is Zaid'), and the key is the speaker's intention to reverse subject and predicate logically, even if the wording does not literally reverse. Next, al-Bajuri clarifies that the 'preservation of truth' in the definition means necessary preservation, not coincidental. For example, 'Every human is rational' converts to 'Every rational is a human' only because rational and human are coextensive; this is not a necessary conversion for a universal affirmative. The author deliberately omitted falsehood from the condition because a false original may have a true conversion (e.g., 'Every animal is a human' is false, but its conversion 'Some human is an animal' is true). Thus, only truth matters. Finally, the passage addresses a subtle point: definitions should not have exceptions because they define essences, not individuals. The response is that this is not a strict definition (hadd) but a practical rule (dābit) for beginners, as al-Malawī explained. Thus, the exception is permissible.

Key Takeaway

Necessity of Truth and Handling Verbal Predicates in Conversion
Author defines 'aks (conversion) with conditions and exception for universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessary preservation of truth from coincidental preservation, and apply the nominalization technique to convert propositions with verbal predicates.

Core Idea
Conversion requires necessary truth, not coincidental; and verbs are replaced by participles to allow reversal.

Al-Bajuri's commentary deepens our understanding of the truth condition and addresses a practical challenge: propositions with verbs. The truth preserved in conversion must be necessary, not coincidental. And when the predicate is a verb, we nominalize it to perform the reversal. Let's examine these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Understand necessary vs coincidental truth
The matn says 'preserving truth (sidq)'. Al-Bajuri clarifies: this means the conversion must be true necessarily, not just coincidentally. For example, 'Every human is rational' converts to 'Every rational is human' only because human and rational are coextensive; this coincidental truth does not hold for every universal affirmative.
Use Islamic example: 'Every prophet is truthful' does not convert to 'Every truthful person is a prophet' necessarily; only 'Some truthful person is a prophet' is necessary.
2
See the consequence of coincidental truth
Al-Bajuri says if we did not require necessity, the conversion would be valid for logically equivalent terms but invalid for others. The condition excludes accidental truth.
3
Address the verbal predicate objection
Propositions like 'Zaid stood' (Zaidun qāma) have a verb as predicate, which cannot become subject directly. The solution: replace the verb with a participle or relative clause. For 'Zaid stood', we say 'Some standing one is Zaid' or 'Some who stood is Zaid'.
The speaker's intention matters: the logical reversal is in meaning, not wording.
4
Practice with Islamic verbal propositions
Convert 'The prayer is completed' into 'Some completed thing is the prayer' using a nominal form.
This technique allows conversion to apply to all propositions.

فصل في العكس المستوي (٦٨) «العَكْسُ : قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّهُ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَيْفِيَّة (٦٩) وَالكَم؛ إِلَّا المُوجَبَ الكُلِّيَّة فَعَوَضُوهَا المُوجَبَ الجُزْئِيَّة
الباجوري: وَبِقَوْلِهِ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الصِّدْقُ ، كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ». - وَبِقَوْلِهِ: وَالكَيْفِيَّةِ : مَا إِذَا لَمْ تَبْقَ الكَيْفِيَّةُ ; كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ »: لَيْسَ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانِ» . - وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «وَالكَمْ» : مَا إِذَا لَمْ يَبْقَ الكَمُ إِلَّا فِيمَا اسْتَتْنَاهُ المُصَنِّفُ; كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ» : «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانُ». فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَتَأَنَّى قَلْبُ جُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَامَ»; لِأَنَّ الفِعْلَ لَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُهُ مَوْضُوعاً . أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصِحَ جَعْلُهُ بِذَاتِهِ مَوْضُوعاً، يُجْعَلْ فِي مَحَلِّهِ مَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْضُوعاً : بَعْضُ القَائِمِ أَوْ بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ، وَيُرْتَكَبُ هَذَا فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : «قَامَ زَيْدٌ»، فَيُقَالُ: بَعْضُ : القَائِمِ، أَوْ : بَعْضُ مَنْ قَامَ زَيْدٌ». لَا يُقَالُ : لَمْ يَحْصُلْ قَلْبُ لِجُزْأَيِ القَضِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، حَتَّى يُسَمَّى ذَلِكَ: «عَكْساً». لأَنَّا نَقُولُ : المَدَارُ فِي مِثْلِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى نِيَّةِ المُتَكَلِّمِ ; بِأَنْ يَنْوِيَ أَنَّ مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً يَصِيرُ مَحْمُولاً وَبِالعَكْسِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ تَقْدِيمُ وَتَأخِيرٌ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ: (مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ) أَيْ : عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ»: «كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ»، فَإِنَّ بَقَاءَ الصِّدْقِ فِي ذَلِكَ لَيْسَ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، بَلْ أَمْرُ اتَّفَقَ مِنْ مُسَاوَاةِ المَحْمُولِ لِلمَوْضُوع; بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَوْ عُكِسَ كُلِّيَّةٌ . وَلَمْ يَقُلِ المُصَنِّفُ : مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الأَصْلِ كَذِبُ العَكْسِ (١) ، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَكَ : كُلُّ حَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، كَاذِبٌ، مَعَ صِدْقٍ عَكْسِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ»، وَبِذَلِكَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ : وُجُودُهُ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَوْجُوداً فِي الأَصْلِ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى. قَوْلُهُ : (وَالكَيْفِيَّة) أي : الإِيجَابِ أَوِ السَّلْبِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالكَمْ) أَي : الكُلِّيَّةِ أَوِ الجُزْنِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (إِلَّا المُوجَبَ الكُلِّيَّهُ اسْتِثْنَاءُ مِنَ الأَخِيرِ، وَحَذْفُ التَّاءِ مِنَ «المُوجَبَةَ» تَرْخِيماً ; لِلضَّرُورَةِ. فَإِنْ قِيلَ : التَّعْرِيفُ لَا يَدْخُلُهُ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ; لأَنَّهُ لِلمَاهِيَّةِ، لَا لِلأَفْرَادِ. أَجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ هَذَا لَيْسَ تَعْرِيفاً ، بَلْ هُوَ ضَابِط ، كَمَا يُشْعِرُ بِهِ كَلَامُ المُصَنِّفِ فِي شَرْحِهِ» (۲) ، وَعَلَى تَسْلِيم أَنَّهُ تَعْرِيفٌ، فَمَا ذَكَرَ مِنْ تَدْقِيقَاتِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، وَالمُصَنِّفُ لَمْ يَعْتَنِ بِذَلِكَ; تَقْرِيباً وَتَسْهِيلاً لِلْمُبْتَدِي ; أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ» (۳) .
Chapter on Simple Conversion. (68) Conversion: reversing the two parts of the proposition while preserving truth, quality, and quantity; except the universal affirmative, for they convert it to the particular affirmative. Al-Bajuri: And by his saying: 'with preservation of truth' [is excluded] that when truth does not remain, as if you say in the conversion of 'Every human is an animal': 'Every animal is a human'. And by his saying: 'and quality' [is excluded] that when quality does not remain, as if you say in the conversion of 'Some human is an animal': 'Some animal is not a human'. And by his saying: 'and quantity' [is excluded] that when quantity does not remain except in what the author excepted; as if you say in the conversion of 'Some human is an animal': 'Animal is a human'. If it is said: The reversal of the two parts of the proposition is not feasible in instances like your saying 'Zaid stood' because the verb cannot validly be made a subject. It is answered: Even though it cannot be made a subject in itself, one puts in its place what can be a subject: 'some standing one' or 'some who stood', and this is employed in instances like your saying 'Zaid stood', so it is said: 'Some standing one' or 'Some who stood is Zaid'. It is not said: The reversal of the two parts of the mentioned proposition has not occurred, such that it is called 'conversion'. For we say: The criterion in such matters is the intention of the speaker; that he intends that what was subject becomes predicate and vice versa, even if there is no advancement and postponement in the wording. So let it be pondered. His saying: 'with preservation of truth' means in the manner of necessity; to exclude that which is not in the manner of necessity but rather in the manner of coincidence, as in your saying in the conversion of 'Every human is rational': 'Every rational is a human'. For the preservation of truth in that is not in the manner of necessity, but rather a matter coincidental from the equality of the predicate to the subject; as evidenced by its failing in your saying 'Every human is an animal' if it were converted as a universal. And the author did not say: 'with preservation of truth and falsehood' because it is not necessary that from the falsehood of the original follows the falsehood of the conversion. For your saying 'Every animal is a human' is false, while its conversion is true, namely 'Some human is an animal'. And by that it is known that the intended meaning of 'preservation of truth' is its existence, even if it is not present in the original, as is not hidden. His saying: 'and quality' means affirmation or negation. And his saying: 'and quantity' means universality or particularity. His saying: 'except the universal affirmative' is an exception from the latter, and the dropping of the tā' from 'al-mūjabah' is a truncation for necessity. If it is said: The definition does not admit exception, because it is for the essence, not for the individuals. It is answered: This is not a definition, but rather a rule, as is suggested by the author's words in his commentary. And on the assumption that it is a definition, what he mentioned is from the refinements of the logicians, and the author did not pay attention to that, simplifying and facilitating for the beginner; al-Malawī stated this in his major commentary.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawī) as taught by the author of al-Sullam al-Munwaraq, with commentary by al-Bajuri explaining each condition. The matn states that conversion is reversing the subject and predicate of a proposition while preserving three things: truth (sidq), quality (kayfiyya, i.e., affirmation or negation), and quantity (kamm, i.e., universality or particularity). However, there is one exception: the universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') does not convert to a universal affirmative but rather to a particular affirmative ('Some animal is a human'). Al-Bajuri then illustrates what happens if each condition is violated: if truth is not preserved, the conversion is invalid (e.g., 'Every animal is a human' is false); if quality changes, it is invalid (e.g., 'Some animal is not a human' changes from affirmative to negative); if quantity changes without necessity, it is invalid (e.g., 'Animal is a human' changes from particular to universal without warrant). The answer is that conversion can still be performed by using a participle or relative clause (e.g., 'Some standing one is Zaid'), and the key is the speaker's intention to reverse subject and predicate logically, even if the wording does not literally reverse. Thus, only truth matters. Finally, the passage addresses a subtle point: definitions should not have exceptions because they define essences, not individuals. The response is that this is not a strict definition (hadd) but a practical rule (dābit) for beginners, as al-Malawī explained. Thus, the exception is permissible.

Immediate Inference
beginner
5 min
Conversion of Affirmative Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert universal affirmative, particular affirmative, and affirmative singular propositions into their correct converse forms.

Core Idea
Universal affirmatives become particular; particular affirmatives stay the same; singulars depend on the predicate.

The rules of conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) are essential for immediate inference in categorical logic. This lesson focuses on the conversion of affirmative categorical propositions, including universal, particular, and singular forms. Understanding these rules allows you to correctly derive the converse of an affirmative proposition, a crucial skill for syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B'), particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some A is B'), or affirmative singular (e.g., 'Zayd is B').
Also note if the singular has a universal or particular predicate.
2
Apply the conversion rule
For universal affirmative: convert to particular affirmative. For particular affirmative: convert to the same (particular affirmative). For affirmative singular: if predicate is universal, convert to particular affirmative; if predicate is particular, convert unchanged (identical).
The quality remains affirmative; the quantity may change.
3
Swap subject and predicate
Place the original predicate as the new subject, and the original subject as the new predicate. Adjust quantifier according to the rule.
For universals, the quantifier becomes 'some'; for particulars, it remains 'some'; for singulars, see step 2.
4
Verify validity
Check that the converse is a valid immediate inference. For example, 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is human', which is true if the original is true.
Some conversions are not reversible (e.g., universal affirmative becomes particular, not the reverse).

فصل في العكس المستوي (٦٩) وَالكَم؛ إِلَّا المُوجَبَ الكُلِّيَّة فَعَوَضُوهَا المُوجَبَ الجُزْئِيَّة. تنبيه : عُلِمَ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنَّ المُوجَبَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئيَّةٌ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ. وَمِثْلُهَا الشَّخْصِيَّةُ المُوجَبَةُ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيًّا؛ فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ زَيْدٌ حَيَوَانُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ زَيْدٌ، فَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئيًّا انْعَكَسَتْ كَنَفْسِهَا؛ فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ هَذَا زَيْدٌ: زَيْدٌ هَذَا. وَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَمِثْلُهَا الشَّخْصِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيًّا؛ فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِحَجَرٍ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِزَيْدٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئيًّا انْعَكَسَتْ كَنَفْسِهَا، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِعَمْرٍو: لَيْسَ عَمْرُو بِزَيْدٍ. وَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنَّ المُوجَبَةَ الجُزْئيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئيَّةٌ. وَالأَرْبَعُ السَّوَالِبُ يَنْعَكِسُ مِنْهَا اثْنَتَانِ وَهُمَا: السَّالِبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ الشَّخْصِيَّةُ الَّتِي مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئِيٌّ، فَيَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيًّا انْعَكَسَتْ سَالِبَةً كُلِّيَّةً، وَلَا يَنْعَكِسُ مِنْهَا اثْنَتَانِ وَهُمَا: الجُزْئِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ، وَالمُهْمَلَةُ السَّالِبَةُ.
Chapter on Conversion (al-'Aks al-Mustawi). (69) And the quantity; except the affirmative universal, so its conversion is the affirmative particular. A note: It is understood from the author's words that the affirmative universal converts to an affirmative particular. So you say in the conversion of 'Every human is an animal': 'Some animal is human'. And similarly the affirmative singular if its predicate is universal; so you say in the conversion of 'Zayd is an animal': 'Some animal is Zayd'. If its predicate is particular, it converts as itself; so you say in the conversion of 'This is Zayd': 'Zayd is this'. And it is also understood from it that the universal negative converts to a universal negative. So you say in the conversion of 'No human is a stone': 'No stone is a human'. And similarly the singular negative if its predicate is universal; so you say in the conversion of 'Zayd is not a stone': 'No stone is Zayd'. If its predicate is particular, it converts as itself; so you say in the conversion of 'Zayd is not Amr': 'Amr is not Zayd'. And it is also understood from it that the affirmative particular converts to an affirmative particular. And of the four negatives, two convert: the universal negative and the singular negative whose predicate is particular, so they convert as themselves; if its predicate is universal, it converts to a universal negative. And two do not convert: the particular negative and the indefinite negative.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage provides the complete rules for conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) for all categorical propositions. The author begins with the rule for the affirmative universal: it converts to an affirmative particular. An example is given: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is human'. The rule is then extended to affirmative singular propositions: if the predicate is universal (like 'animal'), it converts to an affirmative particular; but if the predicate is particular (like 'Zayd'), it converts unchanged — 'This is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is this'. Next, the universal negative converts to a universal negative, as shown: 'No human is a stone' becomes 'No stone is human'. Similarly, singular negatives with universal predicates convert to universal negatives, while those with particular predicates convert unchanged. The affirmative particular also converts to an affirmative particular. Finally, the author states that of the four negative types, only two convert: the universal negative and the singular negative with a particular predicate. The other two — the particular negative and the indefinite negative — do not convert. This is a foundational lesson for understanding immediate inferences in categorical logic, essential for syllogistic reasoning.

Conversion of Negative Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert universal negative and singular negative propositions correctly, and identify which negatives (particular and indefinite) do not convert.

Core Idea
Universal negatives convert to universal negatives; particular negatives never convert.

Negative propositions in categorical logic have more complex conversion rules. While universal negatives and certain singular negatives have valid converses, particular negatives and indefinite negatives do not. This lesson clarifies these rules to prevent invalid inferences in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative proposition type
Determine whether it is universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B'), particular negative (e.g., 'Some A is not B'), singular negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not B'), or indefinite negative (e.g., 'A is not B' without quantifier).
Indefinite negatives are treated as particular or universal by convention; here they do not convert.
2
Apply the conversion rule for universal negatives
Universal negative converts to universal negative with subject and predicate swapped. Example: 'No human is stone' becomes 'No stone is human'.
Quantity and quality are preserved.
3
Apply the conversion rule for singular negatives
If the predicate is universal, convert to universal negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not a stone' -> 'No stone is Zayd'). If the predicate is particular, convert identically (e.g., 'Zayd is not Amr' -> 'Amr is not Zayd').
This mirrors the singular affirmative case.
4
Recognize non-convertible negatives
Particular negatives and indefinite negatives have no valid converse. For example, 'Some animal is not human' cannot be converted to a true proposition. Memorize these exceptions.
The source explicitly states that two of the four negatives do not convert.

Conversion Rules for Universal and Singular Propositions
Al-Bajuri expands the matn's conversion rule to all proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly convert universal affirmative, universal negative, singular affirmative, singular negative, and particular affirmative propositions while preserving truth.

Core Idea
Conversion flips subject and predicate but changes quantity for universal affirmatives — all examples are from Islamic logic.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on the matn of al-Bannani explains how to convert categorical propositions of different quantities and qualities. Building on the matn's rule that universal affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives, al-Bajuri expands the rule to singular propositions and clarifies the conversion of universal negatives and particular affirmatives. Understanding these rules is essential for valid immediate inferences in Islamic logical analysis, especially when preparing premises for syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine the quantity (universal/particular/singular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the given proposition. For example, 'Every human is animal' is universal affirmative; 'No human is stone' is universal negative; 'Zayd is not Amr' is singular negative.
Singular propositions refer to a specific individual. In Arabic logic, they are treated distinctly from universal and particular.
2
Apply the conversion rule for universal affirmatives
The matn states: except the universal affirmative, so you replace it with the particular affirmative. So 'Every human is animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'.
3
Apply conversion rules for universal negatives and singulars
Universal negatives convert to universal negatives: 'No human is stone' becomes 'No stone is human'. Singular negatives: if the predicate is universal (like 'stone' is universal), it converts to a universal negative: 'Zayd is not a stone' becomes 'No stone is Zayd'. If the predicate is particular (like 'Amr' is a singular term), it converts to itself: 'Zayd is not Amr' becomes 'Amr is not Zayd'.
Singular affirmatives: if predicate is particular, it converts to itself: 'This is Zayd' becomes 'Zayd is this'.
4
Apply conversion rule for particular affirmatives
Particular affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives: 'Some animal is human' becomes 'Some human is animal'. The passage also implies that particular negatives? Not covered here.
5
Verify truth preservation
Ensure that the converted proposition is necessarily true given the original, not accidentally. For example, 'Every human is animal' is true, but its direct conversion to 'Every animal is human' is false; hence the rule changes quantity.
This is why conversion rules are crucial for valid inference.
