- وَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ «لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ»: «لَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، وَمِثْلُهَا الشَّخْصِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا كُلِّيًّا؛ فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِحَجَرٍ»: «لَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِزَيْدٍ»، فَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولُهَا جُزْئِيًّا انْعَكَسَتْ كَنَفْسِهَا، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ «لَيْسَ زَيْدٌ بِعَمْرٍو»: «لَيْسَ عَمْرُو بِزَيْدِ». - وَعُلِمَ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً : أَنَّ المُوجَبَةَ الجُزْئِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ «بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ
And the quantity: except the universal affirmative, so you replace it with the particular affirmative. If its predicate is particular, it converts as itself; so you say in the conversion of 'This is Zayd': 'Zayd is this'. And it is also known from it that the universal negative converts as universal negative; so you say in the conversion of 'Nothing from human is stone': 'Nothing from stone is human'. And similarly the singular negative if its predicate is universal; so you say in the conversion of 'Zayd is not a stone': 'Nothing from stone is Zayd'. And if its predicate is particular, it converts as itself; so you say in the conversion of 'Zayd is not Amr': 'Amr is not Zayd'. And it is also known from it that the particular affirmative converts as particular affirmative; so you say in the conversion of 'Some animal
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bajuri on a matn line about conversion (al-‘aks). The matn states that the universal affirmative is an exception in quantity: it converts to a particular affirmative. The commentary expands this rule to other proposition types. First, it clarifies that if a singular affirmative proposition has a particular predicate, it converts to itself (i.e., the same proposition). Then it explains that the universal negative converts to a universal negative, exemplified by 'Nothing of human is stone' becoming 'Nothing of stone is human'. Similarly, a singular negative with a universal predicate converts to a universal negative; but if its predicate is particular, it converts to itself. Finally, the particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, as in 'Some animal is human' becoming 'Some human is animal' (though the example is cut off). The commentary thus provides the conversion rules for these types, emphasizing that the truth must be preserved necessarily, not merely accidentally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct conversion of 'La shay’a min al-insani bi-hajar' (No human is a stone)?
A) 'Ba’d al-hajari laysa bi-insan' (Some stone is not human).
B) 'La shay’a min al-hajari bi-insan' (No stone is human).
C) 'Ba’d al-insani laysa bi-hajar' (Some human is not stone).
D) 'Kullu insanin laysa bi-hajar' (Every human is not stone).

Key Takeaway
Conversion of universal affirmatives changes quantity to particular; universal negatives and singular negatives with universal predicates remain universal negative; singular negatives with particular predicates and particular affirmatives convert as themselves.

The Condition for Direct Conversion: Excluding Propositions with Two Deficiencies
Commentary explaining the exception to direct conversion for propositions with two deficiencies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categorical propositions do not admit direct conversion and explain why truth preservation is not necessary.

Core Idea
Direct conversion fails for particular negatives because truth is not preserved necessarily.

In the theory of immediate inference, direct conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi) is a powerful tool, but it has a critical exception: propositions that combine the two deficiencies of particularity and negation. This lesson unpacks the matn line and commentary to show why the particular negative (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is not wine') cannot be converted directly, and why accidental truth does not matter.

Guided Path
1
Locate the exception clause
Find the phrase 'لغير ما وجد به اجتماع الخستين' in the matn. This is the condition: conversion is necessary for all except those with both deficiencies.
The two deficiencies are particularity (جزئية) and negation (سلب).
2
Identify the excluded proposition
The commentator specifies that the excluded proposition is the particular negative (السالبة الجزئية). For example, 'Some intoxicant is not wine' is a particular negative.
Also includes the universal negative (مهملة سالبة) as mentioned.
3
Test direct conversion on a particular negative
Take 'Some intoxicant is not wine'. Try direct conversion: swap subject and predicate to get 'Some wine is not an intoxicant'. This is false because all wine is intoxicant, so truth is not preserved.
The conversion fails necessarily, not just accidentally.
4
Understand accidental truth
The commentator notes that sometimes conversion may coincidentally hold, as in 'Some human is not a stone' converting to 'Some stone is not a human'. But this is due to specific material, not a logical necessity.
The test is always the general case: when the subject is more general than the predicate, the conversion fails.
5
Formulate the rule
Direct conversion is valid only if the original proposition does NOT combine particularity and negation. For all others (affirmatives, universals, etc.), conversion preserves truth necessarily.
This rule ensures that conversion is a reliable inference tool.

فصل في العكس المستوي
(۷۰) وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ فَاقْتَصِدْ
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ ... إلخ) «أَلْ» فِيهِ لِلعَهْدِ، وَالمَعْهُودُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ العَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي . وَخَرَجَ بِهِ : عَكْسُ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ ، وَالمُخَالِفِ ; فَإِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لِكُلّ قَضِيَّةٍ حَتَّى لِمَا وُجِدَ فِيهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ - وَهِيَ : السَّالِبَةُ الجُزْئِيَّةُ - : 
فَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ : أَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ : بَعْضُ مَا لَا إِنْسَانٍ لَيْسَ لَا حَيَوَان.
وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي : أَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : بَعْضُ مَا لَا إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ.
وَمِثْلُ مَا وُجِدَ فِيهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ : المُهْمَلَةُ السَّالِبَةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ ... إلخ) أَي : الَّذِي هُوَ السَّالِبَةُ الجُزْأَيَّةُ.
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا عَكْسُ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَبْقَى فِيهِ الصِّدْقُ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَدْ يَبْقَى اتِّفَاقاً : فِي بَعْضِ المَوَادِّ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي بَعْضِ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ : بَعْضُ الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ بَقِيَ الصِّدْقُ اتِّفَاقاً لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ; بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِهِ فِي مَادَّةٍ يَكُونُ المَوْضُوعُ فِيهَا أَعمَّ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ، فَيَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَخَصِّ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَصِّ، فَإِنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ وَلَا يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى .
قَوْلُهُ : (بِهِ) أَيْ : فِيهِ، وَذَكَّرَ هُنَا الضَّمِيرَ; نَظَراً لِلَفْظِ مَا ، وَأَنتَهُ فِيمَا بَعْدُ; نَظَراً لِمَعْنَاهَا . قَوْلُهُ : (اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ) أَي : الجُزْئِيَّةِ وَالسَّلْبِ، فَالجُزْئِيَّةُ خِسَةٌ بِالنَّظَرِ لِلكُلِّيَّة; لِأَنَّهَا أَشْرَفُ مِنْهَا ، وَالسَّلْبُ خِسَّةٌ بِالنَّظَرِ لِلإِيجَابِ; لِأَنَّهُ أَشْرَفُ مِنْهُ .
Chapter on Direct Conversion. (70) And conversion is necessary except for that in which there is a combination of the two deficiencies, so limit yourself. His words: (And conversion is necessary... etc.) The 'al' in it is for the definite article referring to something previously mentioned, and the thing referred to is direct conversion. And excluded by it is conversion of contradictory agreement and disagreement; for it is necessary for every proposition, even for that in which there is a combination of the two deficiencies - and it is the particular negative. So an example of the first: you say in conversion of 'Some animal is not a human': 'Some of what is not a human is not an animal'. And an example of the second: you say in conversion of the mentioned example: 'Some of what is not a human is an animal'. And similar to what has the combination of the two deficiencies is the universal negative. And his words: (for other than that in which there is found... etc.) meaning that which is the particular negative. And the reason it does not have a conversion is because truth does not remain in it in a necessary manner, even if it may remain accidentally in some materials; as in your saying in conversion of 'Some human is not a stone': 'Some stone is not a human', for truth remains accidentally due to the specificity of the material; the evidence is its failure in a material where the subject is more general than the predicate, so the negation of the more specific from some individuals of the more general is true, but the negation of the more general from some individuals of the more specific is not true. For example, it is true to say: 'Some animal is not a human' but not true to say: 'Some human is not an animal', as is not hidden. His words: (in it) i.e., in it, and he made the pronoun masculine here, looking at the word 'ma', and you will find it later looking at its meaning. His words: (combination of the two deficiencies) i.e., particularity and negation; for particularity is a deficiency relative to universality, because it is more noble, and negation is a deficiency relative to affirmation, because it is more noble.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line that states a condition for conversion: conversion (al-‘aks mustawi) is necessary for all propositions except those that combine two deficiencies (al-khissatayn). The commentator explains that the 'al' in the line refers to the well-known type, direct conversion. He distinguishes this from conversion by contradiction (al-naqid) which applies to every proposition. The combination of deficiencies is found in the particular negative (al-sāliba al-juz’iyya). Then he gives examples: conversion of 'Some animal is not a human' by contradictory agreement yields 'Some non-human is not an animal'; by contradictory disagreement yields 'Some non-human is an animal'. He also says the universal negative (al-muhmala al-sāliba) is like it. Then he explains why particular negative has no conversion: because truth is not preserved necessarily; it may coincidentally remain, as in the example of 'Some human is not a stone' converting to 'Some stone is not a human', but this is not always true. For instance, when the subject is more general than the predicate (like 'animal' and 'human'), the negation of the specific from some general is true, but the negation of the general from some specific is false. The commentary ends by explaining the pronoun reference and defining the two deficiencies: particularity (juz’iyya) and negation (salab), each being a 'deficiency' (khissa) relative to their opposites.

Key Terms
الخستين
السالبة الجزئية
العكس المستوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the particular negative (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is not wine') have no direct conversion?
A) Because it combines particularity and negation, which are two deficiencies.
B) Because it is a weak proposition.
C) Because its conversion would be a false universal.
D) Because the subject is always more general.

Key Takeaway
Direct conversion is invalid for propositions with both particularity and negation because truth is not preserved necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the fiqh proposition 'Some intoxicants are not wine' (e.g., nabidh). Perform direct conversion and evaluate whether the result is necessarily true. Explain why or why not.
?
Construct a particular negative proposition from usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'Some prohibited things are not intoxicants') and test whether it can be directly converted. Prove your answer using the rule from this lesson.

Distinguishing Direct Conversion from Conversion by Contradiction
Commentary distinguishing direct conversion from conversion by contradiction with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish direct conversion from conversion by contradiction and apply both types using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
Conversion by contradiction works for every proposition, including those that fail direct conversion.

The commentator distinguishes two types of conversion: direct (mustawi) and by contradiction (naqid). While direct conversion has exceptions, conversion by contradiction applies universally. This lesson examines the two subtypes of conversion by contradiction and shows how they differ from direct conversion using concrete examples from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of conversion
The matn mentions 'al-aks' generally, but the commentator clarifies it is direct conversion. He then excludes 'aks al-naqid al-muwāfiq and al-mukhālif (conversion by contradictory agreement and disagreement).
Both subtypes are types of 'aks al-naqid (conversion by contradiction).
2
Identify the example of conversion by agreement
For 'Some animal is not a human', conversion by contradictory agreement yields 'Some non-human is not non-animal' (i.e., some non-human is not an animal). This is true because there exist non-humans that are not animals (e.g., stones).
Replace with Islamic example: 'Some intoxicant is not wine' → 'Some non-wine is not non-intoxicant'.
3
Identify the example of conversion by disagreement
For the same proposition, conversion by contradictory disagreement yields 'Some non-human is an animal'. This is also true (e.g., horses are non-human animals).
Islamic example: 'Some intoxicant is not wine' → 'Some non-wine is an intoxicant' (e.g., beer).
4
Test direct conversion on the same proposition
Direct conversion of 'Some animal is not a human' would be 'Some human is not an animal', which is false. Thus, conversion by contradiction works where direct conversion fails.
The commentator notes that conversion by contradiction is necessary for every proposition, even the particular negative.
5
Generalize the applicability
Direct conversion is only valid for propositions without both deficiencies. Conversion by contradiction is always valid, regardless of quality or quantity.
This makes conversion by contradiction a more flexible tool in logical inference.

Simple Conversion and Its Exception for Particular Negatives
Defines simple conversion and its exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categorical propositions have valid simple conversion and explain why the particular negative is an exception.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth only for propositions lacking both particularity and negation.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam clarifies the rule of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi). The central point is that while universal affirmatives, universal negatives, and particular affirmatives all have valid conversions, the particular negative does not. The reason lies in the combination of two 'deficiencies': particularity and negativity. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly manipulating categorical propositions in Islamic logic, especially when constructing proofs in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
List the four categorical proposition types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Note which types the passage says have conversion and which do not.
The passage also treats indefinite propositions like particular ones.
2
Test conversion on each type
For each proposition type, swap the subject and predicate. For example, convert 'Every S is P' to 'Some P is S'. Check whether truth is preserved.
Universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative; universal negative stays universal negative; particular affirmative stays particular affirmative; particular negative fails.
3
Analyze the failure of particular negative
The passage states that the particular negative has 'the two deficiencies' (particularity and negativity). Explain why: particularity makes the subject range incomplete, and negativity adds a scope issue such that truth is not guaranteed.
Example: 'Some animal is not a man' does not entail 'Some man is not an animal'.
4
Distinguish necessary vs. accidental truth
The commentator notes that sometimes conversion of a particular negative accidentally holds (e.g., 'Some man is not a stone' converts to 'Some stone is not a man'), but this is not logically necessary. Conversion must be valid in all materials.
The passage uses the term 'bi-wajh al-luzum' (by way of necessity).
5
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Take a universal affirmative from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Convert it to 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant.' Then take a particular negative: 'Some drink is not intoxicating.' Confirm that its conversion 'Some intoxicating thing is not a drink' is not valid.
This demonstrates the practical limitation of conversion in legal reasoning.

Source Text

فصل في العكس المستوي
(٧٠) وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِشَتَيْنِ فَاقْتَصِدْ
الباجوري
إِنْسَانُ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ ، وَمِثْلُهَا المُهْمَلَةُ المُوجَبَةُ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ الإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانُ» : «بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ»، وَيَصِحُ أَيْضاً عَكْسُهَا كَنَفْسِهَا ، فَتَقُولُ فِي عَكْسِ المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : «الحَيَوَانُ إِنْسَانُ». - وَأَمَّا الجُزْئِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا عَكْسَ لَهَا ، فَلَا تَقُولُ فِي بَعْضِ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ»: «بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَمِثْلُهَا المُهْمَلَةُ الصَّالِبَةُ، فَلَا تَقُولُ فِي الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ» : «بَعْضُ
الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ»، وَلَا الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ»؛ كَمَا نَبَّهَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ) المُصَنِّفُ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ ... إلخ) «أَلْ» : فِيهِ لِلعَهْدِ، وَالمَعْهُودُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ العَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي . وَخَرَجَ بِهِ : عَكْسُ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ ، وَالمُخَالِفِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لِكُلٍّ قَضِيَّةٍ حَتَّى لِمَا وُجِدَ فِيهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ - وَهِيَ : الصَّالِيَةُ الجُزْنِيَّةُ - :
- فَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ : أَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ : بَعْضُ مَا لَا إِنْسَانٍ لَيْسَ
لَا حَيَوَان».
- وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي : أَنْ تَقُولَ فِي عَكْسِ المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : بَعْضُ مَا لَا إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ».
وَمِثْلُ مَا وُجِدَ فِيهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَتَيْنِ : المُهْمَلَةُ السَّالِبَةُ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ ... إلخ) أَي : الَّذِي هُوَ السَّالِبَةُ الجُزْيَّةُ.
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا عَكْسُ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَبْقَى فِيهِ الصِّدْقُ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَدْ يَبْقَى اتِّفَاقاً
فِي بَعْضِ المَوَادِّ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي بَعْضِ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ : بَعْضُ الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ بَقِيَ الصِّدْقُ اتِّفَاقاً لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِهِ فِي مَادَّةٍ يَكُونُ المَوْضُوعُ فِيهَا أَعمَّ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ، فَيَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَخَصٌ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَص ، فَإِنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ وَلَا يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى .
قَوْلُهُ : (بِهِ) أَيْ : فِيهِ، وَذَكَّرَ هُنَا الضَّمِيرَ؛ نَظَراً لِلَفْظِ مَا ، وَأَنْتَهُ فِيمَا بَعْدُ؛ نَظَراً لِمَعْنَاهَا . قَوْلُهُ : (اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ) أَي : الجُزْنِيَّةِ وَالسَّلْبِ، فَالجُزْئِيَّةُ خِسَةٌ بِالنَّظَرِ لِلكُلِّيَّة؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَشْرَفُ مِنْهَا ، وَالسَّلْبُ خِسَّةٌ بِالنَّظَرِ لِلإِيجَابِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ أَشْرَفُ مِنْهُ .
حاشية الام الناجون على السلم المرونة
(٧٠) وَالعَكْسُ لَازِمٌ لِغَيْرِ مَا وُجِدْ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ فَاقْتَصِدْ
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (فَاقْتَصِدْ) تَنْمِيمٌ لِلبَيْتِ، وَهُوَ مِنَ الِاقْتِصَادِ» ؛ الَّذِي هُوَ : «التَّوسط فِي الأُمُورِ»، وَمِنْهُ : وَلَا عَالَ مَنِ اقْتَصَدَه (۱) ؛ أَي : افْتَقَرَ.
Chapter on simple conversion. (70) And conversion is necessary except for that in which is found the combination of the two deficiencies, so be moderate. Al-Bajuri: ...and its example: 'Some man is an animal', and likewise the indefinite affirmative. So you say in the conversion of 'Man is an animal': 'Some animal is a man'. And its conversion as itself is also valid, so you say in the conversion of the mentioned example: 'Animal is a man'. As for the particular negative, it has no conversion. So do not say in 'Some animal is not a man': 'Some man is not an animal'. And likewise the indefinite negative. So do not say in 'Animal is not a man': 'Some man is not an animal', nor 'Man is not an animal'. As the author alerted to that, so let one ponder. His statement: 'And conversion is necessary... etc.': The 'al' is for covenant, and the covenanted is simple conversion. By it is excluded conversion by agreement and by opposition, for it is necessary for every proposition, even for that in which is found the combination of the two deficiencies – which is the particular negative. So an example of the first: you say in the conversion of 'Some animal is not a man': 'Some non-man is not an animal'. And an example of the second: you say in the conversion of the mentioned example: 'Some non-man is an animal'. And similar to that in which is found the combination of the two deficiencies is the indefinite negative. And his statement: 'for other than that which is found... etc.' meaning the particular negative. And it has no conversion only because truth does not remain in it necessarily, although it may remain incidentally in some cases, like your saying in 'Some man is not a stone': 'Some stone is not a man'. For truth has remained incidentally due to the specificity of the matter; evidence of its failing in a matter where the subject is more general than the predicate, so the negation of the more specific from some individuals of the more general is true, but the negation of the more general from some individuals of the more specific is not true. For it is true to say: 'Some animal is not a man', and it is not true to say: 'Some man is not an animal', as is not hidden. His statement: 'by it' i.e., in it, and here he used the masculine pronoun considering the word 'maa', and he used the feminine later considering its meaning. His statement: 'the combination of the two deficiencies' i.e., particularity and negation, for particularity is deficiency compared to universality because it is inferior, and negation is deficiency compared to affirmation because it is inferior. Gloss of the mother of the successful on the Ladder of Ambition. (70) And conversion is necessary except for that in which is found the combination of the two deficiencies, so be moderate. Al-Bajuri: His statement: 'faqtasid' (so be moderate) is a completion of the verse, and it is from 'iqtisad' which is moderation in affairs, and from it: 'He will not be poor who practices moderation'? i.e., he did not become poor.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion and clarifies its scope. The author begins by stating a general rule: simple conversion is necessary (always applicable) except when a proposition has both 'deficiencies' – namely, particularity and negativity. The term 'two deficiencies' is explained: particularity is a deficiency relative to universality, and negation is a deficiency relative to affirmation. Thus, the exception is the particular negative proposition. The passage then gives examples: a universal affirmative like 'Man is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a man'; the indefinite affirmative behaves similarly. However, a particular negative like 'Some animal is not a man' cannot be converted to 'Some man is not an animal' because such conversion would not preserve truth necessarily. The commentator illustrates this with an example: 'Some man is not a stone' accidentally converts to 'Some stone is not a man' due to the specific subject matter, but this is not a necessary relationship. The key point is that conversion must preserve truth in all cases, not just accidentally. The passage also mentions that the indefinite negative is like the particular negative in having no conversion. The hashiya adds a note on the word 'faqtasid' from the poem, explaining it as 'be moderate' and citing a hadith-like phrase.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الخستين
الجُزْئِيَّةُ السَّالِبَةُ
المُهْمَلَةُ السَّالِبَةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following propositions does NOT have a valid simple conversion?
A) Every intoxicant is forbidden (Universal affirmative)
B) Some animal is not a human (Particular negative)
C) No innovated matter is eternal (Universal negative)
D) Some human is an animal (Particular affirmative)

Key Takeaway
The particular negative is the only categorical proposition that cannot be simply converted, because truth must be preserved necessarily, not just accidentally.

?

Indefinite Negative Conversion Like Particular Negative
Author states that the indefinite negative lacks simple conversion due to non-necessary truth preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite negative proposition and determine whether it has a valid simple conversion.

Core Idea
An indefinite negative cannot be simply converted because its truth is not guaranteed after the operation.

This lesson explains why the indefinite negative proposition, such as 'Animal is not a man', lacks simple conversion, just like the particular negative. The commentator shows that conversion may accidentally preserve truth in some cases but never necessarily. Understanding this prevents invalid logical steps in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite negative proposition
Look for a proposition with no quantifier and a negative copula, e.g., 'Animal is not a man'. Recognize that it has the logical force of a particular negative.
Indefinite negatives are like particular negatives in inference rules.
2
State the rule: indefinite negatives have no simple conversion
The commentator says directly that the indefinite negative has no conversion because truth does not remain on the side of necessity.
3
Analyze the example of accidental conversion
Consider 'Man is not a stone'. Convert it: 'Some stone is not a man' is accidentally true because stone is entirely separate from man. But this truth is not guaranteed by logic; it depends on the material.
The example shows that conversion may work in some materials but not all.
4
Examine a case where conversion fails
Take 'Animal is not a man'. The subject is more general than the predicate. Conversion would give 'Man is not an animal', which is false. Thus, conversion is not valid in general.
The failure occurs when the subject is broader than the predicate.
5
Conclude: indefinite negatives lack necessary conversion
Because truth is not preserved for all materials, the indefinite negative does not have simple conversion. It only works accidentally, which is not sufficient for logical validity.
This aligns with the particular negative rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَمِثْلُهَا) أَيْ: مِثْلُ مَا وُجِدَ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ ، وَأَنَّتَ الضَّمِيرَ؛ نَظَراً لِمَعْنَى «ما» كَمَا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (المُهْمَلَةُ السَّلْبِيَّة) أَيْ: نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : «الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ» .
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا عَكْسُ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا(٢) يَبْقَ فِيهِ الصِّدْقُ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَدْ يَبْقَى اتِّفَاقاً فِي بَعْضِ المَوَادِّ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ»: «الحَجَرُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ أَوْ بَعْضُ : الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ بَقِيَ الصِّدْقُ اتفاقاً لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي مَادَّةٍ يَكُونُ المَوْضُوعُ فِيهَا أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ، فَيَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَخَصَّ عَنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَضُ، فَإِنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ أَوْ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ).
His statement: 'And similar to it' i.e., similar to that in which is found the combination of the two deficiencies, and he made the pronoun feminine considering the meaning of 'maa' as previously. And his statement: 'the indefinite negative' i.e., like your saying: 'Animal is not a man'. And it has no conversion only because truth does not remain in it necessarily, although it may remain incidentally in some cases, like your saying in 'Man is not a stone': 'Stone is not a man' or 'Some stone is not a man'. For truth has remained incidentally due to the specificity of the matter; evidence of its failing in a matter where the subject is more general than the predicate, so the negation of the more specific from individuals of the more general is true, but the negation of the more general from individuals of the more specific is not true. For it is true to say: 'Animal is not a man', and it is not true to say: 'Man is not an animal' or 'Some man is not an animal'.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the indefinite negative (e.g., 'Animal is not a man') is like the particular negative in having no simple conversion. The commentator explains that just as the particular negative lacks a necessary conversion, so does the indefinite because it has the logical force of a particular. The reason is that truth does not remain necessarily, only accidentally in some cases. An example is given: 'Man is not a stone' can be converted to 'Stone is not a man' only by accident, due to the specific material of stone. If the subject is more general than the predicate, the negation of the more specific (e.g., 'Some animal is not man') is true, but the converse ('Some man is not animal') is false. Thus, no necessary conversion is possible.

Key Terms
المُهْمَلَةُ السَّلْبِيَّة (al-muhmala al-salibiyya): Indefinite negative proposition: a proposition with no explicit quantifier and a negative copula, e.g., 'Animal is not a man'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the indefinite negative 'Animal is not a man' not have a valid simple conversion?
A) Because it is equivalent to a universal negative
B) Because truth is not preserved necessarily
C) Because it is affirmative in force
D) Because it is equivalent to a particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
The indefinite negative proposition lacks simple conversion because truth is not preserved necessarily, only accidentally in specific materials.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Find an indefinite negative proposition in a fiqh text (e.g., 'This contract is not valid') and explain why its conversion would not be logically guaranteed.

Conversion is Naturally Ordered, Not Conventionally Ordered
Author explains that conversion involves a natural reordering of terms, not a conventional one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural order from conventional order in conversion by analyzing how meaning changes upon reversal.

Core Idea
Conversion is not arbitrary; it changes meaning because the original order is natural.

In logic, the order of terms in a proposition is not always interchangeable. When we perform conversion, we reverse the order of subject and predicate (in categorical) or antecedent and consequent (in conditional). This passage explains that this reversal is not a mere conventional reordering but a natural transformation that alters the proposition's meaning. Understanding this helps us use conversion correctly in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms 'murattab bi al-tabʻ' (naturally ordered) and 'murattab bi al-waḍʻ' (conventionally ordered). Notice that the passage focuses on the former, contrasting it with the latter.
2
Understand the example of conversion
The passage gives the example of converting 'Every man is an animal' to 'Some animal is a man'. Observe how the original meaning—animality affirmed for every man—changes to humanity affirmed for some animals. This shows that order is not arbitrary.
3
Explain why the order is natural
The commentator argues that the order in categorical and conditional propositions is natural because if you reverse it (delaying subject or antecedent), the meaning changes. This is not the case with conventional orders (e.g., lists) where reversal may not change meaning.
4
Apply to another proposition
Take a proposition from Islamic law, such as 'Every intoxicant is haram'. Convert it to 'Some haram thing is an intoxicant' (simple conversion). Analyze how the meaning shifts from prohibition being a property of intoxicants to intoxicating being a property of some prohibited items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ) أَيْ: بِأَقْسَامِهِ الثَّلَاثَةِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ (۳) ، وَإِنْ كَانَ ظَاهِرُ سِيَاقِ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ أَنَّ المُرَادَ العَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي؛ لِأَنَّهُ بِصَدَدِهِ.
وَقَوْلُهُ : (فِي مُرَتَّبِ بِالطَّبْعِ) أي : الَّذِي هُوَ كُلٌّ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ .
وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا مُرَتَّباً بِالطَّبْعِ؛ لأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهُ اقْتَضَاهُ الطَّبْعُ ، لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْ أُزِيلَ تَغَيَّرَ المَعْنَى؛ ذ بِتَأْخِيرِ المَوْضُوعِ عَنِ المَحْمُولِ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَتَأخِيرِ المُقَدَّمِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ يَتَغَيَّرُ المَعْنَى ؛ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ يَصِيرُ المَعْنَى : تُبُوتُ الإِنْسَانِيَّةِ لِبَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الحَيَوَانِ بَعْدَ أَنْ كَانَ نُبُوتُ الحَيَوَانِيَّةِ لِكُلِّ إِنْسَانٍ، وَأَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ ...
His statement: 'And conversion' i.e., with its three divisions, as Sheikh al-Malawi said in his larger (commentary), although the apparent context of the author's speech is that the intended is simple conversion because he is treating it. And his statement: 'in an order by nature' i.e., which is each of the categorical and the conditional conjunctive. And each of them is ordered by nature only because its order is required by nature, because if it were removed the meaning would change. For by delaying the subject after the predicate in the categorical, and delaying the antecedent after the consequent in the conditional conjunctive, the meaning changes. Do you not see that if you say in the conversion of 'Every man is an animal': 'Some animal is a man', the meaning becomes: the affirmation of humanity for some individuals of animal after it had been the affirmation of animality for every man? And that if you say...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage explains that conversion involves a natural order, not a conventional one. The commentator first notes that the poet's word 'al-aks' (conversion) here may refer to all three types (simple, by agreement, by opposition) according to al-Malawi, though context suggests simple conversion. Then he explains that conversion is 'naturally ordered' because both categorical and conditional propositions have a natural order – subject before predicate, antecedent before consequent – such that reversing them changes the meaning. An example is given: converting 'Every man is an animal' to 'Some animal is a man' changes the affirmed property from animality (for all men) to humanity (for some animals). This shows that conversion is not merely a conventional reordering but a natural transformation.

Key Terms
مُرَتَّب بِالطَّبْعِ
مُرَتَّب بِالوَضْعِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it follows the rules of grammar
B) Because reversing the terms changes the meaning of the proposition
C) Because it is based on the will of the speaker
D) Because it applies only to universal propositions

Key Takeaway
Conversion changes meaning because the original order is natural, not conventional; therefore, the converse is a new proposition requiring independent verification.

The Indefinite Negative Has No Valid Conversion
Explains why indefinite negative propositions have no valid conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite negative proposition and explain why it does not have a valid conversion, distinguishing necessary truth from accidental truth.

Core Idea
An indefinite negative's conversion is not necessary—it may be true accidentally, but that doesn't make it valid.

In this lesson, we examine the indefinite negative proposition (المهملة السلبية), which is a negative statement without a quantifier, e.g., 'The animal is not a human.' The commentator explains that this type of proposition is equated to the particular negative in lacking the conditions for valid conversion. We will see why conversion of such a proposition fails the test of necessary truth preservation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite negative proposition
Look for a sentence like 'A is not B' without 'all' or 'some'. The example given: 'الحيوان ليس بإنسان' (The animal is not a human).
Indefinite means no quantifier, so it is ambiguous between universal and particular.
2
Recall the rule of conversion
Conversion requires that the truth of the original proposition necessarily entails the truth of the converted proposition. It must hold for all cases, not just coincidentally.
This is the critical condition: necessary truth preservation (صدق على وجه اللزوم).
3
Test the conversion of the example
Swap subject and predicate: 'الإنسان ليس بحيوان' (The human is not an animal). Is this necessarily true? No, because all humans are animals. So conversion fails.
The failure occurs because the subject (animal) is broader than the predicate (human).
4
Understand the accidental case
The author notes that in some materials, e.g., 'الإنسان ليس بحجر' (The human is not a stone), conversion to 'الحجر ليس بإنسان' (The stone is not a human) is true coincidentally. But this is not a valid conversion because it does not hold for all materials.
Accidental truth does not satisfy the condition of necessity. The rule requires generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِثْلُهَا المُهْمَلَةُ السَّلْبِيَّةُ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَمِثْلُهَا) أَيْ: مِثْلُ مَا وُجِدَ بِهِ اجْتِمَاعُ الخِسَّتَيْنِ ، وَأَنَّثَ الضَّمِيرَ؛ نَظَراً لِمَعْنَى «ما» كَمَا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (المُهْمَلَةُ السَّلْبِيَّة) أَيْ: نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : «الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ» . وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا عَكْسُ ; لأَنَّهُ لَا يَبْقَى فِيهِ الصِّدْقُ عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَدْ يَبْقَى اتِّفَاقاً فِي بَعْضِ المَوَادِّ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ»: «الحَجَرُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ أَوْ بَعْضُ الحَجَرِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ، فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ بَقِيَ الصِّدْقُ اتفاقاً لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ; بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلْفِهِ فِي مَادَّةٍ يَكُونُ المَوْضُوعُ فِيهَا أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَحْمُولِ، فَيَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَخَصِّ عَنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ سَلْبُ الأَعَمِّ عَنْ أَفْرَادِ الأَخَصِّ، فَإِنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الحَيَوَانُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الإِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ أَوْ بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ).
And similar to it (the proposition with both deficiencies) is the indefinite negative, because it is in the power of the particular. His saying: 'And similar to it' meaning: similar to that in which the conjunction of the two deficiencies is found, and he made the pronoun feminine, considering the meaning of 'ma' as previously. And his saying: 'the indefinite negative' meaning: like your saying: 'The animal is not a human'. And it has no conversion because the truth does not remain in it in a necessary manner, even though it may remain coincidentally in some materials, as in your saying concerning 'The human is not a stone': 'The stone is not a human' or 'Some stone is not a human', for indeed the truth remains coincidentally due to the specificity of the material; evidence is the failure of this in a material where the subject is more general than the predicate, for then the negation of the more specific from the individuals of the more general is true, but the negation of the more general from the individuals of the more specific is not true. For it is true to say: 'The animal is not a human', but it is not true to say: 'The human is not an animal' or 'Some human is not an animal'.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage defines the indefinite negative proposition (مهملة سلبية) and explains why it lacks a valid conversion ('aks). The author equates it to the proposition that combines the two deficiencies (الخِسَّتَيْنِ), i.e., the particular negative. The reason is that for conversion to be valid, the truth must be preserved necessarily (على وجه اللزوم), not just coincidentally. An example: 'The animal is not a human' cannot be converted to 'The human is not an animal' because the subject (animal) is broader than the predicate (human). The indefinite negative's truth may hold in specific materials (like 'The human is not a stone' converting to 'The stone is not a human') but this is accidental. Therefore, it is excluded from the rule of conversion.

Key Terms
المهملة السلبية
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Universal affirmative
B) Particular negative
C) Universal negative
D) Particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
The indefinite negative, like the particular negative, lacks a valid conversion because truth is not preserved necessarily, only accidentally in some cases.

Construct an indefinite negative proposition of your own from Fiqh (e.g., 'Selling alcohol is not permissible'). Then attempt its conversion and check if the converted proposition is necessarily true. Explain why it fails or succeeds based on the subject-predicate relation.

Naturally Ordered vs Conventionally Ordered Propositions in Conversion
Distinguishes naturally ordered from conventionally ordered for conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish naturally ordered propositions (categorical and connective conditional) from conventionally ordered (disjunctive conditional) and explain why conversion applies only to the former.

Core Idea
Conversion (al-'aks) only applies when reversing the parts changes the meaning.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's logic text clarifies a crucial limitation of conversion. Conversion is an immediate inference that swaps the subject and predicate (or antecedent and consequent). But not all propositions can be converted in the same way. The author distinguishes between propositions whose order is natural — meaning swapping changes the meaning — and those whose order is only conventional — where swapping leaves the meaning unchanged. This determines which propositions are subject to the rules of 'aks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find the two types: naturally ordered (murattab bi al-tab') and conventionally ordered (murattab bi al-wad').
2
Understand the criterion
Ask: 'Would swapping the parts change the meaning?' For naturally ordered, yes; for conventional, no.
3
Apply to proposition types
Categorical and connective conditional are naturally ordered; disjunctive conditional is conventionally ordered.
4
Test with examples
Use the author's examples: 'Every human is an animal' → 'Some animal is a human' changes meaning. For disjunctive, 'Either the number is even or odd' → 'Either odd or even' does not change meaning.
5
Conclude the rule
Therefore, formal conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) applies only to naturally ordered propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبِ بِالطَّبْعِ وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبِ بِالوَضْعِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ) أَيْ: بِأَقْسَامِهِ الثَّلَاثَةِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ ظَاهِرُ سِيَاقِ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ أَنَّ المُرَادَ العَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي؛ لِأَنَّهُ بِصَدَدِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (فِي مُرَتَّبِ بِالطَّبْعِ) أي : الَّذِي هُوَ كُلٌّ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ كُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا مُرَتَّباً بِالطَّبْعِ؛ لأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهُ اقْتَضَاهُ الطَّبْعُ ، لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْ أُزِيلَ تَغَيَّرَ المَعْنَى; أَيْ بِتَأْخِيرِ المَوْضُوعِ عَنِ المَحْمُولِ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَتَأخِيرِ المُقَدَّمِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ يَتَغَيَّرُ المَعْنَى ; أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ يَصِيرُ المَعْنَى : ثُبُوتُ الإِنْسَانِيَّةِ لِبَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الحَيَوَانِ بَعْدَ أَنْ كَانَ ثُبُوتُ الحَيَوَانِيَّةِ لِكُلِّ إِنْسَانٍ، وَأَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي عَكْسِ كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ» : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُوداً، فَالشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، يَصِيرُ المَعْنَى : ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُودِ النَّهَارِ بَعْدَ أَنْ كَانَ ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ وُجُودِ النَّهَارِ لِطُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ، فَتَأمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ) أي: الَّذِي هُوَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَتْ مُرَتَّبَةٌ بِالوَضْعِ فَقَطْ ; لِأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهَا لَمْ يَقْتَضِهِ الطَّبْعُ ; لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْ أُزِيلَ لَمْ يَتَغَيَّرِ المَعْنَى ; إِذْ بِتَأْخِيرِ المُقَدَّمِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِيهَا لَا يَتَغَيَّرُ المَعْنَى ; أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً » : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ فَرْداً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً، لَمْ يَخْتَلِفِ المَعْنَى ، بَلْ هُوَ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ; بُدِّلَ ، أَوْ لَمْ يُبَدَّلْ ; إِذِ المَعْنَى عَلَى كُلِّ : ثُبُوتُ العِنَادِ بَيْنَهُمَا، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّرْتِيبُ فِي ذَلِكَ إِلَّا بِالوَضْعِ.
And the conversion is in the naturally ordered, and not in the conventionally ordered. His saying: 'And the conversion' i.e., in its three divisions, as al-Malwi said in his Major [commentary], even if the apparent context of the author's words is that what is intended is the simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) because he is in that context. And his saying: 'in naturally ordered' meaning: which is both the categorical [proposition] and the connective conditional. And each of them is naturally ordered because its order is required by nature, since if it is removed, the meaning changes. That is, by delaying the subject after the predicate in the categorical, and delaying the antecedent after the consequent in the connective conditional, the meaning changes. Do you not see that if you say in converting 'Every human is an animal' to 'Some animal is a human', the meaning becomes: the establishment of humanness for some individuals of animal after it was the establishment of animality for every human; and if you say in converting 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' to 'Sometimes when daytime exists, the sun is rising', the meaning becomes: the establishment of the necessity of sunrise for the existence of daytime after it was the establishment of the necessity of daytime's existence for sunrise, so ponder. His saying: 'And it is not in conventionally ordered' meaning: which is the disjunctive conditional. And it is ordered only by convention because its order is not required by nature, since if it is removed, the meaning does not change. For by delaying the antecedent after the consequent in it, the meaning does not change. Do you not see that if you say concerning their statement 'Either the number is even or it is odd' to 'Either the number is odd or it is even', the meaning does not differ, rather it is one and the same; whether swapped or not; for the meaning in both is the establishment of repugnance between them. Therefore the order in that is only by convention.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage clarifies which types of propositions are subject to conversion ('aks). The author states that conversion applies only to propositions that are 'naturally ordered' (مرتَّب بالطبع), meaning categorical (حملي) and connective conditional (شرطية متصلة). In these, reversing the order of subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent changes the meaning, as illustrated with the examples of 'Every human is an animal' converting to 'Some animal is a human' and 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' converting to 'Sometimes when daytime exists, the sun is rising'. In contrast, the disjunctive conditional (منفصلة) is only 'conventionally ordered' (بالوضع); swapping the parts does not change the essential meaning of mutual exclusion, as in 'Either the number is even or odd' — swapping yields the same meaning. Therefore, conversion in the strict sense is not applicable to disjunctives. This distinction is crucial for correct application of conversion rules.

Key Terms
مرتَّب بالطبع
مرتَّب بالوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of conditional is excluded from the rule of conversion?
A) Connective conditional
B) Disjunctive conditional
C) Categorical
D) Indefinite negative

Key Takeaway
Conversion (al-'aks) applies only to propositions whose order is natural, not to those ordered merely by convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify whether each proposition is naturally or conventionally ordered: (a) 'Every substance is an accident', (b) 'If it is raining, then the ground is wet', (c) 'Either the act is wajib or it is haram'.

The Valid Scope of 'Aks (Conversion) in Conditionals
Defines 'aks and distinguishes ordering types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is suitable for conversion based on its ordering type.

Core Idea
Conversion only preserves truth in naturally ordered conditionals; positionally ordered ones are immune.

In this passage, the author defines 'aks (conversion) and sets its scope: it applies only to conditionals whose order is natural (marattab bil-tab'), not those that are merely positional (marattab bil-wad'). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying immediate inference in Islamic sciences, especially when arguments rely on cause-effect relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'aks
Locate in the Arabic where 'aks is defined. The matn states: 'And the conversion is in what is arranged naturally, and not in what is arranged positionally.' This sets the condition for valid conversion.
The definition is simple: swapping antecedent and consequent while preserving truth, but only under a specific condition.
2
Distinguish natural from positional ordering
Natural ordering means the relationship is inherent, like cause-effect (sunrise causes daytime). Positional ordering is conventional, like disjunctions (even or odd). Reversing natural order changes meaning; reversing positional order does not.
3
Apply the condition to an example
Take the natural conditional: 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists.' When converted to 'If daytime exists, then the sun rises,' the meaning shifts from causation to mere implication. This change shows conversion is not truth-preserving in natural orderings.
The commentator warns: 'so reflect' — meaning the change is subtle but significant.
4
Test a disjunctive conditional
Consider 'Either the number is even or the number is odd.' Reversing to 'Either odd or even' yields the same meaning: mutual exclusivity. Therefore, conversion is valid here.
The disjunctive is 'positionally arranged' because order is conventional.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي (۷۲) وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْعِ وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ . الباجوري ✿ فِي عَكْسِ كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ» : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُوداً، فَالشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، يَصِيرُ المَعْنَى : ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُودِ النَّهَارِ بَعْدَ أَنْ كَانَ ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ وُجُودِ النَّهَارِ لِطُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ، فَتَأمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ) أي: الَّذِي هُوَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَتْ مُرَتَّبَةٌ بِالوَضْعِ فَقَطْ ؛ لِأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهَا لَمْ يَقْتَضِهِ الطَّبْعُ ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْ أُزِيلَ لَمْ يَتَغَيَّرِ الْمَعْنَى؛ إِذْ بِتَأْخِيرِ المُقَدَّمِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِيهَا لَا يَتَغَيَّرُ المَعْنَى ؛ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً » : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ فَرْداً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً، لَمْ يَخْتَلِفِ المَعْنَى ، بَلْ هُوَ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ؛ بُدِّلَ ، أَوْ لَمْ يُبَدَّلْ ؛ إِذِ المَعْنَى عَلَى كُلِّ : ثُبُوتُ العِنَادِ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّرْتِيبُ فِي ذَلِكَ إِلَّا بِالوَضْعِ .
Chapter on simple conversion. (72) And the conversion is in what is arranged naturally, and not in what is arranged positionally. al-Bajuri: In the conversion of 'Whenever the sun is rising, then the daytime exists': 'It might be that if the daytime exists, then the sun is rising.' The meaning becomes: the affirmation of the necessitation of the sun's rising for the existence of daytime, after it was the affirmation of the necessitation of daytime's existence for the sun's rising, so reflect. His saying: 'And not in what is arranged positionally' means: which is the disjunctive conditional. And it is only arranged positionally because its ordering is not required by nature; since if it were removed, the meaning would not change. For by delaying the antecedent from the consequent in it, the meaning does not change. Do you not see that if you say in their saying 'Either the number is even or the number is odd': 'Either the number is odd or the number is even', the meaning does not differ, rather it is one thing, whether changed or not, since the meaning in every case is the affirmation of opposition between them. Therefore, the ordering in that is only positional.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks (conversion) and explains its scope. The author states that conversion applies only to conditionals that are naturally ordered (marattab bil-tab'), not to those that are positionally arranged (marattab bil-wad'). The natural ordering means the antecedent and consequent have a logical order that cannot be reversed without changing meaning. For example, in the conditional 'Whenever the sun is rising, the daytime exists', reversing it to 'If the daytime exists, then the sun is rising' changes the meaning from a cause-effect relationship to a mere indication. In contrast, in disjunctive conditionals like 'Either the number is even or odd', reversing the order does not change the meaning; it is merely a positional arrangement. The author illustrates that the meaning is the same whether we say 'even or odd' or 'odd or even', as both affirm the opposition between the two. Therefore, conversion is only valid in naturally ordered conditional propositions.

Key Terms
العكس
مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْعِ
مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ
الشَّرْطِيَّةُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of conditional proposition can be validly converted (reversed) without changing the meaning?
A) A naturally ordered conditional like 'If the sun rises, daytime exists.'
B) A positionally ordered conditional like 'Either the number is even or odd.'
C) Both types.
D) Neither type.

Key Takeaway
Conversion ('aks) of conditionals preserves truth only when the ordering is positional (e.g., disjunctives); in natural orderings, reversal changes the logical relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following conditional proposition is naturally or positionally ordered: 'If Zayd is human, then Zayd is mortal.' Can it be validly converted? Explain your reasoning.
?
Take a disjunctive proposition from usul al-fiqh: 'The act is either wajib or mubah.' Show that reversing the order does not change the meaning, and thus conversion is valid.

Natural vs. Positional Ordering: Commentary Analysis
Commentary expanding the distinction between natural and positional ordering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural from positional ordering in conditional propositions using the commentator's examples and explain why the distinction matters for conversion.

Core Idea
In disjunctive conditionals, order is conventional; reversing does not change the meaning.

The commentator al-Bajuri expands the matn's concise rule with concrete examples that illuminate the difference between natural and positional ordering. By examining these examples, you will see how the nature of the logical relationship determines whether conversion is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim
The matn states: 'Conversion is in what is arranged naturally, and not in what is arranged positionally.' This is the abstract rule.
2
Analyze the commentator's example of natural ordering
Al-Bajuri provides the example: 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists.' He then shows the converted form: 'If daytime exists, then the sun rises.' He notes that the meaning changes from daytime's existence being necessitated by sunrise to sunrise being necessitated by daytime's existence. This shows that natural ordering reflects a causal dependence that cannot be reversed without altering truth.
The commentator says 'so reflect'—indicating the subtle but important shift.
3
Analyze the commentator's example of positional ordering
Al-Bajuri then discusses disjunctive conditionals: 'Either the number is even or odd.' He demonstrates that reversing to 'Either odd or even' yields the same meaning—mutual exclusivity. The order is merely conventional; nature does not dictate which disjunct comes first.
This is the 'positionally arranged' type.
4
Synthesize the distinction
Natural ordering involves a real-world dependence (e.g., cause-effect), while positional ordering is a matter of linguistic convention. Therefore, conversion is invalid in natural orderings (meaning changes) but valid in positional orderings (meaning unchanged).
The commentator concludes: 'Therefore, the ordering in that is only positional.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العكس المستوي (۷۲) وَالعَكْسُ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالطَّبْعِ وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ . الباجوري ✿ فِي عَكْسِ كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ ، فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ» : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُوداً، فَالشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، يَصِيرُ المَعْنَى : ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُودِ النَّهَارِ بَعْدَ أَنْ كَانَ ثُبُوتُ لُزُومِ وُجُودِ النَّهَارِ لِطُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ، فَتَأمَّلْ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَيْسَ فِي مُرَتَّبٍ بِالوَضْعِ) أي: الَّذِي هُوَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَتْ مُرَتَّبَةٌ بِالوَضْعِ فَقَطْ ؛ لِأَنَّ تَرْتِيبَهَا لَمْ يَقْتَضِهِ الطَّبْعُ ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ لَوْ أُزِيلَ لَمْ يَتَغَيَّرِ الْمَعْنَى؛ إِذْ بِتَأْخِيرِ المُقَدَّمِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِيهَا لَا يَتَغَيَّرُ المَعْنَى ؛ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّكَ إِذَا قُلْتَ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً » : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ فَرْداً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً، لَمْ يَخْتَلِفِ المَعْنَى ، بَلْ هُوَ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ؛ بُدِّلَ ، أَوْ لَمْ يُبَدَّلْ ؛ إِذِ المَعْنَى عَلَى كُلِّ : ثُبُوتُ العِنَادِ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّرْتِيبُ فِي ذَلِكَ إِلَّا بِالوَضْعِ .

Absolute Generality and Specificity: Definition and Application
Definition of absolute generality/specificity with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify absolute generality and specificity in a pair of terms and apply the concept to Islamic concepts like hamd and shukr.

Core Idea
Absolute generality occurs when one term is more general than another because of exclusive aspects not shared.

In this passage, the commentator defines the logical relation of 'absolute generality and specificity' (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq). Using the classic example of animal and man, he clarifies how two terms can share a common application while one has an exclusive scope. He then applies this to the relationship between linguistic ḥamd and customary shukr, showing how ḥamd is more general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of absolute generality/specificity
Find the two conditions: two terms coincide on one locus, and one term is exclusive to an aspect not shared by the other.
The definition is given as 'عبارة عن مقولين تواردا في محلّ واحدٍ، وانفرد أحدهما بطرف لا يشاركه صاحبه فيه'.
2
Examine the example of animal and man
Note how they coincide on 'rational animal' (man) and how animal is exclusive to non-rational beings like 'neighing' (horse).
3
Apply the definition to ḥamd and shukr
Determine which term is more general and which is more specific based on the restrictions mentioned: linguistic ḥamd is done by tongue alone for any perfection, while customary shukr requires all instruments and is only for blessings from Allah.
The passage states that customary shukr has two additional restrictions, making it absolutely more specific.
4
Verify the logical rule
Confirm the principle: 'كل ما فيه قيد زائد فهو أخص' (everything with an additional restriction is more specific).
This rule is explicitly stated at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على مقدمة
ظـ
وَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مُطْلَقاً مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الثَّلَاثَةِ قَبْلَهُ .
قوله : وَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مُطْلَقاً مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الثَّلَاثَةِ حقيقةُ العموم والخصوص المطلق : عبارة عن مقولين تواردا في محلّ واحدٍ، وانفرد أحدهما بطرف لا يشاركه صاحبه فيه»؛ ومثال ذلك : «الحَيَوَان، والإِنْسَان»، فإنَّهما تواردا على «الحَيَوَان النَّاطِق»، وانفرد الحَيَوَان» بـ: «الصَّاهِل» وغيره ممَّا ليس بناطق .
فأما القسم الأوّل من هذه الثلاثة، وهو : الحمد اللُّغوي، فإنَّ الحمد فيه أعم مطلقاً، فإنَّه يكون باللسان وحده، ويكون في مقابلة النّعمة وغيرها مِنَ الكمالات، والشُّكرُ العرفي لا يكون إِلَّا [بجميع] الآلات، ولا يكون إلا في مقابلة نعمة منعم واحد وهو الله تعالى، ففيه قيدان زائدان، وكلُّ ما فيه قيد زائد فهو أخص.
Discourse on the Introduction. And it is absolutely more specific than each of the three before it. His saying: 'And it is absolutely more specific than each of the three' — the reality of absolute generality and specificity is: it is an expression of two terms that coincide in one locus, and one of them is exclusive to an aspect in which its companion does not share it; and an example of that is 'animal' and 'man', for they coincide on 'rational animal', and 'animal' is exclusive to 'neighing' and other than that which is not rational. As for the first of these three, which is linguistic hamd, indeed hamd in it is absolutely more general, for it is done by the tongue alone, and it is in return for blessings and other perfections, while customary shukr is only with [all] the instruments, and it is only in return for a blessing from one Benefactor, which is Allah the Exalted, so it has two additional restrictions, and everything that has an additional restriction is more specific.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary explaining the logical concept of 'absolute generality and specificity' (العموم والخصوص المطلق) and applying it to the relationship between hamd and shukr. The commentator begins by stating that 'it' (likely the customary shukr) is absolutely more specific than each of the three preceding terms (probably hamd lughawi, hamd 'urfi, and perhaps shukr lughawi). The definition of absolute generality/specificity is given: two terms that coincide on one locus (i.e., share an application) but one of them has an exclusive aspect not shared by the other. The classic example is 'animal' and 'man'. They coincide on 'rational animal' (man), but animal is exclusive to non-rational animals like 'neighing' (horse). Then he applies this to the three terms: the first is linguistic hamd. Linguistic hamd is absolutely more general than customary shukr because hamd is only done by the tongue and can be for blessings as well as other perfections. Customary shukr, on the other hand, is done with all instruments (all bodily faculties) and only in return for a blessing from Allah. Thus it has two additional restrictions: (1) using all instruments, not just tongue, and (2) being specifically for a blessing from Allah. Any term with an extra restriction is more specific. So linguistic hamd is more general, and customary shukr is more specific. This illustrates the logical concept using religious terms, tying it back to the earlier discussion.

Key Terms
العموم والخصوص المطلق
الحمد اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Customary shukr is absolutely more specific than linguistic hamd.
C) They are synonymous.
D) They have mutual generality and specificity.

Key Takeaway
Absolute generality/specificity is a relation where one term has a broader application than another due to an exclusive aspect, as exemplified by animal vs. man and hamd vs. shukr.

Three Types of Conventional Signification
Author defines and explains the three divisions of conventional signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conventional signification and explain the commentator's correction of the base text's phrasing.

Core Idea
Conventional signification is either conformity, inclusion, or implication — but the phrasing must avoid circularity.

In this passage, the author defines the three types of conventional signification used in Islamic logic. These types are essential for understanding how words convey meanings in definitions and arguments. The commentator not only explains the division but also corrects an ambiguous expression in the base text to prevent a logical error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the definitions of مطابقة (full meaning), تضمن (part of the meaning), and التزام (external necessary meaning). Note that each type refers to a different relation between the word and the meaning it signifies.
These are the standard divisions in the tradition.
2
Understand the basis of division
The division is based on the relationship between the word and the meaning: either the word is directly placed for the meaning (مطابقة), or it is placed for something that necessarily implies the meaning, which itself is either internal (تضمن) or external (التزام) to the original meaning.
This logic ensures all possible significations are covered.
3
Recognize the inductive nature
The author states that the confinement to three is inductive, not rational, because it does not cover combinations like signifying both the whole and the part simultaneously. This shows that the enumeration is based on observation of common usage.
Inductive means derived from experience, not pure reason.
4
Analyze the commentator's correction
The commentator critiques the base text's phrase 'ما وضع له اللفظ' (what the word was placed for) as causing confusion. He corrects it to 'المعنى الذي وافق اللفظ' (the meaning that agrees with the word) to avoid implying that the word is placed for a meaning that agrees with another meaning, which would be circular.
This correction preserves the direct relationship between word and meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَهُ» وَجُزْئِهِ : «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ الْتِزَامَ، إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمُ وَلَمَّا كَانَتْ دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةُ - وَهِيَ المُعْتَبَرَةُ عِنْدَهُمْ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ - تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : دَلَالَةِ مُطَابَقَةٍ، وَدَلَالَةِ تَضَمُّنٍ، وَدَلَالَةِ الْتِزَامِ: أَشَارَ النَّاظِمُ إِلَى القِسْمِ الأَوَّلِ بِقَوْلِهِ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ) أَيْ: عَلَى المَعْنَى الَّذِي وَافَقَ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ ؛ أَيْ : مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ وُضِعَ لَهُ.
قوله : (تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامِ) وجه الحصر في الثلاثة : أنَّه لا يلزم مِن حُضُور اللفظ في الذهن حضور المعنى إلا لعلاقة بينهما، وهي إما كون اللفظ موضوعاً للمعنى كما في المطابقة، أو لأمرٍ يلزمه ذلك المعنى . ثمَّ هذا اللازم : إمَّا داخل في ملزومه كما في التَّضمن، وإمَّا خارج كما في الالتزام، وهذا الحصر استقرائي لا عقليّ؛ لأنَّه بقي دلالة اللفظ على مجموع الثلاثة، أو على الكلِّ والجزء، أو على الكلِّ واللازم، أو على الجزء واللازم .
قوله : (مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ صوابُهُ : «على المعنى الذي وافق اللفظ»، وأما عبارته ففيها قلق؛ لأن معناها حينئذ : على المعنى الذي وافق المعنى الذي وُضِعَ له اللفظ، والموافق غير الموافق، فيلزم أن يكون اللفظ غير موضوع للمعنى بنفسه، بل للمعنى الموافق للمعنى الذي وضع له اللفظ، وهو فاسد.
The signification of a word for what agrees with it, they call it 'signification of conformity'; and for its part, 'inclusion'; and for what necessarily accompanies it, 'implication', if by the mind it is bound. And since conventional signification - which is considered by them as previously mentioned - is divided into three divisions: signification by conformity, signification by inclusion, and signification by implication: the author indicated the first division by his saying: 'The signification of a word for what agrees with it', i.e., for the meaning that agrees with that for which the word was placed; that is, from the aspect that it was placed for it. His saying: 'It is divided into three divisions' - the reason for the confinement to three is that the presence of the word in the mind does not necessitate the presence of the meaning except through a relationship between them, and that relationship is either that the word is placed for the meaning as in conformity, or for something that necessitates that meaning. Then this necessary accompaniment: either internal to its accompanied [meaning] as in inclusion, or external as in implication. And this confinement is inductive, not rational; because there remains the signification of the word for the sum of the three, or for the whole and the part, or for the whole and the necessary, or for the part and the necessary. His saying: 'that for which the word was placed' - the correct expression is: 'on the meaning that agrees with the word'; but his expression has unease, because its meaning then would be: on the meaning that agrees with the meaning for which the word was placed, and the agreeing is not the same as that which is agreed with, so it would necessitate that the word is not placed for the meaning itself, but for the meaning that agrees with the meaning for which the word was placed, which is corrupt.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of conventional signification (دلالة وضعية) that logicians consider. The first type is مطابقة (conformity): the word signifies its full meaning as it was placed for it. The second is تضمن (inclusion): the word signifies a part of that meaning. The third is التزام (implication): the word signifies something external to the meaning that is necessarily associated with it by the mind. The author then explains why there are only these three: every relationship between word and meaning is either the word being placed for that meaning (مطابقة) or for something that necessitates it. The necessitated thing is either inside the original meaning (تضمن) or outside (التزام). The author notes that this enumeration is inductive (استقرائي), not rational (عقلي), because it does not cover combinations like signifying both whole and part simultaneously, etc. Then he critiques the phrasing 'what the word was placed for' (ما وضع له اللفظ) in the original text, arguing that it leads to a contradiction because it would imply the word is placed for a meaning that agrees with another meaning, not for the meaning itself. He corrects it to 'the meaning that agrees with the word'. This correction ensures that the definition applies to both literal and metaphorical usage, as the next lesson explains.

Key Terms
دلالة مطابقة
دلالة تضمن
دلالة التزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of signification does the word 'human' (إنسان) have when signifying 'animal' (حيوان)?
A) مطابقة
B) تضمن
C) التزام
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three types of conventional signification cover all possible word-meaning relations, but the enumeration is inductive and requires precise phrasing to avoid circular definitions.
