Definition of Mental Implication and Its Two Types
Author defines mental implication and distinguishes its two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of mental implication (both mind and external vs. only mind) and identify which type qualifies as implicative indication in logic.

Core Idea
Mental implication in logic requires a necessary mental association, not just external correlation.

In this passage, al-Bannānī defines mental implication (al-luzūm al-dhihnī), a crucial concept for understanding implicative indication (dalālat al-iltizām) in logic. He distinguishes two types based on whether the concomitant exists only in the mind or also in external reality. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying logical analysis to terms and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of mental implication
Find the exact statement: 'that the meaning, whenever it is understood from the word, its necessary concomitant is understood mentally.' This is the core condition for mental implication.
2
Identify the first type: both mind and external
The example is 'four' implying 'evenness'. Note that evenness is a necessary concomitant both in the mind (conceptually) and in external reality (the number four is even).
This is the only type considered 'implicative indication' in logic.
3
Identify the second type: mind only
The example is 'blindness' implying 'sight'. Whenever you think of blindness, you must think of sight because blindness is absence of sight, but externally they are contrary. So the concomitant is only mental.
The author explains why sight is not part of the meaning via tadhammun.
4
Contrast with external implication (not mental)
The passage notes that external implication (e.g., 'raven' implies blackness externally, but not necessarily mentally) is not considered implicative in logic, but is in usul and bayan.
This clarifies the scope of logic: only mental implications are relevant.
5
Note the terminological nuance
The super-commentator points out that the matn's phrase 'bi-'aql' (by intellect) might suggest synonymy with mental, but 'aqli is more general; the author used it conventionally to mean mental.
This highlights precision in terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِقَوْلِهِ : إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمْ أَيْ : إِنِ الْتَزَمَ بِذِهْنِي. وَمُرَادُهُم بِـ «اللُّزُومِ الذِّهْنِيِّ : أَنْ يَكُونَ المَعْنَى كُلَّمَا فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ فُهِمَ ذِهْناً لَازِمُهُ. فَمِنْهُ : لَزِمَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالخَارِج مَعاً كَـ: «الزَّوجِيَّةِ المَدْلُولِ عَلَيْهَا بِلَفْظِ «الْأَرْبَعَةِ»، فَإِنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لِمَعْنَى الأَرْبَعَةِ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَفِي الخَارِج عَنْهُ. وَمِنْهُ : لَزِمَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَط دُونَ الخَارِج كَـ: «البَصَرِ» المَدْلُولِ عَلَيْهِ بِلَفْظِ العَمَى، فَإِنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تُصُوِّرَ العَمَى فِي الذِّهْنِ تُصُوِّرَ مَعَهُ البَصَرُ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لِلْعَمَى إِلَّا عَدَمُ البَصَرِ عَمَّا هُوَ مِنْ شَأْنِهِ، فَالبَصَرُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ ذِهْناً، وَهُوَ مُنَافٍ لَهُ خَارِجاً. وَلَيْسَ البَصَرُ مَدْلُولاً عَلَيْهِ بِالعَمَى تَضَمُّناً؛ إذْ لَيْسَ العَمَى هُوَ العَدَمُ وَالبَصَرُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ البَصَرُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَعْنَاهُ، وَإِنَّمَا مَعْنَاهُ العَدَمُ المُضَافُ إِلَى البَصَرِ، وَالمُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ خَارِجٌ عَنِ المُضَافِ. وَأَمَّا دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى خَارِجِ عَنْ مَعْنَاهُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ فِي الخَارِجِ دُونَ الذِّهْنِ فَلَا تُسَمَّى فِي المَنْطِقِ : دَلَالَةَ الْتِزَامِ، وَتُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ فِي عِلْمَيِ الأُصُولِ وَالبَيَانِ، وَذَلِكَ كَدَلَالَةِ لَفْظِ «الغُرَابِ» عَلَى السَّوَادِ، وَ«التَّلْحِ» عَلَى البَيَاضِ. قوله : (أَيْ : إِنِ الْعَزَمَ بِذِهْنِ ظاهرُهُ : مرادفة العقلي للذهني، وليس كذلك، بل العقلي أعم كما يأتي بيانه، فالنَّاظمُ أطلق العام وأراد به الخاص والقرينةُ العرف فإنَّ المعتبر عند المناطقة هو اللازم الذهني.
By his saying: 'if by intellect he committed' i.e., if he committed mentally. And their intention by 'mental implication' is that the meaning, whenever it is understood from the word, its necessary concomitant is understood mentally. So from it: what is implied both in the mind and externally, like 'evenness' indicated by the word 'four', for it is a concomitant of the meaning of four in the mind and externally. And from it: what is implied only in the mind without the external, like 'sight' indicated by the word 'blindness', for whenever blindness is imagined in the mind, sight is imagined with it; because there is no meaning for blindness except the absence of sight from that which is its proper subject, so sight is a mental concomitant of it, while it is contrary to it externally. And sight is not indicated by blindness by way of entailment (tadhammun); since blindness is not the absence and sight such that sight would be part of its meaning, but rather its meaning is the absence attributed to sight, and the attribute is external to the attribution. As for the indication of the word to something external to its meaning that is a concomitant of it externally without the mind, it is not called in logic 'implicative indication', but it is called that in the two sciences of usul and bayan, such as the indication of the word 'raven' upon blackness, and 'salt' upon whiteness. His statement: (i.e., if he committed with the mind its apparent meaning is the synonymy of 'aqli with dhihni, but it is not so, rather 'aqli is more general as will be explained, so the author used the general intending the specific, and the contextual clue is convention, for what is considered by the logicians is the mental concomitant.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage defines 'mental implication' (al-luzūm al-dhihnī), a key concept in logic for determining whether a linguistic indication qualifies as implicative (dalālat al-iltizām) within the discipline. The author first explains the meaning of the matn's phrase 'in bi-'aql iltazam': it means the mind commits to a mental association. The definition is: whenever the meaning of a word is understood, its necessary concomitant is understood mentally along with it. Two types are distinguished: (1) the concomitant holds both in the mind and externally, exemplified by 'evenness' (al-zawjiyya) being understood from 'four' – the number four always implies evenness, both conceptually and in reality; (2) the concomitant holds only in the mind, not externally, exemplified by 'sight' (al-basar) being understood from 'blindness' (al-'amā) – whenever one conceives blindness, sight is necessarily imagined because blindness is defined as the absence of sight, but in external reality sight is absent (blindness means no sight), so the two are contrary. The author then clarifies why sight is not part of the meaning of blindness through entailment (tadhammun): blindness is not the combination of absence and sight, but rather the absence attributed to sight; the attribute (sight) is outside the attribution, so it is not a component of the definition. Next, the author contrasts with a third type of indication: external implication only (lāzim fi al-khārij dūn al-dhihn), such as 'raven' implying blackness or 'salt' implying whiteness in external reality without a necessary mental association. This type is not considered implicative indication in logic but is so called in uṣūl and bayān. Finally, the commentator notes that the matn's phrase 'bi-'aql' (by intellect) might suggest synonymy between 'aqlī and dhihnī, but 'aqlī is more general; the author used it specifically to mean dhihnī based on conventional usage among logicians.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذهني
دلالة الالتزام
اللازم المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The indication of the word 'blindness' upon sight.
D) The indication of the word 'salt' upon whiteness.

Key Takeaway
Mental implication in logic is defined as a necessary mental association between the meaning of a word and its concomitant, and it is divided into two types: one that holds both mentally and externally, and one that holds only mentally.

Two Schools on Mental and Evident Concomitants
Author discusses two schools on synonymity of mental and evident concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two schools regarding the synonymity of mental and evident concomitants and state the definition of evident and non-evident.

Core Idea
The majority view: every mental concomitant is evident, but not all evident are mental.

In Islamic logic, necessary concomitants are classified by their epistemic accessibility. One key distinction is between mental and evident concomitants. Are they the same? This passage from al-Bājūrī’s commentary presents two schools of thought. Understanding this disagreement is essential for correctly analyzing definitions and arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
In the Arabic, find the phrase 'في ذلك مذهبان' and read the two descriptions. Note that the first school says they are synonymous, the second says the evident is broader.
2
Extract the definitions
The passage defines 'evident' as what is known from knowing both the concomitant and the concomitant together, and 'non-evident' as what requires a separate clue. Note the example of non-evident: the implication of temporality for bodies requires an intermediate (change).
3
Assess the commentator's stance
Though the author presents both views, the phrase 'وهذه الطريقة أتم فائدة' indicates preference for the second method, which treats evident as broader.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: هل اللازم الذهني مرادف للازم البيِّن أم لا؟ قلت: في ذلك مذهبان: أحدهما وهو مذهب كثير من المناطقة: ترادفهما، وعليه درج السنوسي، وشيخنا سيدي الطيب، وهو ظاهر كلام الشَّارح هنا. ثانيهما وهو مذهب الأكثر: أنَّ البين أعمُّ مِنَ الذهني. فالبين هو: ما يلزم من العلم باللازم والملزوم معاً العلم به. وغيرُ البيِّن ما لا يكفي فيه ذلك، بل يحتاجُ إلى قرينةٍ منفصلة: لزوم الحدوث للأجرام؛ لأنَّه لا بدَّ مِن أمرٍ ثالث وهو التَّغير. ثمَّ البيِّن ينقسم إلى: ذهني وغيره فإن كفى في العلم به العلم بالملزوم؛ بمعنى: أنَّه كلَّما حصل العلم بالملزوم حصل العلم باللازم، وبلزومه للملزوم، فهو ذهني كـ: لزوم الزوجية للأربعة، والبصر للعمى، وإن لم يكف فيه العلم بالملزوم وحده، بل افتقر إلى العلم باللازم أيضاً ك: مغايرة الإنسان للفرس، فإنَّ مغايرة الإنسان للفرس أمرٌ لازم للإنسان، لكن من تصوّر «الإنسان» لا يلزمُهُ بمجرَّد ذلك أن يخطر بباله مغايرته للفرس، بل يتصوَّر «الإنسان» وهو غافل عن «الفرس» جملة، فكيف عن مغايرته إيَّاه، فهو بين غير ذهني، وهذه الطريقةُ أتمُّ فائدةً، وعلى الطريقة الأولى درج صاحب القادرية تبعاً لأصله، فقال: وَاللُّزُومُ يَنْقَسِم : بَينِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَذَا إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ يَحْتَاجُ ، وَالبَيَّنُ لَا بَلْ هُوَ كُلَّمَا المُسَمَّى فُهِمَا فُهِمَ ذِهْناً مَا لَهُ قَدْ لَزِمَا قوله : ((اللَّازِمُ المُطْلَقُ» سمِّي : «مطلقاً؛ لعدم تقييد لزومه بالدهن ولا بالخارج.
For if you say: Is the mental concomitant synonymous with the evident concomitant or not? I say: There are two schools: One of them, which is the view of many logicians, is that they are synonymous, and al-Sanūsī and our master Sayyidī al-Ṭayyib proceeded upon it, and it is the apparent meaning of the commentator here. The second, which is the view of the majority, is that the evident is more general than the mental. So the evident is: what knowledge of it is necessitated by knowledge of the concomitant and the concomitant together. And what is non-evident is what is not sufficient by that, but rather requires a separate contextual clue: the implication of coming-to-be (ḥudūth) for bodies; because it needs a third thing, which is change. Then the evident divides into mental and other: if knowledge of it is sufficient by knowledge of the concomitant alone, meaning that whenever knowledge of the concomitant occurs, knowledge of the concomitant and its implication for the concomitant occurs, then it is mental, like the implication of evenness for four, and sight for blindness. But if knowledge of the concomitant alone is not sufficient, but rather also requires knowledge of the concomitant itself, like the difference of man from horse: for the difference of man from horse is a necessary concomitant of man, but whoever conceives 'man' does not, by that alone, necessarily bring to mind his difference from horse; rather he conceives 'man' while being completely unaware of 'horse', so how about his difference from it? So it is evident but not mental. And this method is more complete in benefit. And upon the first method proceeded the author of al-Qādiriyya, following his source, and he said: 'And implication divides into evident and other, and this requires an intermediary, while the evident is not rather it is that whenever the named entity is understood, what is its necessary concomitant is understood mentally.' His statement: 'the absolute concomitant' is called 'absolute' because its implication is not restricted to the mind nor to the external.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This classification passage addresses a key terminological distinction in Islamic logic: the relationship between 'mental concomitant' (al-lāzim al-dhihnī) and 'evident concomitant' (al-lāzim al-bayyin). The author begins with a question: Are they synonymous? Two schools are presented. The first, held by many logicians including al-Sanūsī and the commentator's teacher, asserts synonymy. The second, held by the majority, holds that 'evident' (al-bayyin) is more general than 'mental'. The author then defines the evident: it is that which is known by knowing the concomitant and its concomitant together. In contrast, the non-evident (ghayr al-bayyin) requires an external indicator, such as the implication of temporality (ḥudūth) for bodies, which requires a third factor (change) to be known. Next, the evident is subdivided: (1) mental (dhihnī) – knowing the concomitant alone suffices to know the concomitant and its implication, e.g., evenness for four or sight for blindness; (2) non-mental evident – knowing the concomitant alone does not suffice but requires also knowing the concomitant itself, e.g., the difference between man and horse is a necessary concomitant of man, but one may conceive man without thinking of horse, so it is not mental. The author notes that this second method is more beneficial. The passage concludes with a citation from the matn of al-Sullam (by al-Qādirī) summarizing the division, and a brief gloss on the term 'absolute concomitant' (al-lāzim al-muṭlaq) – so called because its implication is not restricted to mind or external.

Key Terms
اللازم البيّن
غير البيّن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) All mental concomitants are non-evident.

Key Takeaway
The classification of necessary concomitants into evident and non-evident is foundational for understanding immediate inferences in Islamic logic.

Division of Evident into Mental and Non-Mental
Author divides evident concomitants into mental and non-mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an evident concomitant as mental or non-mental by applying the definition given in this passage.

Core Idea
An evident concomitant is mental if knowing the concomitant alone suffices; otherwise it is non-mental.

The passage further divides evident concomitants into two types: mental (dhihnī) and non-mental. The mental type is known automatically when the correlate is conceived, while the non-mental requires additional awareness of the concomitant itself. This subtle distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subdivision
Find the sentence 'ثم البيّن ينقسم إلى: ذهني وغيره' to see how the evident is further divided.
2
Understand the criterion for mental
The mental evident is when knowing the correlate alone suffices to know the concomitant and its implication. Example: evenness for four.
3
Analyze the non-mental example
The difference between man and horse is a necessary concomitant of man, but one can conceive man without thinking of horse. Thus, it is evident but not mental.
This illustrates the need to know the concomitant itself.
4
Reflect on the poetic citation
The author cites al-Sullam's poem summarizing the division and glosses 'absolute concomitant' to clarify that it is not restricted to mental or external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: هل اللازم الذهني مرادف للازم البيِّن أم لا؟ قلت: في ذلك مذهبان: أحدهما وهو مذهب كثير من المناطقة: ترادفهما، وعليه درج السنوسي، وشيخنا سيدي الطيب، وهو ظاهر كلام الشَّارح هنا. ثانيهما وهو مذهب الأكثر: أنَّ البين أعمُّ مِنَ الذهني. فالبين هو: ما يلزم من العلم باللازم والملزوم معاً العلم به. وغيرُ البيِّن ما لا يكفي فيه ذلك، بل يحتاجُ إلى قرينةٍ منفصلة: لزوم الحدوث للأجرام؛ لأنَّه لا بدَّ مِن أمرٍ ثالث وهو التَّغير. ثمَّ البيِّن ينقسم إلى: ذهني وغيره فإن كفى في العلم به العلم بالملزوم؛ بمعنى: أنَّه كلَّما حصل العلم بالملزوم حصل العلم باللازم، وبلزومه للملزوم، فهو ذهني كـ: لزوم الزوجية للأربعة، والبصر للعمى، وإن لم يكف فيه العلم بالملزوم وحده، بل افتقر إلى العلم باللازم أيضاً ك: مغايرة الإنسان للفرس، فإنَّ مغايرة الإنسان للفرس أمرٌ لازم للإنسان، لكن من تصوّر «الإنسان» لا يلزمُهُ بمجرَّد ذلك أن يخطر بباله مغايرته للفرس، بل يتصوَّر «الإنسان» وهو غافل عن «الفرس» جملة، فكيف عن مغايرته إيَّاه، فهو بين غير ذهني، وهذه الطريقةُ أتمُّ فائدةً، وعلى الطريقة الأولى درج صاحب القادرية تبعاً لأصله، فقال: وَاللُّزُومُ يَنْقَسِم : بَينِ وَغَيْرِهِ، وَذَا إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ يَحْتَاجُ ، وَالبَيَّنُ لَا بَلْ هُوَ كُلَّمَا المُسَمَّى فُهِمَا فُهِمَ ذِهْناً مَا لَهُ قَدْ لَزِمَا قوله : ((اللَّازِمُ المُطْلَقُ» سمِّي : «مطلقاً؛ لعدم تقييد لزومه بالدهن ولا بالخارج.
For if you say: Is the mental concomitant synonymous with the evident concomitant or not? I say: There are two schools: One of them, which is the view of many logicians, is that they are synonymous, and al-Sanūsī and our master Sayyidī al-Ṭayyib proceeded upon it, and it is the apparent meaning of the commentator here. The second, which is the view of the majority, is that the evident is more general than the mental. So the evident is: what knowledge of it is necessitated by knowledge of the concomitant and the concomitant together. And what is non-evident is what is not sufficient by that, but rather requires a separate contextual clue: the implication of coming-to-be (ḥudūth) for bodies; because it needs a third thing, which is change. Then the evident divides into mental and other: if knowledge of it is sufficient by knowledge of the concomitant alone, meaning that whenever knowledge of the concomitant occurs, knowledge of the concomitant and its implication for the concomitant occurs, then it is mental, like the implication of evenness for four, and sight for blindness. But if knowledge of the concomitant alone is not sufficient, but rather also requires knowledge of the concomitant itself, like the difference of man from horse: for the difference of man from horse is a necessary concomitant of man, but whoever conceives 'man' does not, by that alone, necessarily bring to mind his difference from horse; rather he conceives 'man' while being completely unaware of 'horse', so how about his difference from it? So it is evident but not mental. And this method is more complete in benefit. And upon the first method proceeded the author of al-Qādiriyya, following his source, and he said: 'And implication divides into evident and other, and this requires an intermediary, while the evident is not rather it is that whenever the named entity is understood, what is its necessary concomitant is understood mentally.' His statement: 'the absolute concomitant' is called 'absolute' because its implication is not restricted to the mind nor to the external.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This classification passage addresses a key terminological distinction in Islamic logic: the relationship between 'mental concomitant' (al-lāzim al-dhihnī) and 'evident concomitant' (al-lāzim al-bayyin). The author begins with a question: Are they synonymous? Two schools are presented. The first, held by many logicians including al-Sanūsī and the commentator's teacher, asserts synonymy. The second, held by the majority, holds that 'evident' (al-bayyin) is more general than 'mental'. The author then defines the evident: it is that which is known by knowing the concomitant and its concomitant together. In contrast, the non-evident (ghayr al-bayyin) requires an external indicator, such as the implication of temporality (ḥudūth) for bodies, which requires a third factor (change) to be known. Next, the evident is subdivided: (1) mental (dhihnī) – knowing the concomitant alone suffices to know the concomitant and its implication, e.g., evenness for four or sight for blindness; (2) non-mental evident – knowing the concomitant alone does not suffice but requires also knowing the concomitant itself, e.g., the difference between man and horse is a necessary concomitant of man, but one may conceive man without thinking of horse, so it is not mental. The author notes that this second method is more beneficial. The passage concludes with a citation from the matn of al-Sullam (by al-Qādirī) summarizing the division, and a brief gloss on the term 'absolute concomitant' (al-lāzim al-muṭlaq) – so called because its implication is not restricted to mind or external.

Key Terms
اللازم الذهني
البين غير الذهني
اللازم المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Evident concomitants are either mental (known immediately from the correlate) or non-mental (require additional knowledge of the concomitant itself).

Connotation of a General Term: Mutabaqa and Tadammun
Author argues that a general term indicates all individuals by mutabaqa and each by tadammun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutabaqa and tadammun connotation of a general term and apply this distinction to universal propositions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A general term indicates the whole set by mutabaqa and each individual by tadammun.

In this passage, the author clarifies how a general term (عام) in a universal proposition indicates its individuals. Some thinkers mistakenly thought the term indicates each individual directly. The correct view is that the term signifies the entire collection directly (mutabaqa) and each individual as a part of that whole (tadammun). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analysing universal propositions in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of connotation
Mutabaqa is direct correspondence, where the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was placed. Tadammun is implication of a part within the whole: the word indicates a part of its meaning.
2
Understand the whole-part structure
The general term is a whole that encompasses all its individuals. Each individual is a part of that whole.
3
Apply to a universal proposition
In 'Every scholar is honored,' the term 'every scholar' indicates the set of all scholars by mutabaqa and each individual scholar (e.g., Zayd) by tadammun.
4
Refute the opposing view
The author refutes the claim that a general term indicates only by mutabaqa like particular propositions. It also indicates by tadammun because each individual is part of the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ تِلْكَ القَضَايَا تَدُلُّ مُطَابَقَةٌ، فَكَذَا مَا فِي قُوَّتِهَا، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ صَحِيح . وَالحَقُّ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ : أَنَّهَا تَضَمُّنْ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ العَامَّ مُسْتَغْرِقُ لِجَمِيعِ الْأَفْرَادِ، وَالفَرْدُ الوَاحِدُ بَعْضُ مِنَ الجَمِيعِ قَطعاً، فَيَكُونُ مَنْهُوماً فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ قطعاً وَقَوْلُهُمْ : «العَامُّ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُؤَوَّلٌ ; أَيْ : مَدْلُولُهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ الحُكْمِ عَلَيْهِ كُلِّيَّةٌ، أَوْ مَدْلُولُهُ مَعَ مَا نُسِبَ إِلَيْهِ قَضِيَّةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَإِنَّمَا العَامُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ كُلٌّ، وَإِنَّمَا الكُلِّيَّةُ القَضِيَّةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ، وَلَا مُنَافَاةَ بَيْنَ كَوْنِهِ كُلَّا ، وَكَوْنِ قَضِيَّتِهِ كُلِّيَّةٌ، إِذْ لَا مَانِعَ مِنْ تَوَجِهِ الحُكْمِ الإِيجَابِيٍّ أَوِ السَّلْبِيِّ إِلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ مِنَ الأَفْرَادِ المَدْلُولِ عَلَى جُمْلَتِهَا بِالعَامِّ مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ مِنْهَا تَضَمُّناً .
And every one of those particular propositions indicates by mutabaqa, so also what is in their strength, but this is not correct. And the truth according to the verifiers: that it indicates by tadammun necessarily because the general term is comprehensive of all individuals, and the single individual is definitely a part of the whole, so it is understood in the inclusion of the whole definitely. And their saying: 'The general is a universal' is interpreted; meaning its meaning from the aspect of the judgment upon it is universal, or its meaning with what is attributed to it is a universal proposition. And the general is only a whole from the aspect that it is a whole, and the universality is only the proposition that includes it. And there is no contradiction between its being a whole and its proposition being universal, since there is no prevention from the direction of the affirmative or negative judgment to every individual of the individuals whose totality is indicated by the general term by mutabaqa, and to every individual of them by tadammun.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage clarifies the type of connotation (dalala) of a general term (عام) used in a universal proposition. It refutes the claim that such a term indicates only in the same manner as particular propositions (mutabaqa). The correct view, according to the verifiers, is that the general term indicates the entire set of individuals by mutabaqa (direct correspondence) and each individual by tadammun (implication of part within whole). The general term is a whole, and the individuals are its parts. The author then explains the statement 'al-amm kulliyya' (the general is a universal) as requiring interpretation: the universality refers to the proposition containing the term, not the term itself. Hence, no contradiction exists between the term being a whole and its proposition being universal. For example, in 'Every scholar is honored,' the term 'every scholar' indicates the set of all scholars by mutabaqa and indicates each scholar (e.g., Zayd) by tadammun.

العام
المطابقة
التضمن

D) Because the term has multiple meanings.

Key Takeaway
A general term indicates the totality of its individuals by mutabaqa and each individual by tadammun, resolving the apparent contradiction between being a whole and being universal.

Distinguishing Universal Negation from Negation of Universality: Critique of a Hadith Example
Author critiques the example of 'kull' from the hadith and provides three reasons for its classification as 'kulliyya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal negation (umum al-salb) and negation of universality (salb al-umum) in Arabic logical propositions and apply this distinction to critique a hadith example.

Core Idea
When 'kull' is negated, it often means 'none are' not 'not all are'.

The author critiques a logician's example from a hadith, arguing that the Prophet's statement 'All of that did not happen' is a universal negation, not a negation of a composite whole. This lesson equips you to recognize the quantifier distinction that governs such cases, a key tool in evaluating logical examples from Islamic sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier phrase
Locate the phrase containing 'kull' and the negation. In the hadith: 'All of that did not happen' (كل ذلك لم يقع). Note the position of 'kull' relative to the negation.
2
Determine the logical type
In Arabic, when 'kull' precedes the negation, it usually indicates universal negation (umum al-salb): the predicate is negated for every individual. When the negation precedes 'kull' (like 'ليس كل'), it indicates negation of universality (salb al-umum): not all individuals have the predicate.
Example: 'كل حيوان ليس بحجر' vs. 'ليس كل حيوان بإنسان'
3
Apply the distinction to the hadith
The Prophet's statement 'كل ذلك لم يقع' is universal negation: each of the two possibilities (forgetfulness or shortening) did not occur. This is a universal negative judgment.
4
Understand the critique
The author argues the hadith exemplifies kulliyya (universal judgment), not kull (composite whole), because the negation applies to every individual part, not to the whole entity. The three reasons support this.
First reason: linguistic rule; second: nature of the question; third: Dhul-Yadain's reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا مُرَادُ المُصَنِّفِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ صَحِيحٍ، بَلِ الحَدِيثُ مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّيَّةِ ؛ أَي: الحُكْمِ عَلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ بِخُصُوصِهِ كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ السَّعْدُ وَغَيْرُهُ؛ لِوُجُوهٍ : (١) - أَحَدُهَا : أَنَّ كُلَّ إِذَا وَقَعَ فِي حَيْزِهَا نَفْيٍّ، كَانَتْ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ، لَا لِسَلْبِ العُمُومِ، هَذَا هُوَ المُقَرَّرُ فِي اللُّغَةِ، وَلِذَلِكَ عَدَّهُ المَنَاطِقَةُ مِنْ أَسْوَارِ الكُلِّيَّةِ الصَّالِبَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ»، بِخِلَافِ عَكْسِهِ؛ نَحْو: «لَيْسَ كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ بِإِنْسَانٍ» فَمِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْيَّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ . (۲) - الثَّانِي : أَنَّ السَّائِلَ بـ «أَمْ» وَ الهَمْزَةِ يَطْلُبُ تَعْيِينَ أَحَدِ أَمْرَيْنِ يَعْتَقِدُ ثُبُوتَ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَجَوَابُهُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ أَحَدِهِمَا ، أَوْ بِنَفْيِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا رَدًّا عَلَى السَّائِلِ، وَتَخْطِئَةً لَهُ فِي اعْتِقَادِهِ ثُبُوتَ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَلَا يَصِحُّ بِنَفْيِ الجَمْعِ بَيْنَهُمَا ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّائِلَ لَمْ يَعْتَقِدِ الجَمْعَ بَيْنَهُمَا، حَتَّى يُجَابَ بِنَفْيهِ . (۳) - وَالثَّالِثُ : مَا رُوِيَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ»، قَالَ لَهُ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : «بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ»، فَلَوْ كَانَ قَوْلُهُ : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّ [؟]
This is what the author intended, but it is not correct. Rather, the hadith belongs to the category of universality (kulliyya), i.e., the judgment on every individual specifically, as verified by al-Sa'd and others. This is for several reasons: (1) One of them: that when 'kull' falls in the scope of negation, it is for the universality of negation (umum al-salb), not the negation of universality (salb al-umum). This is what is established in language, and therefore the logicians count it among the negative universal quantifiers. For example: 'Every animal is not a stone', unlike its converse, such as: 'Not every animal is a human', which is among the negative particular quantifiers. (2) The second: that the questioner using 'am' and the interrogative hamzah seeks to specify one of two things, believing one of them to be established. His answer can only be by specifying one of them, or by negating both as a refutation of the questioner and an error in his belief that one of them is established. It is not valid to negate the combination of the two because the questioner did not believe in the combination of the two such that one could answer by negating it. (3) The third: what is reported that when the Prophet ﷺ said: 'All of that did not happen', Dhul-Yadain said to him: 'Rather, some of that has occurred.' So if his statement 'All of that did not happen' belonged to the category of the whole (kull)...
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a super-commentator (likely al-Bannani) correcting the author (al-Akhdari in al-Sullam) who gave an example of 'kull' (whole) from a hadith. The super-commentator argues that the hadith is actually an example of 'kulliyya' (universality), not 'kull' (whole). The distinction is crucial: 'kull' refers to a composite whole (like a group of parts), while 'kulliyya' refers to a universal concept applied to every individual. The author provides three reasons. First, linguistically, when 'kull' is in a negative context, it indicates universal negation (i.e., the negation applies to every individual), not negation of the whole. For example, 'Every animal is not a stone' means no animal is a stone, which is a universal negative. In contrast, 'Not every animal is a human' is a particular negative, because it negates the universality. The Prophet's statement 'All of that did not happen' (in response to Dhul-Yadain's question whether the prayer was shortened or forgotten) is like the universal negative: it means each of those two possibilities did not occur individually. Second, the form of Dhul-Yadain's question used 'am' (or) and the interrogative hamzah, which seeks specification of one of two mutually exclusive options. The answer must either specify one or negate both; it cannot simply negate the combination because the questioner did not assume both. The Prophet's answer 'All of that did not happen' negates both individually, which fits universal negation. Third, Dhul-Yadain's immediate response 'Rather, some of that has occurred' shows that he understood the Prophet's statement as a universal negation (each did not happen), not as a negation of the whole, because if it were the latter, his objection would not make sense—he would have accepted that the whole did not occur but some part did. This third reason is cut off in the text but the argument is clear: the hadith exemplifies kulliyya, not kull.

Key Terms
الكل
الكليّة
السلب
عموم السلب
سلب العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first reason given, what does the statement 'Every animal is not a stone' (كل حيوان ليس بحجر) exemplify?
A) Universal negation (umum al-salb)
B) Negation of universality (salb al-umum)
C) Composite whole negation
D) Particular negation

Key Takeaway
When 'kull' precedes a negation, it indicates universal negation (none are), not negation of universality (not all are).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether 'ليس كل عالم بفاضل' (Not every scholar is virtuous) is universal negation or negation of universality, and explain the logical difference.
?
Analyze the following hadith: 'كل ابن آدم خطّاء' (Every son of Adam errs) – is this universal affirmation or something else? How would negation affect this statement if the Prophet had said 'ليس كل ابن آدم بخطّاء' (Not every son of Adam is an erring person)?

The Universal Negative Quantifier in Logical Propositions
Explaining the negative particular quantifier with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a negative particular proposition (juz'iyya sāliba) and distinguish it from universal negatives and affirmatives using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The quantifier 'not every' signals a particular negative, not a universal.

In Islamic logic, every proposition has quantity and quality. The source passage gives an example: 'Not every animal is a man' (ليس كل حيوان بإنسان). This might seem like a universal negative, but it is actually a particular negative. This lesson will clarify that distinction and show why it matters for understanding hadith and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terms
The passage introduces 'جزئية سالبة' (negative particular). Recall that 'kull' means 'all/every'. When negated, 'laysa kullu' means 'not every', which is equivalent to 'some...not' — a particular negative. A universal negative would be 'no' or 'not a single' (e.g., 'no animal is a man').
2
Analyze the example
Take the sentence 'ليس كل حيوان بإنسان'. Identify the quantifier 'كُلّ' and the negation 'لَيْسَ'. The resulting proposition is 'some animals are not men' — a particular negative. A universal negative would be 'لا حيوان بإنسان' (no animal is a man).
3
Apply to Islamic text
Compare with a Qur'anic verse: 'إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ بِهِ' (Allah does not forgive shirk) — that is a universal negative (no shirk is forgiven). Contrast with 'لَيْسَ كُلُّ مَشْرِكٍ مُخَلَّدٌ فِي النَّارِ' — that would be a particular negative (not every polytheist is eternally punished).

Source Text

نَحْو: «لَيْسَ كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ بِإِنْسَانٍ» فَمِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (۲) - الثَّانِي : أَنَّ السَّائِلَ بـ «أَمْ» وَ الهَمْزَةِ يَطْلُبُ تَعْيِينَ أَحَدٍ أَمْرَيْنِ يَعْتَقِدُ ثُبُوتَ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَجَوَابُهُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ أَحَدِهِمَا، أَوْ بِنَفْيِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا رَدًّا عَلَى السَّائِلِ، وَتَخْطِئَةً لَهُ فِي اعْتِقَادِهِ ثُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَلَا يَصِحُ بِنَفْيِ الجَمْعِ بَيْنَهُمَا ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّائِلَ لَمْ يَعْتَقِدِ الجَمْعَ بَيْنَهُمَا ، حَتَّى يُجَابَ بِنَفْيهِ . (۳) - وَالثَّالِثُ : مَا رُوِيَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ»، قَالَ لَهُ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : «بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ»، فَلَوْ كَانَ قَوْلُهُ : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّ؛ لَمَا حَسُنَ مِنْ ذِي اليَدَيْنِ أَنْ يَقُولَ: «بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ». (٤) - الرَّابِعُ : أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : «بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ»، قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلشَّيْخَيْنِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمَا : «أَحَقُّ مَا يَقُولُ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ؟»، فَقَالَا: «نَعَمْ»، فَلَوْ كَانَ قَصْدُهُ بِقَوْلِهِ أَولاً : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» ثُبُوتَ البَعْضِ مَا سَأَلَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ آخِراً . فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : حَيْثُ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ «كُلَّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» كُلِّيَّةٌ، لَا كُلُّ، فَمَا وَجْهُ صِدْقِهَا ، وَالفَرَضُ أَنَّهُ قَدْ وَقَعَ أَحَدُهُمَا، وَهُوَ النِّسْيَانُ؟ أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ: «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ فِي ظَنِّي»؛ بِقَرِينَةِ المُرَاجَعَةِ وَسُؤالِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلشَّيْخَيْنِ آخِراً.
For example: 'Not every animal is a man' is from the quantities of the negative particular. (2) The second: The questioner with 'am' and the hamzah seeks the determination of one of two things, believing in the existence of one of them. His answer is only by determining one of them, or by negating each of them as a refutation of the questioner and a correction of his belief in the existence of one of them. And it is not valid by negating the conjunction of both, because the questioner did not believe in the conjunction of both, so that he would be answered by its negation. (3) The third: What is narrated that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'All of that did not happen,' Dhu al-Yadayn said to him: 'Rather, some of that did happen.' So if his statement 'All of that did not happen' were from the door of al-kull [the universal], it would not be proper for Dhu al-Yadayn to say 'Some of that did happen.' (4) The fourth: When Dhu al-Yadayn said: 'Some of that did happen,' the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to the two shaykhs (may Allah be pleased with them): 'Is what Dhu al-Yadayn says true?' They said: 'Yes.' So if his intent in his first statement 'All of that did not happen' was the affirmation of the part, he would not have asked about that later. If you say: Since it became clear that 'All of that did not happen' is a universal negative, not a universal affirmative, then what is the aspect of its truth, given the assumption that one of the two things did occur, namely forgetfulness? The answer: That the intended meaning is: 'All of that did not happen in my estimation,' by the evidence of the consultation and his (peace be upon him) question to the two shaykhs later.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that defines and distinguishes between different types of universal and particular quantifiers in logic, using examples and a famous hadith to illustrate the subtlety. The author begins by giving an example of a negative particular proposition: 'Not every animal is a man' (laysa kullu hayawanin bi-insanin). This is classified as one of the quantities of the negative particular (السَّالِبَة الجُزْئِيَّة). The point is that the quantifier 'kull' (every) when preceded by negation does not mean 'all' in the universal affirmative sense, but rather 'not every', which is a particular negative (some are not). The commentator then, in point (2), discusses the rule for answering questions posed with 'am' (or) or the hamzah (interrogative 'a-') that seek determination of one of two mutually exclusive options. The answer must either specify one of them or negate both individually, not negate the conjunction, because the questioner did not assume both together. This is a logical-linguistic rule. In point (3), the commentator invokes the hadith where the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' (kullu dhalika lam yaqa') and Dhu al-Yadayn objected that some of it did happen. If the Prophet's statement were a universal affirmative (i.e., 'all of that did not happen' meaning each individual thing), then Dhu al-Yadayn's objection would be inappropriate because a universal affirmative would be contradicted by a particular negative; but the fact that Dhu al-Yadayn felt able to object shows that the Prophet's statement was not a universal affirmative but rather a universal negative? Actually, the text says: 'fa-law kana qawluhu ... min babi al-kulli' – if it were from the door of al-kull (meaning the universal affirmative?), then it would not be proper for Dhu al-Yadayn to say 'some of that did happen'. The logic is that a universal affirmative (kullu dhalika lam yaqa'? Actually 'kullu dhalika lam yaqa' is a universal negative because it negates an affirmative: 'all of that did not occur' is a universal negative proposition (كلية سالبة). But the commentator is distinguishing between 'kulliyya' (universal) and 'kull' (the word 'kull' used as a universal quantifier). The nuance is that the Prophet's statement is not a universal affirmative (kulliyya mūjiba) but rather a universal negative (kulliyya sāliba) which is logically equivalent to 'no part of that occurred' – that would be contradicted by the occurrence of some part. So Dhu al-Yadayn's statement 'some of that did occur' would be a direct contradiction. The fact that Dhu al-Yadayn did not see a contradiction indicates that the Prophet's statement was understood as a negation of the totality, not a negation of each individual. The commentator then adds point (4): when Dhu al-Yadayn said 'some of that did happen', the Prophet asked the two shaykhs if Dhu al-Yadayn was right, and they affirmed. If the Prophet's initial statement had meant to affirm the occurrence of the part (i.e., he meant 'not all of that occurred' but some occurred), then he would not have needed to ask, because it would be consistent. The objection is then raised: If the statement is a universal negative (kulliyya sāliba), then it is false because one part (forgetfulness) did occur. The answer is that the intended meaning is 'all of that did not occur in my estimation' (fi zanni), i.e., it is about the Prophet's knowledge, not objective reality. The evidence is that he later consulted the two shaykhs to confirm. Thus the universal negative is true with respect to his immediate knowledge, and the objection is resolved. The commentary thus clarifies the logical difference between universal affirmative and negative, and the role of context (qarīna) in interpreting quantifiers.

Key Terms

كُلِّيَّة
جُزْئِيَّة سَالِبَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Not every animal is a man.
C) Every animal is a man.
D) Some animals are men.

Key Takeaway
The negative quantifier 'not every' yields a particular negative proposition, not a universal negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Logical Rule for Answering Disjunctive Questions
Rule for answering disjunctive questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for answering disjunctive questions (with 'am' or hamzah) in Islamic scholarly debate, distinguishing between negating each individually and negating the conjunction.

Core Idea
When asked 'Is it A or B?', answer by choosing one or denying both, never by denying the pair.

The passage explains a crucial rule for responding to questions that present two alternatives, such as 'Is this act wajib or mubah?' The questioner assumes one is true. The answerer must either specify which one, or negate both individually to correct the assumption. Negating the conjunction (i.e., saying 'not both') is invalid because the questioner did not assume both together. This logical-linguistic rule is essential in Islamic disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question type
Recognize when a question uses the interrogative hamzah (أ) or the disjunctive 'am' (أم). For example, 'أزيد في الدار أم عمرو؟' (Is Zayd in the house or Amr?).
2
Recall the rule from the passage
The answer is only by determining one of the two (تعيين أحدهما), or by negating each of them (نفي كل منهما). Negating the conjunction (نفي الجمع بينهما) is invalid because the questioner did not believe in the conjunction.
3
Apply to a fiqh example
Suppose someone asks: 'أهذا الفعل واجب أم مكروه؟' (Is this act obligatory or disliked?). The correct answer is either 'It is wajib' or 'It is makruh' if one applies, or 'It is neither wajib nor makruh' if both are false. Answering 'It is not both' would be incorrect because the questioner assumed one is true, not both.
4
Reflect on the logical rationale
The rule prevents a non-answer. The question seeks a specific determination, so the answer must either affirm one or deny all options individually to show the assumption is wrong.

Resolving the Hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn: Universal Negative vs. Contextual Knowledge
Analysis of the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Prophet's statement 'All of that did not happen' as a universal negative in terms of knowledge, using logical quantifier theory and the evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
The Prophet's 'all did not happen' is a universal negative about his knowledge, not a false universal affirmative.

The hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn presents a seeming contradiction: the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' but Dhu al-Yadayn replied 'Some of that did happen'. The commentary resolves this by showing that the Prophet's statement was a universal negative proposition (كُلِّيَّة سَالِبَة) qualified by 'in my estimation' (في ظني). This lesson unpacks the logical analysis of the hadith and shows how context (qarīna) disambiguates quantifiers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two statements
The Prophet says: 'كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ' (All of that did not happen). Dhu al-Yadayn says: 'بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ' (Some of that did happen). Determine the logical form of each.
2
Analyze the Prophet's statement
The phrase 'كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ' is a universal negative: no part of that occurred. If taken literally, it contradicts Dhu al-Yadayn's particular affirmative. But Dhu al-Yadayn's objection shows he understood it differently.
3
Understand Dhu al-Yadayn's response
Dhu al-Yadayn says 'Some of that did happen', which is a particular affirmative. This would be a direct contradiction of a universal negative. The fact that he objected indicates the Prophet's statement was not a universal negative about reality.
4
Consider the commentator's explanation
The commentator says: if the Prophet's statement were a universal (كُلّ) in the sense of universal affirmative, then Dhu al-Yadayn's remark would not be appropriate. But the passage distinguishes between 'kull' (the word) and 'kulliyya' (universality). The Prophet's statement is not a universal affirmative but rather a universal negative. However, even a universal negative would be contradicted by a particular affirmative. So the resolution is that the Prophet meant 'in my estimation'.
5
Resolve with the evidence of consultation
The Prophet later asked the two shaykhs to confirm, which shows his statement was about his immediate knowledge, not objective reality. Thus, the universal negative is true with respect to his knowledge, and the objection is resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَحْو: «لَيْسَ كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ بِإِنْسَانٍ» فَمِنْ أَسْوَارِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (۲) - الثَّانِي : أَنَّ السَّائِلَ بـ «أَمْ» وَ الهَمْزَةِ يَطْلُبُ تَعْيِينَ أَحَدٍ أَمْرَيْنِ يَعْتَقِدُ ثُبُوتَ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَجَوَابُهُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ أَحَدِهِمَا، أَوْ بِنَفْيِ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا رَدًّا عَلَى السَّائِلِ، وَتَخْطِئَةً لَهُ فِي اعْتِقَادِهِ ثُبُوتِ أَحَدِهِمَا ، وَلَا يَصِحُ بِنَفْيِ الجَمْعِ بَيْنَهُمَا ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّائِلَ لَمْ يَعْتَقِدِ الجَمْعَ بَيْنَهُمَا ، حَتَّى يُجَابَ بِنَفْيهِ . (۳) - وَالثَّالِثُ : مَا رُوِيَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ»، قَالَ لَهُ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : «بَلْ بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ»، فَلَوْ كَانَ قَوْلُهُ : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» مِنْ بَابِ الكُلِّ؛ لَمَا حَسُنَ مِنْ ذِي اليَدَيْنِ أَنْ يَقُولَ: «بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ». (٤) - الرَّابِعُ : أَنَّهُ لَمَّا قَالَ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ : «بَعْضُ ذَلِكَ قَدْ كَانَ»، قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلشَّيْخَيْنِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمَا : «أَحَقُّ مَا يَقُولُ ذُو اليَدَيْنِ؟»، فَقَالَا: «نَعَمْ»، فَلَوْ كَانَ قَصْدُهُ بِقَوْلِهِ أَولاً : «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» ثُبُوتَ البَعْضِ مَا سَأَلَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ آخِراً . فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : حَيْثُ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ «كُلَّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ» كُلِّيَّةٌ، لَا كُلُّ، فَمَا وَجْهُ صِدْقِهَا ، وَالفَرَضُ أَنَّهُ قَدْ وَقَعَ أَحَدُهُمَا، وَهُوَ النِّسْيَانُ؟ أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ: «كُلُّ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَقَعْ فِي ظَنِّي»؛ بِقَرِينَةِ المُرَاجَعَةِ وَسُؤالِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِلشَّيْخَيْنِ آخِراً.
For example: 'Not every animal is a man' is from the quantities of the negative particular. (2) The second: The questioner with 'am' and the hamzah seeks the determination of one of two things, believing in the existence of one of them. His answer is only by determining one of them, or by negating each of them as a refutation of the questioner and a correction of his belief in the existence of one of them. And it is not valid by negating the conjunction of both, because the questioner did not believe in the conjunction of both, so that he would be answered by its negation. (3) The third: What is narrated that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'All of that did not happen,' Dhu al-Yadayn said to him: 'Rather, some of that did happen.' So if his statement 'All of that did not happen' were from the door of al-kull [the universal], it would not be proper for Dhu al-Yadayn to say 'Some of that did happen.' (4) The fourth: When Dhu al-Yadayn said: 'Some of that did happen,' the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to the two shaykhs (may Allah be pleased with them): 'Is what Dhu al-Yadayn says true?' They said: 'Yes.' So if his intent in his first statement 'All of that did not happen' was the affirmation of the part, he would not have asked about that later. If you say: Since it became clear that 'All of that did not happen' is a universal negative, not a universal affirmative, then what is the aspect of its truth, given the assumption that one of the two things did occur, namely forgetfulness? The answer: That the intended meaning is: 'All of that did not happen in my estimation,' by the evidence of the consultation and his (peace be upon him) question to the two shaykhs later.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that defines and distinguishes between different types of universal and particular quantifiers in logic, using examples and a famous hadith to illustrate the subtlety. The author begins by giving an example of a negative particular proposition: 'Not every animal is a man' (laysa kullu hayawanin bi-insanin). This is classified as one of the quantities of the negative particular (السَّالِبَة الجُزْئِيَّة). The point is that the quantifier 'kull' (every) when preceded by negation does not mean 'all' in the universal affirmative sense, but rather 'not every', which is a particular negative (some are not). The commentator then, in point (2), discusses the rule for answering questions posed with 'am' (or) or the hamzah (interrogative 'a-') that seek determination of one of two mutually exclusive options. The answer must either specify one of them or negate both individually, not negate the conjunction, because the questioner did not assume both together. This is a logical-linguistic rule. In point (3), the commentator invokes the hadith where the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' (kullu dhalika lam yaqa') and Dhu al-Yadayn objected that some of it did happen. If the Prophet's statement were a universal affirmative (i.e., 'all of that did not happen' meaning each individual thing), then Dhu al-Yadayn's objection would be inappropriate because a universal affirmative would be contradicted by a particular negative; but the fact that Dhu al-Yadayn felt able to object shows that the Prophet's statement was not a universal affirmative but rather a universal negative? Actually, the text says: 'fa-law kana qawluhu ... min babi al-kulli' – if it were from the door of al-kull (meaning the universal affirmative?), then it would not be proper for Dhu al-Yadayn to say 'some of that did happen'. The logic is that a universal affirmative (kullu dhalika lam yaqa'? Actually 'kullu dhalika lam yaqa' is a universal negative because it negates an affirmative: 'all of that did not occur' is a universal negative proposition (كلية سالبة). But the commentator is distinguishing between 'kulliyya' (universal) and 'kull' (the word 'kull' used as a universal quantifier). The nuance is that the Prophet's statement is not a universal affirmative (kulliyya mūjiba) but rather a universal negative (kulliyya sāliba) which is logically equivalent to 'no part of that occurred' – that would be contradicted by the occurrence of some part. So Dhu al-Yadayn's statement 'some of that did occur' would be a direct contradiction. The fact that Dhu al-Yadayn did not see a contradiction indicates that the Prophet's statement was understood as a negation of the totality, not a negation of each individual. The commentator then adds point (4): when Dhu al-Yadayn said 'some of that did happen', the Prophet asked the two shaykhs if Dhu al-Yadayn was right, and they affirmed. If the Prophet's initial statement had meant to affirm the occurrence of the part (i.e., he meant 'not all of that occurred' but some occurred), then he would not have needed to ask, because it would be consistent. The objection is then raised: If the statement is a universal negative (kulliyya sāliba), then it is false because one part (forgetfulness) did occur. The answer is that the intended meaning is 'all of that did not occur in my estimation' (fi zanni), i.e., it is about the Prophet's knowledge, not objective reality. The evidence is that he later consulted the two shaykhs to confirm. Thus the universal negative is true with respect to his immediate knowledge, and the objection is resolved. The commentary thus clarifies the logical difference between universal affirmative and negative, and the role of context (qarīna) in interpreting quantifiers.

Key Terms
كُلّ
كُلِّيَّة
جُزْئِيَّة
ظَنّ
قَرِينَة

The truth of a universal negative proposition can be relative to the speaker's knowledge, as shown by the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn.

A student objects: 'بل بعضها قد اختلفوا فيه' (Rather, some of them they did differ). The Logical Distinction Between 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس'
Objection and response regarding the context of negation in quantifier phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate over the quantifier phrases 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' and identify the correct distinction based on negation context and affirmative aduli usage.

Core Idea
Context of negation transforms indefinite nouns; pronoun definiteness blocks an objection.

This passage presents a dialectical refinement of a distinction between two quantifier phrases. The initial claim is that 'شبيه بالنكرة' (similar to an indefinite) in the context of negation generalizes, unlike 'بعض ليس'. An objection is raised concerning the pronoun, and the response clarifies the definiteness of the pronoun and introduces a deeper distinction regarding affirmative aduli predication. You will learn how logical distinctions are sharpened through debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial distinction
The passage starts: 'شَبِيهُ بِالنَّكِرَةِ، وَالنَّكِرَةُ فِي سِيَاقِ النَّفْيِ تَعُمُّ، بِخِلَافِ الثَّانِي...' What is the difference between the two phrases? Note that the second phrase ('بعض ليس') is not in the negation context.
The first phrase 'ليس بعض' is similar to an indefinite noun in negation context, hence it generalizes.
2
Follow the objection by Ibn Mansur
Ibn Mansur objects that the pronoun 'هو' in 'بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر' is in the negation context, thus the distinction collapses because the pronoun refers back to 'بعض'.
The objection relies on the pronoun being indefinite and generalizing in negation context.
3
Examine the response on pronoun definiteness
The response argues that the pronoun is definite, not indefinite, so it does not generalize. Therefore, the objection fails.
The truth is that the pronoun is definite, so it cannot be generalized in negation context.
4
Understand the additional distinction: affirmative aduli
The passage then provides a substantive difference: 'بعض ليس' is used in affirmative aduli (with the copula and negation after the predicate), while 'ليس بعض' has negation prefixed and cannot be used that way.
Affirmative aduli: e.g., 'بعض الحيوان هو ليس بإنسان' – negation follows the copula.
5
Assess the outcome
Determine whether the original distinction survives the objection and is strengthened by the response. The response refutes the objection and adds a deeper logical distinction.
The original distinction is upheld and refined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شَبِيهُ بِالنَّكِرَةِ، وَالنَّكِرَةُ فِي سِيَاقِ النَّفْيِ تَعُمُّ، بِخِلَافِ الثَّانِي، فَإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي سِيَاقِهِ. قوله : (فَإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي سِيَاقِهِ) هذا الفرق سلَّمه الشَّيخ الطَّيِّب، وكذا الهلالي، واعترضه شيخنا ابن منصور : بأنَّ الضَّمير العائد عليه واقع في سياقه : «بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر»، ولا فرق بين الضمير ومعاده . وهذا الاعتراضُ إِنَّما يتمشَّى على القول بأنَّ الضَّمير هنا نكرة، وفيه خلاف ـ أي : في ضمير النكرة ـ ؛ والحقُ : أنَّه معرفةٌ . وأيضاً : الضَّمير تابع لمعاده في عمومه وخصوصه؛ إذ لا يتجاوز مفسّره، ومفسره شيءٌ محصورٌ لا يمكن التعميمُ فيه، وما استدلَّ به لا يثبت مدَّعاه على تقدير بطلان هذا الفرق، فيقال: الفرق أنَّ «بعض ليس» يستعمل في الإيجاب العدوليّ ؛ بأن تذكر الرابطة أو تنوى بعده، وقبل السَّلب؛ نحو: «بعض الحيوان هو ليس بإنسان»، بخلاف «ليس بعض»؛ لتقدم السلب لتقدُّم السَّلب فيه لفظاً ، فلا يُستعمل في الإيجاب العدولي.
Similar to a non-specific noun (nakira), and the non-specific noun in the context of negation is general, unlike the second, for it is not in its context. His statement: 'for it is not in its context' — this distinction was accepted by al-Shaykh al-Tayyib and al-Hilali, but our teacher Ibn Mansur objected to it: that the pronoun referring back to it occurs in its context: 'some humans are not stones' — and there is no difference between the pronoun and its referent. This objection only proceeds on the view that the pronoun here is indefinite, and there is disagreement — i.e., regarding the pronoun of the indefinite noun; and the truth is that it is definite. Also: the pronoun follows its referent in generality and specificity; since it does not exceed its explicative, and its explicative is a limited thing in which generalization is not possible. And what he adduced does not prove his claim on the assumption of the invalidity of this distinction. So it is said: the distinction is that 'some not' is used in affirmative aduli (justificatory) predication — by mentioning the copula or intending it after it, before the negation — e.g., 'some animals are not human' — unlike 'not some', because the negation precedes it in wording, so it is not used in affirmative aduli.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical critique (naqd) of a distinction made by the commentator regarding quantifiers. The initial point was that 'شبيه بالنكرة' (similar to an indefinite noun) when in the context of negation (like 'ليس بعض') it generalizes, unlike the second case ('بعض ليس') which is not in that context. The commentator accepts this distinction but an objection is raised by Ibn Mansur: the pronoun 'هو' in 'بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر' refers back to 'بعض', and it is in the context of negation, so there is no difference. The response argues that the pronoun is definite, not indefinite, and thus the objection fails. The final resolution provides a substantive difference: 'بعض ليس' can be used for affirmative aduli (where negation is postponed), while 'ليس بعض' has negation prefixed and cannot be used that way. The lesson thus refines the logical distinction through a careful analysis of pronouns and negation context.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun that, when in the context of negation, becomes general (تعميم). Here it contrasts with 'شبيه بالنكرة'. [Note: Standard usage in logic; no significant shift.]
سياق النفي (siyaq al-nafy): The syntactic context of negation that makes an indefinite noun general. It is the key to distinguishing the two quantifier phrases. [Note: Standard logical principle; no shift.]
الإيجاب العدولي (al-ijab al-'aduli): Affirmative predication that includes a negation in the predicate, equivalent to a negative copula construction (e.g., 'هو ليس'). It is distinct from simple negation. [Note: Stable term in logic, referring to 'affirmative of the negative' or justificatory affirmation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) بعض ليس
C) لا بعض

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' turns on the context of negation and its interaction with indefinite nouns, as refined by the definiteness of the pronoun and the possibility of affirmative aduli usage.

Distinguishing Exclusive Disjunctions by the Last Disjunct
Author explains how to distinguish exclusive from non-exclusive disjunctions by the last disjunct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a disjunctive proposition is exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya) or merely preventive of union (māni'at al-jam') by analyzing the exhaustiveness of its division, focusing on the last disjunct.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunction requires an exhaustive division; the last disjunct reveals whether it is exhaustive.

In logical divisions, not all disjunctions are truly exclusive. The author presents a practical test: examine the last disjunct in the chain. If the initial division covers all possibilities, the disjunction is exclusive; if not, it is merely a prevention of union. This lesson shows how to apply that test using examples from Arabic grammar and animal classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of disjunctions
Look for the series of disjunctive propositions. For example, 'The word is either a noun or other than it, and other than it is either a verb or a particle.' Note the first disjunction (noun vs. other) and the second (verb vs. particle).
2
Check exhaustiveness of the first division
Determine if the first disjunct pair covers all possibilities of the broader category. In the word example, 'noun' and 'other' exhaustively divide 'word' because any word is either a noun or not a noun.
An exhaustive division is also called qisma ḥāṣira.
3
Examine the last disjunct's exhaustiveness
Look at the final disjunction in the chain. In the word example, 'verb' and 'particle' exhaustively divide 'other' because the parts of speech in Arabic are only noun, verb, and particle. So this last disjunct is an exclusive disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya).
If the division is not exhaustive, the last disjunct is māni'at al-jam'.
4
Practice with the animal example
Consider 'The animal is either human or other, and other is either horse or bird.' The first division is exhaustive (human vs. non-human). However, the second division (horse or bird) does not exhaust 'other' because there are many other animals. Therefore, the last disjunct is a prevention of union (māni'at al-jam').
The same logic applies to māni'at al-khuluw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : عَلَى تَقْدِيرِهِ لَا يَبْقَى فَرْقُ بَيْنَ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى حَقِيقِيَّاتٍ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَوَانِعَ جَمْعِ أَوْ خُلُوِّ. قُلْتُ : الفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا يَتَبَيَّنُ بِالأَخِيرَةِ مِنَ القَضَايَا ، فَإِنَّهَا إِذَا كَانَتِ القِسْمَةُ حَاصِرَةٌ تَكُونُ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ؛ نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فِعْلٌ أَوْ حَرْفٌ»، فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ غَيْرَ حَاصِرَةٍ كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ الثَّانِي تَكُونُ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْع؛ لِأَنَّ الأَصْلَ: «الحَيَوَانُ : إِمَّا إِنْسَانُ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فَرَسٌ أَوْ طَائِرٌ، فَهَذِهِ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْعٍ
If you say: on his assumption, no distinction remains between what contains exclusive disjunctions and what contains preventions of union or of emptiness. I say: the distinction between them is shown by the last of the propositions: if the division is exhaustive, it is exclusive, like 'the word: either a noun or other than it, and other than it: either a verb or a particle' – each of them is exclusive. If it is not exhaustive, as in the second example, the last is a prevention of union, because the original [division] is: 'the animal: either human or other than it, and other than it: either a horse or a bird' – so this last is a prevention of union.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (if you say / I say) clarifying the distinction between exclusive disjunctions (ḥaqīqiyya) and non-exclusive ones (māni'at al-jam', māni'at al-khuluw). The basis is the nature of the last disjunct in a chain of divisions. If the initial division is exhaustive (covering all possibilities), then each disjunct is a true exclusive, as in the example dividing 'word' into 'noun' and 'other' then further dividing 'other' into 'verb' and 'particle' – here each disjunct is mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive. If the division is not exhaustive, like dividing 'animal' into 'human' and 'other', then subdividing 'other' into 'horse' and 'bird' – the last disjunct (horse or bird) is not exhaustive of 'other' (there are other animals), so it is a prevention of union (māni'at al-jam'), meaning it only prevents the conjunction of its own parts but does not cover all possibilities. The same logic applies to prevention of emptiness.

Key Terms
حقيقية
مانعة جمع
قسمة حاصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'The animal: either human or other than it, and other than it: either horse or bird', why is the last disjunction a prevention of union (māni'at al-jam')?
A) Because it contains only two options.
B) Because the division is not exhaustive; there are other animals besides horse and bird.
C) Because it is the last in the chain.
D) Because it includes 'other than it' which is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The exhaustiveness of the division determines whether a disjunction is exclusive; the last disjunct is the practical indicator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the division: 'The created thing is either a substance or an accident.' Is this an exclusive disjunction? Why? (Answer: Yes, because the division of created things into substance and accident is exhaustive in kalam.)
?
Now consider: 'The animal is either a horse or a bird.' Is this an exclusive disjunction? Why? (Answer: No, because the division of animal into horse and bird is not exhaustive; there are other animals.)

Truth of Conditionals: Relationship Over Parts
Author defines truth conditions for conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth of a conditional proposition by assessing the claimed relationship (companionship or opposition) rather than the truth values of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional's truth is about the relationship, not about the parts' individual truth.

This passage clarifies a common misconception: a conditional proposition is not evaluated by the truth of its parts. Instead, its truth hinges on whether the claimed connection or opposition between the parts holds in reality. Using examples like 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' and a Quranic verse, the author demonstrates that even impossible parts can yield a true conditional if the entailment is valid. Grasping this principle is essential for analyzing logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the essence of a conditional
The author states that the purpose of a conditional is the judgement of companionship (ṣuḥba) or opposition (tanāfur) between two parts, or their negation. So a conditional itself asserts a relationship, not the independent truth of its components.
Companionship means the parts coexist; opposition means they cannot coexist.
2
Define truth and falsity for conditionals
Truth corresponds to the judgement of relationship matching reality. Falsity is when the claimed relationship does not hold. The truth of the parts is irrelevant.
3
Apply to the example: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate'
Check the entailment: does stoneness necessarily imply inanimateness? Yes. So the conditional is true, despite both parts being impossible (Zayd is neither stone nor inanimate).
The relationship of entailment (luzūm) is real and necessary.
4
Recognize the Quranic example
The verse 'If there were gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' asserts an opposition: multiple gods lead to disorder. The conditional is true because the disconnection holds logically, even if the hypothesis is impossible.
This shows the same principle in a revelatory context.
5
Understand the qualification 'in the origin'
The parts are considered for truth only outside the proposition. Within the conditional, they are not described as true or false because they are components of a proposition, not complete propositions themselves.
This prevents confusion about evaluating parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي : قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ هُوَ الحُكْمُ بِمَا تَضَمَّنَتْهُ مِنْ صُحْبَةٍ أَوْ تَنَافُرٍ بِأَنْوَاعِهِ ، أَوْ سَلْبِهِمَا ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَصِدْقُهَا هُوَ مُطَابَقَةُ حُكْمُهُمَا المَذْكُورُ للواقع، وَكَذِبُهَا عَدَمُهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارٍ لِكَوْنِ طَرَفِيهَا فِي أَصْلِهِمَا صَادِقَيْنِ أَوْ كَاذِبَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ، فَتَصْدُقُ مَعَ كَذِبِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي الأَصْلِ؛ نَحْرُ: لَوْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حَجَراً لَكَانَ جَمَاداً»، فَهَذِهِ صَادِقَةٌ لِصِدْقِ لُزُومِ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ لِحَجَرِيَّتِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ وَحَجَرِيَّتِهِ مُسْتَحِيلَتَيْنِ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : «وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَنَا» [الأنبياء: ٢٢]. وَإِنَّمَا قَيَّدْنَا بِـ «الأَصْلِ» ؛ لِأَنَّ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يُوْصَفَانِ بِالكَذِبِ حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا فِي القَضِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّ جُزْءَ القَضِيَّةِ لَا يُوْصَفُ بِصِدْقٍ وَلَا كَذِبٍ، افْهَمْ
Second: It is known that the purpose of the conditional is the judgement containing companionship or opposition in its types, or the negation of both. Then its truth is the correspondence of its mentioned judgement to reality, and its falsity is its absence, without consideration of whether its two parts in their origin are true or false or different. So it is true with the falsity of both parts in the origin, as in: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' – this is true because of the truth of the entailment of his inanimateness by his stoneness, even if his inanimateness and stoneness are impossible. And from that is His saying: 'And if there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' [Quran 21:22]. We only qualified with 'origin' because the two parts are not described as falsity while they are in the proposition, for a part of a proposition is not described as truth or falsity. Understand.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the truth conditions for conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author begins by stating the essence of a conditional: a judgement about a relationship (companionship or opposition, or their negation) between two parts. Truth is defined as the correspondence of this judgement with external reality; falsity is its lack of correspondence. Importantly, truth does not depend on whether the individual parts are true or false in themselves. The example 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' is true because the entailment (luzūm) holds – if we assume stoneness, inanimateness follows – even though both parts are impossible (since Zayd is neither a stone nor inanimate). The Quranic verse illustrates the same: the conditional about having many gods is true because the disconnection (fasād) logically follows from the premise, regardless of the impossibility of many gods. Finally, the author clarifies that the parts are not evaluated for truth/falsity within the conditional itself; they are only considered 'in the origin' (i.e., in themselves outside the proposition).

Key Terms
شرطية
صحة
تنافر
لزوم
أصل

A) Because Zayd is actually a stone.
B) Because the entailment of inanimateness by stoneness is a real necessary relation.
C) Because both parts are false.
D) Because the conditional is a tautology.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional proposition depends solely on whether the claimed relationship (connection or disconnection) matches reality, irrespective of the truth of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If the world were eternal, it would have no beginning.' The antecedent is false (the world is not eternal) and the consequent is false (the world has a beginning). According to the passage, is this conditional true? Explain your reasoning by evaluating the relationship claimed.
