Truth Conditions for Muttasila Luzumiyya
Truth combinations for muttasila luzumiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conjunctive implicational conditional is true or false by checking the truth values of its parts and the existence of a necessary entailment (luzum).

Core Idea
A muttasila luzumiyya is false only when antecedent is true and consequent false, OR when both parts are true/false without real entailment.

The muttasila luzumiyya requires a real logical connection. This lesson unpacks the seven combinations (three true, four false) taught by the author, crucial for evaluating conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
Locate the muqaddam (antecedent) and tālī (consequent) in the conditional. For example, in 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal', 'Zayd is human' is the antecedent.
2
Determine actual truth values
Decide if each part is true or false in the real world. The truth of the conditional does not depend only on these values.
3
Check for necessary entailment (luzum)
Assess whether the antecedent genuinely implies the consequent. If there is no real connection (e.g., 'human rational' and 'donkey braying'), the conditional is false even if both parts are true.
This is the key difference from material implication in modern logic.
4
Apply the truth rules
The conditional is true only if (a) both parts true with luzum, (b) both false with luzum, or (c) antecedent false and consequent true with luzum. All other combinations are false.
Memorise: false from true antecedent + false consequent, and from both true/false without luzum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْيْلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ) بيان ذلك : أنَّ المتّصلة الصادقة تتركَّب مِن صادقين؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»، ومن كاذبين كما مرَّ عنِ الشَّارح، ومِن مقدَّم كاذب وتال صادق؛ كما مر، دون العكس. والمتّصلة الكاذبة : تكذب عن كاذبين؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حماراً كان حجراً»، وعن صادقين؛ نحو: كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً حيث لا علاقة وعن مقدَّم صادق وتال كاذب؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد حيواناً كان حجراً، وعن عكسه؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حجراً كان ناطقاً». فقد بان لك أنَّ المتّصلة اللُّزوميّة تكذب عن أقسام أربعة، وتصدق عن ثلاث. وأما المنفصلة : فالحقيقيَّة : تصدق عن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وتكذب عن صادقين نحو: إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً وإما أن يكون ناطقاً، وعن كاذبين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد حجراً أو شجراً». ومانعة الجمع: تصدق عن كاذبين كالمثال المذكور قبل وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حماراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إمَّا أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً». ومانعة الخلو : تصدقُ عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحَيَوان متحرِّكاً أو نامياً، وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحيوان متحرِّكاً أو جامداً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحجر ناطقاً أو متحرّكاً بالإرادة
His saying: (And the inclination of the remaining divisions is not hidden). The explanation of that: The true conjunctive [conditional] is composed of two true [parts], like: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal'; and from two false, as mentioned by the commentator; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, as mentioned, without the opposite. The false conjunctive: it is false from two false, like: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is a stone'; and from two true, like: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' where there is no connection; and from a true antecedent and false consequent, like: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone'; and from its opposite, like: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is rational'. So it has become clear to you that the implicational conjunctive is false from four divisions, and true from three. As for the disjunctive: the exclusive [ḥaqīqiyya] is true from one true and one false, like: 'Either this number is even or odd'; and false from two true, like: 'Either Zayd is human or rational'; and from two false, like: 'Either Zayd is a stone or a tree'. The prevention of union [māni'at al-jam']: it is true from two false, as in the previous example, and from one true and one false, like: 'Either Zayd is human or a donkey'; and false from two true, like: 'Either Zayd is human or rational'. The prevention of emptiness [māni'at al-khuluw]: it is true from two true, like: 'The animal is either moving or growing'; and from one true and one false, like: 'The animal is either moving or inanimate'; and false from two false, like: 'The stone is either rational or moving by will'.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is an exhaustive enumeration of the truth and falsity conditions for two types of conditionals: conjunctive (muttasila luzūmiyya) and disjunctive (munfasila) subdivided into three subtypes (ḥaqīqiyya, māni'at al-jam', māni'at al-khuluw). For the implicational conjunctive, which expresses a necessary connection (luzūm), there are four combinations that yield falsity and three that yield truth (the missing combination being true antecedent + false consequent, which is impossible due to the nature of implication). The examples illustrate each: false from two true when no real connection exists (rationality of human and braying of donkey), false from true antecedent and false consequent (animal → stone), and false from false antecedent and true consequent (stone → rational). True cases are when both true, both false, or false antecedent with true consequent. For disjunctives, each type has its own pattern: exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya) requires exactly one true part to be true; prevention of union (māni'at al-jam') allows both false but not both true; prevention of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) allows both true but not both false. All examples use terms from ordinary language (human, animal, stone, number) to illustrate logical relationships, consistent with the Islamic logic tradition's approach of using concrete examples from natural language.

Key Terms
متصل لزومي
مقدم وتال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following makes a muttasila luzumiyya false?
A) Both parts true with entailment.
B) Antecedent false, consequent true with entailment.
C) Both parts false with entailment.
D) Antecedent true, consequent false.

Key Takeaway
The muttasila luzumiyya requires both a truth-value pattern and a real entailment to be true; falsehood occurs in four specific combinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the conditional: 'If something is intoxicating, then it is khamr.' Check whether it is a true muttasila luzumiyya by examining the truth values of parts and the existence of entailment. (Hint: in Islamic fiqh, khamr is defined as intoxicating, so the entailment holds. But if you say 'If something is intoxicating, then it is wine,' that may be false if there is not the same entailment.)

Truth Conditions for Munfasila Haqiqiyya
Truth combinations for munfasila haqiqiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive proposition is exclusive (haqiqiyya) and determine its truth value based on the truth values of its parts.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunction is true when exactly one of its parts is true.

The munfasila haqiqiyya is the strongest form of disjunction, used in kalam for dichotomies. This lesson covers its truth conditions: true from one true and one false, false from both true or both false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts
Find the two parts separated by 'إما...أو...' (either...or...). For example, 'إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً' (either this number is even or odd).
2
Check the truth values of each disjunct
Determine if each disjunct is true or false. For the number example, a specific number like 2 makes 'even' true and 'odd' false.
3
Apply the exclusive rule
The haqiqiyya is true only if exactly one disjunct is true. If both are true or both false, it is false.
This is the standard 'exclusive or' in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْيْلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ) بيان ذلك : أنَّ المتّصلة الصادقة تتركَّب مِن صادقين؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»، ومن كاذبين كما مرَّ عنِ الشَّارح، ومِن مقدَّم كاذب وتال صادق؛ كما مر، دون العكس. والمتّصلة الكاذبة : تكذب عن كاذبين؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حماراً كان حجراً»، وعن صادقين؛ نحو: كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً حيث لا علاقة وعن مقدَّم صادق وتال كاذب؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد حيواناً كان حجراً، وعن عكسه؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حجراً كان ناطقاً». فقد بان لك أنَّ المتّصلة اللُّزوميّة تكذب عن أقسام أربعة، وتصدق عن ثلاث. وأما المنفصلة : فالحقيقيَّة : تصدق عن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وتكذب عن صادقين نحو: إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً وإما أن يكون ناطقاً، وعن كاذبين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد حجراً أو شجراً». ومانعة الجمع: تصدق عن كاذبين كالمثال المذكور قبل وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حماراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إمَّا أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً». ومانعة الخلو : تصدقُ عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحَيَوان متحرِّكاً أو نامياً، وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحيوان متحرِّكاً أو جامداً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحجر ناطقاً أو متحرّكاً بالإرادة
His saying: (And the inclination of the remaining divisions is not hidden). The explanation of that: The true conjunctive [conditional] is composed of two true [parts], like: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal'; and from two false, as mentioned by the commentator; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, as mentioned, without the opposite. The false conjunctive: it is false from two false, like: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is a stone'; and from two true, like: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' where there is no connection; and from a true antecedent and false consequent, like: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone'; and from its opposite, like: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is rational'. So it has become clear to you that the implicational conjunctive is false from four divisions, and true from three. As for the disjunctive: the exclusive [ḥaqīqiyya] is true from one true and one false, like: 'Either this number is even or odd'; and false from two true, like: 'Either Zayd is human or rational'; and from two false, like: 'Either Zayd is a stone or a tree'. Which combination makes a munfasila haqiqiyya false?
A) One disjunct true, the other false.

Key Takeaway
The exclusive disjunction is true exactly when one part is true; any other combination is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) is either alive in his grave or dead.' Is this a haqiqiyya? Determine its truth value based on actual beliefs. (Note: In Islamic creed, the Prophet is alive in his grave in a special barzakh life, so the first part is true and the second false; thus the disjunction is true.)

Truth Conditions for Mani'at al-Jam' and Mani'at al-Khuluw
Truth combinations for disjunctive subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two non-exclusive disjunctions and determine their truth values by checking the truth values of the parts.

Core Idea
Mani'at al-jam' allows both false but not both true; mani'at al-khuluw allows both true but not both false.

Not all disjunctions are exclusive. This lesson explains the truth conditions for prevention-of-union (māni'at al-jam') and prevention-of-emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw), using examples from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Determine whether the text specifies māni'at al-jam' (prevents both true) or māni'at al-khuluw (prevents both false). The passage gives examples for each.
2
Check truth values of disjuncts
Find out if each disjunct is true or false.
3
Apply the type-specific rule
For māni'at al-jam': false if both true; otherwise true. For māni'at al-khuluw: false if both false; otherwise true.
These are sometimes called 'inclusive or' variants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْيْلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ) بيان ذلك : أنَّ المتّصلة الصادقة تتركَّب مِن صادقين؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»، ومن كاذبين كما مرَّ عنِ الشَّارح، ومِن مقدَّم كاذب وتال صادق؛ كما مر، دون العكس. والمتّصلة الكاذبة : تكذب عن كاذبين؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حماراً كان حجراً»، وعن صادقين؛ نحو: كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً حيث لا علاقة وعن مقدَّم صادق وتال كاذب؛ نحو: «إن كان زيد حيواناً كان حجراً، وعن عكسه؛ نحو: إن كان زيد حجراً كان ناطقاً». فقد بان لك أنَّ المتّصلة اللُّزوميّة تكذب عن أقسام أربعة، وتصدق عن ثلاث. وأما المنفصلة : فالحقيقيَّة : تصدق عن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وتكذب عن صادقين نحو: إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً وإما أن يكون ناطقاً، وعن كاذبين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد حجراً أو شجراً». ومانعة الجمع: تصدق عن كاذبين كالمثال المذكور قبل وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حماراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إمَّا أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً». ومانعة الخلو : تصدقُ عن صادقين؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحَيَوان متحرِّكاً أو نامياً، وعن صادق وكاذب؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون الحيوان متحرِّكاً أو جامداً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحجر ناطقاً أو متحرّكاً بالإرادة
His saying: (And the inclination of the remaining divisions is not hidden). The explanation of that: The true conjunctive [conditional] is composed of two true [parts], like: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal'; and from two false, as mentioned by the commentator; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, as mentioned, without the opposite. The false conjunctive: it is false from two false, like: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is a stone'; and from two true, like: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' where there is no connection; and from a true antecedent and false consequent, like: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone'; and from its opposite, like: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is rational'. So it has become clear to you that the implicational conjunctive is false from four divisions, and true from three. As for the disjunctive: the exclusive [ḥaqīqiyya] is true from one true and one false, like: 'Either this number is even or odd'; and false from two true, like: 'Either Zayd is human or rational'; and from two false, like: 'Either Zayd is a stone or a tree'. The prevention of union [māni'at al-jam']: it is true from two false, as in the previous example, and from one true and one false, like: 'Either Zayd is human or a donkey'; and false from two true, like: 'Either Zayd is human or rational'. The prevention of emptiness [māni'at al-khuluw]: it is true from two true, like: 'The animal is either moving or growing'; and from one true and one false, like: 'The animal is either moving or inanimate'; and false from two false, like: 'The stone is either rational or moving by will'.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is an exhaustive enumeration of the truth and falsity conditions for two types of conditionals: conjunctive (muttasila luzūmiyya) and disjunctive (munfasila) subdivided into three subtypes (ḥaqīqiyya, māni'at al-jam', māni'at al-khuluw). For the implicational conjunctive, which expresses a necessary connection (luzūm), there are four combinations that yield falsity and three that yield truth (the missing combination being true antecedent + false consequent, which is impossible due to the nature of implication). The examples illustrate each: false from two true when no real connection exists (rationality of human and braying of donkey), false from true antecedent and false consequent (animal → stone), and false from false antecedent and true consequent (stone → rational). True cases are when both true, both false, or false antecedent with true consequent. For disjunctives, each type has its own pattern: exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya) requires exactly one true part to be true; prevention of union (māni'at al-jam') allows both false but not both true; prevention of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) allows both true but not both false. All examples use terms from ordinary language (human, animal, stone, number) to illustrate logical relationships, consistent with the Islamic logic tradition's approach of using concrete examples from natural language.

Key Terms
مانعة جمع
مانعة خلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of disjunction is false when both disjuncts are false?
A) Mani'at al-jam'
B) Mani'at al-khuluw
C) Haqiqiyya
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Mani'at al-jam' is false only when both true; mani'at al-khuluw is false only when both false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(Hint: both can be true? No, they are opposite times. Both false? The sun always rises in east and sets in west, so one true and one false? Actually both are true at different times? The disjunction is about a single moment: at a given moment, the sun is either rising or setting? It cannot be both, nor neither at noon? So it might be haqiqiyya.)

The Need for Contradiction and Conversion in Proof
Author explains why contradiction and conversion are needed, with an example of qiyas al-khalf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why contradiction and conversion are necessary for logical proof, using the example of qiyas al-khalf.

Core Idea
To prove something, you may need to invalidate its opposite or convert its proposition.

In this chapter, the author begins the study of the properties of propositions, specifically contradiction and conversion. He quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who explains that proofs often target the invalidation of a proposition's contradictory or the derivation of its converse. An example from Ibn Harun illustrates this using a reductio argument for the temporality of the world. Understanding this rationale reveals how these concepts are essential for constructing and analyzing theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two properties under discussion
The author states he will now discuss 'some of their rulings' (أحكامها), namely contradiction (التناقض) and conversion (العكس). These are properties that affect how propositions relate to each other in proofs.
2
Understand the reason given by Ibn al-Hajib
Ibn al-Hajib's saying: 'Since the proof may be established on the invalidation of a thing, while the sought is its contradictory, and may be established on the thing while the sought is its converse, one needs to define them.' This means proofs can aim at proving a proposition by disproving its opposite (contradiction) or by deriving a logically equivalent proposition (conversion).
3
Examine the example of reductio (qiyas al-khalf)
Ibn Harun provides an example: to prove the world is originated (حادث), one assumes the opposite (that it is not originated) and shows that this leads to a contradiction with the observed fact that it is changing. The conclusion is that the world is originated.
The example in the text: 'If it were not originated, it would not be changing; but it is changing, so it is originated.'
4
Refine the understanding: what does reductio actually produce?
The commentator clarifies a nuance: in the reductio, the proof does NOT directly produce the contradictory of the sought; rather, it produces the sought itself by invalidating its opposite. The conclusion is the original claim, not its contradictory. The example confirms this: the conclusion 'it is originated' is the sought, not its contradictory.
The author says 'تأمل' (ponder), indicating this is a subtle but important point.
5
Apply the example to the rationale
This reductio illustrates why contradiction is needed: the proof assumes the negation of the sought and derives an impossibility, thereby establishing the sought. Without understanding contradiction, one could not follow the logical structure of such arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
لَمَّا فَرَغَ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ القَضَايَا ، أَخَذَ يَذْكُرُ بَعْضَ أَحْكَامِهَا ، وَهُوَ : التَّناقُضُ، وَالعَكْسُ . وَوَجْهُ الحَاجَةِ إِلَيْهِمَا : مَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ بِقَوْلِهِ : وَلَمَّا كَانَ الدَّلِيلُ قَدْ يُقَامُ عَلَى إِبْطَالِ الشَّيْءِ ، وَالمَطْلُوبُ نَقِيضُهُ ، وَقَدْ يُقَامُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ وَالمَطْلُوبُ عَكْسُهُ، احْتِيجَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهِمَا» . قَالَ ابْنُ هَارُونَ : مِثَالُ المَظْلُوبِ الَّذِي يَقُومُ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى إِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ : قِيَاسُ الخُلْفِ، فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ نَقِيضَ المَطْلُوبِ . اهـ . وَمِثَالُهُ أَنْ تَقُولَ فِي الِاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى حُدُوثِ العَالَمِ : لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ حَادِثاً ، لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَغَيِّراً ، لَكِنَّهُ مُتَغَيِّرُ ، فَهُوَ حَادِثُ»
لَكِنَّ الوَاقِعَ فِي قِيَاسِ الخُلْفِ كَمَا مَثَّلْنَا هُوَ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ عَلَى المَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ، لَا أَنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ نَقِيضَ المَطْلُوبِ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ ؛ تَأمَّلْ
Chapter on contradiction. When he finished the divisions of propositions, he began to mention some of their rulings, namely: contradiction and conversion. The reason for needing them is what Ibn al-Hajib indicated by his saying: 'Since the proof may be established on the invalidation of a thing, while the sought is its contradictory, and may be established on the thing while the sought is its converse, one needs to define them.' Ibn Harun said: An example of the sought for which the proof is established on the invalidation of its contradictory is the reductio (qiyas al-khalf), for it produces the contradictory of the sought. Its example is that you say in proving the temporality of the world: If it were not originated, it would not be changing; but it is changing, so it is originated. But what actually occurs in the reductio as we exemplified is that it is inference to the sought by invalidating its contradictory, not that it produces the contradictory of the sought as he mentioned; ponder.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on contradiction (al-tanaqud) and explains why contradiction and conversion are studied. The author first notes that after discussing propositions, he turns to their properties. The necessity of these topics arises because a logical proof may aim at invalidating a proposition's contradictory or at deriving its converse, as Ibn al-Hajib states. Ibn Harun then illustrates the first case with the reductio argument (qiyas al-khalf): to prove the temporality of the world, one assumes its opposite (that it is eternal) and shows an impossibility, thereby establishing the original claim. However, the commentator corrects a nuance: in a proper reductio, the proof does not directly produce the contradictory of the sought; rather, it establishes the sought by refuting its opposite. The example provided uses the fact that the world is changing (a premise) to conclude that it must be originated. The commentator advises careful reflection on this subtlety.

تناقض
عكس
قياس الخلف

Key Takeaway
The need to study contradiction and conversion arises from the structure of proofs: sometimes you need to refute an opposite, sometimes you need to convert a proposition; understanding these operations is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology.

The Formal Definition of Contradiction (Tanaquḍ)
Author defines contradiction formally and explains each term in the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for contradiction between two propositions: difference in quality, and necessary truth of exactly one.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not just difference in quality—it requires that exactly one proposition must be true.

In this passage, the author of al-Sullam gives the precise logical definition of contradiction. This definition is crucial for understanding immediate inferences and for ensuring that two statements are truly contradictory. The commentator unpacks each term to avoid common misunderstandings. Let's walk through the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of the definition
The definition has three parts: opposition in quality (khulf fi kaif), truth of one (sidq wahid), and necessary entailment (tufti). These three together constitute a complete definition.
2
Understand 'quality' as affirmation or negation
The commentator explicitly states that 'kaif' means affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab). Contradiction requires that one proposition affirms and the other denies the same subject-predicate relation.
3
Clarify 'truth of one' as exactly one
The author says 'truth of one', but the commentator adds 'only' to emphasize that the two cannot both be true nor both false. Exactly one is true; the other is false.
4
Recognize the role of 'entailed' as a necessary condition
The word 'tufti' indicates that the truth of one necessarily follows from the opposition in quality. This is not accidental but a logical consequence. The whole circumstantial clause restricts the opposition to those that are contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّناقُضُ فِي الاصْطِلاح هُوَ مَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ المُصَنِّفُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «تَنَاقُضُ»: خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفٍ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ تُفي
((خُلْفُ) بِمَعْنَى : اخْتِلَافِ ، وَالكَيْفُ : الإِيْجَابُ وَالسَّلْبُ، وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ» أَيْ: فَقَط ، وَقُفِيْ بِمَعْنَى : تَبِعَ ؛ أَي لُزُوماً ، وَالجُمْلَةُ حَالِيَّةٌ فَهِيَ قَيْدٌ فِيمَا قَبْلِهَا مِنْ تَمَامِ
قوله : (أَيْ : فَقَط) قيد زائد على كلام النَّاظم ; لأنَّه إنَّما قال: «وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ»، وهل الآخر صادق أو كاذب يبقى ما هو
And contradiction in terminology is what the author indicated by his saying: 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is necessarily entailed.' 'Opposition' means difference, and 'quality' is affirmation and negation. His saying 'and the truth of one' means only one is true, and 'is entailed' means follows necessarily. The sentence is circumstantial, thus a restriction on what precedes it from the complete (definition). His saying 'only' is an extra restriction on the author's words, because he only said 'and the truth of one', and whether the other is true or false remains open.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of contradiction (tanaquḍ) as given by the author of the matn (al-Sullam). The definition states that contradiction is the opposition (khulf) of two propositions in quality (kaif), i.e., one affirmative and one negative, with the consequence that exactly one of them is true. The commentator explains each term: 'khulf' means difference/opposition; 'kaif' refers to affirmation and negation; 'truth of one' means exactly one is true; and 'tuftī' (entailed) indicates necessary logical consequence. The whole phrase 'truth of one is entailed' is a circumstantial clause that conditions the opposition: the two propositions must not only differ in quality but also be such that one is true (and the other false). The commentator adds that the author's wording 'truth of one' already implies that only one is true, but some might think it allows both to be true; thus the extra restriction 'only' is needed to clarify that contradiction requires mutual exclusivity in truth value.

Key Terms
خلف
كيف
تفي

Key Takeaway
Contradiction requires both opposition in quality (affirmation vs. negation) and the necessary truth of exactly one proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The One Condition for True Contradiction
Author reduces contradiction conditions to unity of judgmental relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two propositions are genuine contradictories by verifying unity of judgmental relation.

Core Idea
True contradiction requires unity of all aspects except truth value; only the relation matters.

The classical logicians debated how many conditions are needed for a genuine contradiction. Some listed eight, others three or two. But the verified position simplifies everything: only one condition matters—unity of the judgmental relation. This lesson walks you through that reduction and shows you how to apply it to real examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the eight unities
Some scholars required eight conditions for contradiction: unity of subject, predicate, time, place, position, condition, relation, and action. These are meant to ensure the two propositions are exactly parallel except in truth value.
These eight are often memorized in a verse by al-ʿArabī al-Fāsī.
2
See the reductions to fewer unities
Other scholars reduced the eight to three (subject, predicate, time) or even two (subject and predicate). Each reduction aims to capture what is essential.
The reduction shows that many conditions are redundant or derivable.
3
Grasp the verified single condition: unity of judgmental relation
The final verified position is that only one condition is necessary: the unity of the judgmental relation (waḥdat al-nisbah al-ḥukmiyyah). This means the negation must apply to exactly the same aspect that was affirmed. All other conditions are reducible to this, because any difference in subject, time, etc., changes the relation.
This condition also covers additional aspects like instrument or state that were not in the original eight.
4
Apply the condition to examples
Analyze the examples given: 'Zayd came riding' and 'Zayd did not come not riding' are not contradictory because the negation applies to a different aspect (riding vs. not riding). Similarly, 'Zayd ate bread' and 'Zayd did not eat meat' differ in object. These are rhetorical figures (ṭibāq), not logical contradictions.
To test a pair, ask: does the negation target exactly the same aspect that was affirmed? If not, it's not a contradiction.

فصل في التناقض. وَقَدْ جَمَعَ بَعْضُ الشُّيُوخِ هَذِهِ الثَّمَانِيَةُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَمَا لَهُ تَحَقُقٌ إِلَّا بِانْ تُوجَدَ وَحَدَاتٌ ثَمَانٍ فَاعْلَمَنْ وَضْعٌ، وَوَقْتُ، وَمَكَانٌ، حَمْلُ كُلُّ إِضَافَةٌ، وَشَرْطُ، فِعْلُ وَاكْتَفَى بَعْضُهُمْ بِثَلَاثِ وَحَدَاتٍ وَحْدَةُ المَوْضُوع وَوَحْدَةُ المَحْمُولِ، وَوَحْدَهُ الزَّمَانِ ؛ وَيَرْجِعُ بَعْضُ مَا بَقِيَ إِلَى : وَحْدَةِ المَحْمُولِ، وَبَعْضُهُ إِلَى : وَحْدَةِ المَوْضُوعِ . وَاكْتَفَى بَعْضُهُمْ بِوَحْدَةِ الطَرَفَيْنِ ، وَهُوَ أَقْرَبُ مِمَّا قَبْلَهُ. وَالتَّحْقِيقُ : اشْتِرَاطُ وَحْدَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ هِيَ : وَحْدَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الحُكْمِيَّةِ؛ بِحَيْثُ يَرِدُ السَّلْبُ عَلَى مَا وَرَدَ عَلَيْهِ الإِيجَابُ ؛ إِذْ جَمِيعُ الوَحَدَاتِ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى وَحْدَةِ النِّسْبَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَتَى اخْتَلَفَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهَا ، اخْتَلَفَتِ النِّسْبَةُ ، بَل اتِّحَادُ النِّسْبَةِ يَشْمَلُ أُمُوراً أَخَرَ غَيْرَ الثَّمَانِيَةِ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ التَّنَاقُضُ إِلَّا بِهَا ; كَاتِّحَادِ المَفْعُولِ بِهِ وَلَهُ وَمَعَهُ وَالحَالُ وَالآلَهُ وَغَيْرُ ذَلِكَ; إِذْ مَتَى اخْتَلَفَ شَيْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَكُنْ تَنَاقُضٌ. فَلَا تَنَاقُضَ فِي قَوْلِكَ: «جَاءَ زَيْدٌ» أَيْ رَاكِباً، وَلَمْ يَجِئْ زَيْدٌ أَيْ غَيْرَ رَاكِبٍ. وَزَيْدٌ كَلَّ أَيْ: الخُبْزَ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِآكِلٍ أَيْ: اللَّحْمَ. وَقَدْ عُدَّ نَحْوُ هَذَا مِمَّا فِيهِ نَفْيُ الشَّيْءِ وَإِثْبَاتُهُ بِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ مِنَ المُحَسَّنَاتِ البَدِيعِيَّةِ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ البَيَانِ وَيُسَمَّى عِنْدَهُمُ: «الطِّبَاقَ». فَبِاعْتِبَارِ مَا ذُكِرَ لَمْ يَكُنْ فِي هَذَا تَنَاقُضٌ.
Chapter on Contradiction. And one of the shaykhs has collected these eight in his saying: 'And it has no realization except when eight unities are found, so know: position, time, place, predicate, whole, relation, condition, action.' And some of them sufficed with three unities: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time. And some of what remains is reducible to unity of predicate, and some to unity of subject. And some of them sufficed with unity of the two extremes, which is closer than what preceded. The verification: requiring a single unity, which is the unity of the judgmental relation; such that the negation applies to what the affirmation applied to. For all the unities are reducible to unity of the relation; because whenever one of them differs, the relation differs. Rather, unification of the relation includes other matters besides the eight without which contradiction is not realized, like unification of the object with which, for which, with whom, the state, the instrument, and other than that. For whenever any of that differs, there is no contradiction. So there is no contradiction in your saying: 'Zayd came' meaning riding, and 'Zayd did not come' meaning not riding. And 'Zayd ate' meaning bread, 'Zayd did not eat' meaning meat. And such is counted among what involves negation and affirmation of a thing from two different aspects among the rhetorical beautifications according to scholars of rhetoric, and it is called by them 'ṭibāq'. So by what is mentioned, there is no contradiction in this.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for a proper logical contradiction (tanāquḍ). The author begins by noting that one shaykh (al-ʿArabī al-Fāsī) collected eight conditions for contradiction in verse: unity of position, time, place, predicate, whole, relation, condition, and action. He then reports that some scholars reduced these to three unities: subject, predicate, and time. Others reduced them further to just two: unity of subject and predicate (unity of the two extremes), which is considered better. The author then presents the 'verified position' (al-taḥqīq) attributed to al-Fārābī: only one condition is necessary — the unity of the judgmental relation (waḥdat al-nisbah al-ḥukmiyyah). This means the negation must apply to exactly the same aspect of the same judgment. All other conditions are reducible to this because any difference in subject, predicate, time, etc., changes the relation. Moreover, this single condition covers additional aspects beyond the eight, such as the object with which, for which, with whom, the state, and the instrument. If any of these differ, there is no real contradiction. He gives examples: 'Zayd came riding' and 'Zayd did not come not riding' are not contradictory because the aspect differs. Similarly, 'Zayd ate bread' and 'Zayd did not eat meat' differ in object. Such statements are rhetorical devices (ṭibāq), not logical contradictions. Thus, proper contradiction requires that all aspects be identical except for affirmation and negation.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ) – contradiction
وحدة النسبة الحكمية (waḥdat al-nisbah al-ḥukmiyyah) – unity of judgmental relation
المحسّنات البديعية (al-muḥassināt al-badīʿiyyah) – rhetorical beautifications
الطباق (al-ṭibāq) – antithesis

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs is a true contradiction according to the verified position in this passage?
A) 'Zayd came riding' and 'Zayd did not come not riding'
B) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing at the same time and place'
C) 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden'
D) 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created by a creator'

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be genuine contradictories, the negation must apply to exactly the same aspect as the affirmation; this is the single condition of unity of judgmental relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'The world is temporally originated' and 'The world is not temporally originated by a creator.' Are these genuine contradictories? Check unity of judgmental relation: both refer to the same world and same attribute (temporally originated), but one adds 'by a creator,' which changes the relation. Therefore, they are not true contradictories.
?
Construct a pair of statements from fiqh that are genuine contradictories according to the verified condition. For example: 'Khamr is forbidden' and 'Khamr is not forbidden' (same subject, same predicate, same time, same aspect). Explain why they satisfy unity of judgmental relation.
